THE RED PAGE
A Cornstalk’s Gleanings
01 ( all the Australian contributions to t
literature of the Boer war there is no
more characteristically Australian, no
more interesting than “ Tommy Cor:
stalk, by J. H. M. Abbott, corporal of the Fir
Australian Horse. The writer has cast aside a
the trammels of conventional record, all details o
how he got to the war, what troops he marchec
with, what battles he fought in, and plunges
once into a series of impressionist pictures o
wnat struck him most vividly. r i?h6rG is i
attempt at “tine-writing,” but there is
air of realism and a flavor of fine, rac
humor in his pages. Further, one gets the im
pression that the writer wants to be honest
what he says; the deep disappointment at no
being able to class the Boer as a dirty, treachero
pig is obvious ; the fear of being classed as pr
Boer in an intolerant land evident. Still, M
Abbott tells what, truth he sees ; and, taking in
account the first deep prejudice in his mine
against the Boers, and the strong temptation
look for evil rather than for good in them (anc
what one looks for one sees), his testimony is o
great value in'judging the character of .the litt
cocky people whom we’ve been helping to evi
from their farms in S’Africa.
Bet Mr. Abbott tell first his frame of mind
going to the war
“Fetch us Kruger’s scalp,” said the crowd that lin
the streets, as we struggled hotly to Woolloomo
100 Bay on a sweating afternoon in January. “ Don
take any notice of white flag‘3 ; play ’em at their owr
game ; wipe’em out; give ’em hell!’’—they cried as
led our horses up the gangway. Good-bye, boys
screeched an excited lady on the wharf; “put the
blanky lights out! ”
There had been Stormberg, and Magersfontein, anc
Golenso—and Spion Kop was to come. The facile pen
the paragraphist had described the incidents of treache
ous ambush. The tame leader-writers of the nios
respectable morning paper in the world had, with a
deference to possible varieties of opinion—which the
would not for all the riches of Mount Morgan unwitting
offend—given it as their unalterable dictum that th
Boer was an excrescence upon the face of all humanit
that he should, without doubt, be made to feel th
iron hand, that this war was none of your commo:
bloody, uninviting struggles, but a holy crusade agains
infamous wrong, and injusl.ice, and unreasonable tvramn
Most touchingly symbolical of its righteous approval
our going, the enthusiastic crowd had given us to drin
of raw whisky as we shoved, and pushed, and pante.
thirstily through it. And doubtful damsels, to whom on
never before had attributed any of the broad feeling tha
leads to publicly expressed emotion, clung weepingl
about our uecks, and kissed us lovingly, as thev implorec
us to go forth and battle for what was right, and gooc
and noble, against oppressive wrong, and wretohec
heartless, cruelty.
And so, by the time we were up the shrouds, and th
crowd was a mere blur along the shore, expressive onl
by points of waving white and a murmuring roar tha
lost itself across the widening waters, we really did fee
enthusiastic admiration for ourselves. We honestly in:
derstood ourselves to be, as it were, knight-errants of n
mean order, going forth to wrest the fair maiden o
African freedom from the vile clutches of the Boe
dragon.
„And we sailed away knowing all about everything
There was no possibility of our having made any mistake
as to the Boer. We had him at our finger-ends. He was
a low liar, a cunning, unscrupulous cheat, an oppressor o
the unfortunate philanthropists who had come to make
his country rich.
The Uitlander, for whom we were going to do battle
was indeed a long-suffering, overburdened martyr
Purely for philanthropy he had sought to do the Trans
vaal a good turn. He had toiled, with some success, to
wrest the golden treasure of the unwilling Rand from its
hidden depths. He had made a bankrupt State into
rich one. He was the kind of man who shoved the work
along. And they wouldn’t give him a vote. So we were
going to get it for him. The Boer had been weighed in
the balance and found wanting. We would adjust the
scale.
And his first conclusions (he sees a train-load o
Boer prisoners for the first time).
We looked upon an excursion train-load of
“cockies,” or ordinary “bush-hands” going down en
masse with their coats off, to “ blue their cheques ’’ . .
Boers! These men Boers ? Oh, no ; thev could not" be
surely ! Where were the brutal faces of the English illus-
trated papers'? .... These quiet, orderly, rather good
humored people !
And afterwards, from a long experience of the
Boer as fighter, prisoner, citizen, Mr. Abbott
says
We had come to respect him, in the mass, as a very
gallant man, and to envy in him the possession of hardy
virtues such as we had never expected to find, and which
we would not mind feeling quite sure that we possessed
ourselves.
And, as to the future, Mr. Abbott’s opinion:
For years and years the sad women of those countries
will loathe and detest all things English more bitterly
than before. Their hatred df us already dates back from
before the Great Trek, from before the hanging of
Slacliter’s Nek, and comes.across the war of “81. Now it
will have all the hard misery of this war to add to its
rankling bitterness. There is a difficult task before the
coming rulers of the two late Republics. And so, when
we hear that the Boers have been unduly lax in honour-
able dealings with our kinsfolk, we must remember how
they regard them. What an invasion and conquering of
our country by Chinese would mean to us, the invasion
and taking of theirs by the English means to them.
We have emphasised this section of the book—
the last—because it is most important to Aus-
tralians. From a literary point of view, the rest
of the work is equally good—as breezy and fresh
in style, as frank and knowledgeable in comment.
The author’s pictures of the veldt:
Here, in this naked Africa, everything is stiller than
the grave at night.
of the march, of the bivouac, of sentry duty, show
a capacity for seeing things. Almost always the
actual phrase used might be a little bit improved
in wording, but there is, on the other hand,
almost always that actual literary gift of insight
to atone for merely verbal lapses. Here’s a pen-
picture, vigorously drawn of Roberts
Of all the staff he was the freshest and. most active-
looking by a very great deal. It was not hard to realise
that, since the army had left the camp at Osfontein, it
had been a time of great strain and long hours for all of
them. The tired, weary figures sitting their horses
stiffly, spoke eloquently enough of the state of being of
the Staff as a whole. But the little man at their head
rode as a “flash” shearer who has just “rung out” a
shed—alert, springy, vigorous and very fit. Excuse the
comiiarison, you who know flash shearers. It merely
refers to deportment.
In course of conversation with an engine-driver on the
railway between Kroonstad and the Transvaal capital, the
manner of the two men, Roberts and Kitchener, was
strikingly exemplified by his words.
“ t)h, yes,” he said. “ Bobs an’ Kitchener comes along
sometimes. My colonial, y* oughter see the difference at
the stations, though ! W’en Bob’s train pulls up, he gets
out an strolls along the platform, an’ everybody knocks
oft work so’s to come up an’ h ive a '.ook at him. ' He jes’
walks about among the crowd, talkin’ to ’em like me an*
you would. Ask ’em hoiv they’re gettin’ on for rations, an’
so on. Course, he’s never familiar, or anything like that
y can always see he’s Boss —and if he notices anything
wrong he lets ’em know, quick an' lively—but he seems to
be more of a friend to everybody than anything else.
But wen “Herbert” steps out of his carriage there’s
hardly a soul to be seen on the platform—they’re all away
diggin trenches, or mountin’ guns, or scoutin’ rounl the
country any blessed thing, 1 so long as he can see ’em
wo non . Lord help ’em if they ain’t ! W’y, I b’lieve if
Kitchener was to he given command of Heaven’s gates
he d jes as soon gaol Peter, ’spite of all his long service,
supposin’ he caught him nappin’ any warm afternoon !
• book is truly well worth the read-
ing by Australians, spite of the flood of war-works.
Certain Books.
THrs is the spring season in England for
novels, and the mail brings them out in
scores to the unhappy reviewer. Of a big
batch received this week, “The Westcntes,”
by hjuiller Couch, is the best. Quiller Couch
has a manner, and weaves a pretty story in
not too many words. At the other mid of the
scale _ Blue Lilies,” by Lucas Cleeve (Fisher
Unwin), is absolute trash, full of a nasty, yet
futile, effort at “smartness.” “The Hound-of
the Has kervi lies,” by Conan Doyle (Longmans), is
on a par with Mr. Doyle’s other imaginative works
before he took to war polemics ; that is to say. it is
an interesting, workmanlike detectivestory. “The
Dark of the Mo . m,” by S. It. Crockett (Macmillan),
doesn t read as interestingly as earlier works of
this writer: either he has exhausted his vim, or
tl e taste for Scotch tales of robbery and piety is
departing. “A Bid for Empire,” by Major
Arthur Grifhths (George Bell and Sons), is a good
adventure yarn for a railway journey, and the
same may be said of “The Shoes of Fortune,” by
Neil Munro (Grant Richards). “Charlotte,” by
. B. Walford (Longmans), is a fairly bright
picture of an unpleasant phase of society, and will
probably find many women readers.
Wanted—a Better “Full Stop.”
The present full stop is so small, and often
so battered through constant use, that it
doesn’t stop you at all. Only the capital
letter that follows pulls you up. But, then
m poetry the capital letter is no guide to
the division of sentences because of the question-
able system of putting a capital at the head of
each line. One printer suggests the use of
florets of various sizes for the various degrees
of stoppage. Thus:—
In clays of yore how fortunately fared
the Minstrel* wandering on from hall to hail*
baronial court or cheered with gifts
munificent* and love* and ladies’
now.meeting on his .road an armed knight *
now resting with a pilgrim by the side
of a clear brookgj beneath an abbey’s roof
one evening sumptuously the next*
humbly in a religious
or with some merry outlaws of the wood£J
or haply shrouded in a hermit cell
But it won’t do; the marks are too obtrusive.
And how should we write them ? Still tlie stupid
little full-stop ought to be altered. Press-writers
nowadays rarely use the wretched little thing :
they rather make a x or run a ring round the dot
That perhaps gives the right hint to reformers.
What is Truth?
“ E.F.L.” writes:
_Dr. W. Stern, of Breslau, lecturing before Berlin
1 sychological Society recently, gave the results of his
experiments upon 80 students (amongst them a good
proportion of females) in regard to memory-delusions.
Ihe persons on whom the experiments were made had to
relate the facts as to certain events just conveyed to their
minds by illustrations. Considerable pains and trouble
was taken to lucidly explain the illustrations. The
persons were, in nearly all instances, unable to give a
correct version of what they had just heard and seen
Not only were substantial parts left out, but additions
and qualitative changes were made. A hare became a
cat, a walking-stick changed .into an umbrella, right into
left, a walking man was running, and so on. The per-
centage of errors increased very considerably when the
same event was related some weeks later. At last a mock
trial was instituted, where witnesses had to give evidence
on oath, the result of which was that, upon every oath
taken, there were recorded three perjuries in male, and
seven perjuries in female evidence. It was proved that
women displayed a considerably better memorv for
details than men, but were not reliable as to' the
objectiveness of evidence. The remarkable fact was
recorded that all evidence was tendered without reserva-
tion of a mistake and with positive, certainty, it was
shown that the relation of a comparatively simple storv
transmitted to a third person, underwent most astound-
ing changes.
The result of these direct experiments is valuable
in reinforcing scientific conclusions as to “ evi-
dence,” which, however, have been long almost
established as indisputable. The personal experi-
ence of every man of the world comprises some
case, if not, several, of very remarkable “honest
mistake. ’ The writer, some years ago, attended
as a reporter, a Victorian public meeting which
was of a very lively character. Between two
persons present there was a hot recrimination
which he reported as faithfully as possible. After
copy” was handed in, the editor, for reasons of
his own, interpolated a most insulting remark-as
having been made by Mr. A. concerning Mr B
The remark was certainly not made at the meet-
mg, but it was reported in that paper as having
been made. A libel-action was instituted, based
on this report, and no less than seven persons
came forward to volunteer evidence that the
remark ascribed to Mr. A. had been made by
him: they had no reasonable motive for false-
hood, and, no doubt, believed that the insult had
passed, because they had seen it reported. More
remarkable still, Mr. A. was prepared to swear
that he had made the statement attributed to
him! The action never came into Court,
and a tall and bewildering conflict of evi-
dence was thus avoided. Who is there who
cannot recall a case, if not as remarkable, at
any rate pointing the same moral—the possi-
bility of honest mistake of, .seemingly, the most
extraordinary kind? Truth, no doubt, is oc-
casionally sworn to, even in the witness-box, butthe
scientific investigation of the human mind suggests
that an up-to-date judicial system would make
a much greater allowance for error than is done
now. Trial by ordeal, trial by single combat, trial
by computation have long been condemned as bar-
barous. Will not civilisation have to discard also
the present system of trial by evidence which is,
as a rule, examined into by no scientific or reason-
able method ? Science tells us now that it is an
absolute impossibility for the average human being
to tell the whole truth about a complex set of cir-
cumstances. It would need a mind almost
omniscient, a memory almost omnipotent, an
honesty almost divine, to do that. Yet the evi-
dence of witnesses swearing to the various move-
ments of a distant busy day is freely taken in the
law-courts, and sometimes men hanged on its
strength. Then, diverging from the average mind,
there is the female mind (existing very largely in
one sex, and somewhat considerably in another)
which is forbidden, by hysteria or some other form
of emotionalism, even the capacity to dis-
tinguish between truth and falsehood in the
simplest of matters: this type of mind
can “honestly” evolve a criminal outrage or
other story which an honest (but stupid)
jury can 'afterwards hang someone on. By an.
almost infinite series of gradations the unconscious
liar merges into the conscious liar, who is more
wicked but far less harmful on the whole. Here
are the four extreme points of the compass of
witnesses: — ‘
1. The person; who by desire to be honest, and by
sound mental training is able to approximate to the
Truth in most matters (usually to be recognised by a
habit of reservation from positiveness in the witness-box)
“That is my impression,'though I am not absolutely
sure.”
2. The p.erson, with a strong desire to be honest and a
profound faith in his or her power of accurate observa-
tion, who cheerfully swears without reservation to identi-
fications, to handwritings, to dates, and so on.
3. The person with a mental kink who imagines vain
things, and believes them profoundly, and swears to them
by all the gods of creation.
4. The person who lies# deliberately with a purpose.
Between these cardinal points there are a hundred
or more minor gradations. Of these, Nos. 2 and
3 are the most dangerous enemies of Justice.
No. 4, paradoxical as it may seem, is almost more
valuable in eliciting the truth than No. 1. Never
are the facts more clearly established than when
there is an attempt to deliberately falsify them.
The problem then of the deliberate perjurer,
alluded to in The 8., 5/4/’O2, is not the only one
confronting tbdse who wish to administer even
justice. It is not even the most difficult one.
Yet Jedges are recruited most often from the ranks
of political lawyers, and political lawyers are
generally the least skilled and the most self-
confident of the prominent men of their profes-
sion. Justice certainly has bandaged her eyes
under such a system !

Extract from a, Nineteenth Century paper on
“Australian Literature,” by PercyF. Rowland,
of 39 Upper William-street, Sydney, N.S.W. Is
Percy’s knowledge of letters on a par with his
knowledge of horseracing ?
Until almost the end of his life he (Adam Lindsay
Gordon) retained his passion for racing, and by winning,
in 1868, three steeplechases in one day—one being tfie
world-famed Melbourne Cup—acquired a reputation as
the most brilliant rider in Australia.

Some correspondents—
Alba D. : Your remarks against tobacco sent on to King
Janies 1., of sainted memory. Pastor Fitchett is roughly
right, in as much as mo-t modern literary men take a
little tobacco for their nerves’ sake. What they did in an
age when tobacco was unknown has nothing to do with
the case : possibly they used another sedative, and
Homer indulged in some cunning drug coming out of
Egypt, such as Helen provided on the visit of Telemachus
to Sparta ?
Rebel writes : Why extol as poetry the dreary, choppily-
arranged, bizarre, and frequently far from refined prose
of that humourless literary crank—Walt Whitman ? Your
Meredith worship is another sweet insanity that many of
us marvel at, but it is much more reasonable than the
Whitman complaint. -Meredith undoubtedly has clever-
ness, but he will misapply it. Chapter after chapter in
“Richard Feverel” is like a separate essav, brilliant
enough at times, but it has nothing to do with the story.
In fact, there rarely is a story forthcoming when Mere-
dith allows his literary conceits to run away with him.
Tin reply : As to Meredith, it’s the old issue between
“good plain cooking ’’ and the ambrosial perfections of
the consummate chef. It needs a palate to appreciate the
latter. As .to Whitman, is the essential of poetry, form,
or poetic thought? If it is form, then some of the inani-
ties of Pope and the sterile sonnets of album versifiers are
the highest poetry. If it is thought—vigorous, original,
poetic thought—then Whitman is one of the world’s fine
poets.]

portance. The daughter of to-day becomes
the wife of to-morrow, and it is abso-

that good health should be hers; good
health which brings happiness and en-
sures vigour in posterity. A woman’s
nervous system is very highly strung.
Her mechanism is far more delicate and
complex than that of a man, and there
is little to wonder at when it breaks down
under the continuous high pressure of
modern life.
Most women require some aid to enable
them to support the daily stress and
strain, and this aid is found in WARNER’S
SAFE CURE a medicine wonderfully
adapted to their delicate organisation—
which has for twenty years secured the
grateful endorsement of women in all
walks of life in all parts of the civilised
world.

MANY COMPLICATIONS LEFT BEHIND.
All Banished and Health Restored
by CLEMENTS TONIC.
The Case of Mr J. BARTLETT.
(BY A SYDNEY REPORTER.)
It is to the interest of this country that
all citizens should be so educated that they
may become a self-reliant and prosperous
people, and to put this idea into practical
effect a reporter was commissioned to bring
before the community a matter here dealt
with by Mr. James Bartlett, of No. 5
Patten’s Buildings, Kent-street, Sydney.
“In the time of a great epidemic,” said
Mr. Bartlett, “ when influenza was ram-
pant in Sydney, I was one of the many
who failed to elude that terrible disease.”
“Were you in danger of meeting with
serious consequences ? ” asked the writer.
“ There was every likelihood that I
would never get well again, as I was in a
frightfully low state for many months.
Matters went so badly with me that I
thought I was fighting a hopeless fight
against an overwhelming force, and as I
I was laid up for weeks, and could not do a
stroke of work for seven months, I think I
had good cause to form that opinion. I got
so thin and ghastly white that everybody
thought; I was going into consumption, be-
sides getting awfully weak into the bar-
gain. 5) The pains in my head were enough
to run away with one’s senses, being of that
heavy, depressing nature which makes the
victim feel as if there is only care and
sorrow in the world, It was not the in-
fluenza itself that I had to regret so much
as the effects it left behind, for my system
seemed so upside down that there was not
a sound part about me. My limbs were so
racked with aches that if I walked a few
yards I had to sit down and rest for the
purpose of easing the pain, but another
reason why I could not go far without
taking a spell was because I used to turn
very giddy. Through that cause I nearly
fell down several times, so
that I was almost afraid to move in case
of meeting with an accident. I wondered
what was the good of living in such misery,
for I had no energy, no pleasure, no any-
thing tha,t was worth having. For weeks
and weeks I could not get any sleep, and
there was a feeling in my eyes as if they had
swelled up. A dark haze was visible at
every turn, my sight being so bad that I
could scarcely see. and if I wanted to get
anything for myself I had to grope about
as if it were dark. I could not hear very
distinctly either, owing to a strange drum-
ming in my ears, so that I was not much
use to anybody. Indeed, I was a nuisance
to myself, and would like to have been out
of the way of the persecuting pains which
came to the small of my back and almost
paralysed me whenever I stooped. This,
together with the thickness of my urine,
told me that my kidneys were disordered,
and I was afterwards told so by the doctors
whom I was treated by, with the additional
information that my liver was also dis-
eased.”
“ Did they give you any medicine ? ”
“Of course they did, and I was never
behind hand in taking it, as my enforced
absence from work meant a great loss to
me, and I wanted to get better. But, some-
how, my condition remained stationary.
When I examined my tongue it was al-
ways furred and rough, while my mouth
had a foul taste as if I had been drinking.
Not a morsel of food could I enjoy, but I
am not one to give in till I am fairly beaten,
and I used to make myself eat; but it
would have been better if I had spared my-
self the trouble, as everything seemed to
decay in the stomach, and give rise to ex-
cessive torture in the form of windy spasms.
My chest also felt as if a piece of lead had
l»dged there for the purpose of stopping my
breath, and there was no relief to be got
till I vomited everything up again. The
weakness of my stomach was so advanced
that I could not keep my food down, so
that accounts for my feebleness and the loss
of flesh I sustained. There was also some-
thing wrong with my heart, which palpi-
tated as if I had been indulging in some ex-
cessive exercise, and with my nerves all
gone I was completely wrecked from every
point of view. A twitching pain below my
neck irritated me, but for that matter I
was always in an irritable mood, even the
sound of voices raised in laughter being
sufficient to annoy me; but I was so
desperate and despondent that I did not
know what to do with myself. In any
short stretches of sleep I got there flashed
an awful picture upon my vision, and when
I woke up I could not help thinking thai
something I dreaded would surely happen,
although I recognised that death would
have been preferable to any more suffer-
ing.”
“ Why did you not try a change of
medicine
“If I had not done so I would not be
here to-day, as I was sinking very fast after
the long and torturing captivity in the
hands of my diseases, when a fellow-work-
man prompted me to try Clements Tonic.
It was not till I carried out his advice that
the influenza was beaten, and now, after a
long and convincing experience, I am sure
there is no medicine on earth like Clements
Tonic, for after taking a couple of bottles I
was feeling a new interest in existence.
The improvement was then so marked that
I ventured to hope that a possibility still
existed that I would yet recover, and by
closely pursuing my latest course of treat-
ment my heartfelt hopes were; gratified. I
started on the right track with a slight
appetite, which came through using
Clements Tonic, and which that medicine
continued to increase. Then my liver be-
gan to work more regularly, a fact which
afforded great relief in several ways. For
instance, my headaches slackened off, and
the sickly taste left my mouth, besides hav-
ing full use of my eyes in consequence of the
disappearance of the haziness before them.
The pains in my back and shoulders were
lighter than before, and I had no trouble m
breathing, as my chest was quite free from
oppression. A feeling of vigour crept
through my system through the medium of
Clements Tonic, and I was gaining in flesh
already, which showed that my food was
digesting well. I marvelled at the rapidity
with which my vomiting troubles were
stopped, while the action of Clements Tonic
on my nervous system could not be too
highly spoken of. When .my liver and
kidneys had been relieved of all symptoms
of disease by Clements Tonic I hardly knew
how to suppress the gladness I felt, es-
pecially as no cause remained for anxiety
as to the state of my heart, which ceased
palpitating shortly after flatulence was
remedied.”
‘ ‘ And now your health is perfect, I
believe ?”
“It could not be better, for I took
Clements Tonic till I was cured outright,
and for the past three years I have scaled
over 13 stone. lam thankful to be able to
give this information, which you can pub-

Various Verses.
Twilight.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When a heavy surf is droning,
In the twilight on the bar;
When our Mother Sea is crooning
Her quaint cradle-song afar ;
When the wild black swans are lining
Towards some still, remote lagoon,
And above the headland, shining,
Hangs a quiet, crescent moon ;
When the panoply, the splendor
Of the tropic sunset dies, —
Then my Fancy turns to tender
Dreams beneath the queenly skies.
Dear-loved Loadstone of my longing,
Fair, fond Woman of my heart!
When the twilight thoughts are thronging,
Art thou dreaming, too, apart ?
Yes, my Spirit echoes truly ;
Circling seas shall, with the tide,
Pulse on either shore of Thule,
In the Dream Beatified,
Surely as the mystic Crescent
Silvers now a garden fair,
Will the shining, white, liquescent
Light of Love burn also there !
So I mourn not that the splendor
Of the dead Day lies in pall,
When the Night her brooding, tender
Wings of fantasy lets fall.
In the dusk I’m sitting, building
Tall cloud-castles by the sea;
In the dusk my Love is gilding
Castles fair for her and me.
N.S.W.
E. J. BRADY.
Jenny.
I’ve had my fill of good and ill,
Of little gains and losses ;
As best I may, I meet each day,
Life’s minor joys and crosses.
I take the bitter with the sweet,
The light and dark together;
One day I shiver ’mid the sleet —
Next, frolic ’mid the heather.
But, while kind Heaven grants me breath,
One joy I’ll keep, I know.
My heart half danced itself to death
When Jenny jilted Joe !
Supple and sweet, from head to feet —
Her every glance entrances.
In deep surprise, ’neath her dear eyes,
I think of bygone fancies.
Her mouth is such as sun-gods long
To kiss, but can’t discover ;
Her speech is just the soul of song,
And gladdest for her lover.
I’m scant of brain, half-worn with years,
Poor as a rat —but, oh !
I took possession of the spheres
When Jenny jilted Joe !
On sorry quest —concealed, confessed—
Fools spend their trash and treasure ?
Blind dolts pursue each day anew
The grinding whirl of Pleasure.
I love at length the quiet ways
Where olden fragrance lingers;
At this world’s artificial bays
I snap uncaring fingers.
I care no whit for grief or fame.
For me —God willed it so —
The Beatific Vision came
When Jenny jilted Joe !
TAS.
FRANK MORTON.
The Green Harper.
Once again the music sweet,
With its magical refrain,
‘Through the noises of the street,
Steals into my heart and brain :
I am like a moonlit tree
Thrilled with silver melody.
Some enchantment in the room
Fills it with a radiance rare,
And a marvellous sweet bloom j
There is glamour in the air,
And my soul is drawn from me
By the wondrous melody.
Long ago, as poets tell,
Dectora, the shining Queen,
Rose and followed Forgael,
Whom men called the Harper Green ;
Followed him from Erin’s strand
Far and far to Fairyland.
Is it Forgael I hear
Making music magical ?
0 Green Harper, do not fear,
I will follow at thy call!
Over seas or mountains high
I will follow till I die.
Deep and dim in Fairyland,
Far beyond the Perilous Sea,
Lies the Wood of Broceliande
Where the haunted waters be—
Haunted lakes and singing streams,
And the high green Rath of Dreams.
There the knight whose sword was sharp,
Lancelot, in woodland dress,
Walks with Tristram of the Harp,
Lately come from Lyonesse—
Lyonesse that lies below
All the waves of long ago.
There, with beard as white as wool,
Merlin on the future dreams,
And Blanaid the Beautiful
Walks beside the singing streams
With fair Queens whose white and red
Loveliness made many dead.
Far-off is that country fair,
But the road is green and gay ;
0 Green Harper, lead me there,
Ere I take the darker way !
N.S.W.
VICTOR J. DALEY.

SATURDAY, MAY 31, 1902.
State Rights and Public Wrongs.
The recent conference of State Premiers left
behind it one or two sensible suggestions of
a small kind, a few vague remarks of no im-
portance, and a loud and hostile declaration
that it is necessary to protect State Rights
to the bitter end against the encroachments of
the Federal Parliament. Most of the Premiers
seemed to be much more earnest about State
Rights than about anything else, and in
view of the fact that there has lately been
a visible effort in a number of quarters to
raise an agitation about these rights, it would
be interesting to know exactly what they
are. Also it would be interesting to know
exactly what good they are, and who is in
charge of them, and what damage will be
done if they are lost, and—supposing they
are lost—what benefit will accrue when they
are found again, and to whom it will accrue.
The term has a loud, brassy sound. The
people who mouth it so freely try to make it
appear that State Rights are in some way
associated with the sacred cause of Liberty,
and that if the country fails to defend them it
will be ground under the heavy heel of
Oppression. And the fact that all this is
bunkum, that the people are likely to be
much happier without any State Rights, and
that the sooner they lose these alleged rights
the better it will be for them, is apt to be
overlooked amid all the whirling talk about
the heel of Oppression. Australia badly
wants one paper at least to rise up and vote
for putting State Rights in the garbage-box.
* ♦ * *
The question of State versus Federal Rights
is simply a question as to how far the people
shall govern themselves through the State
Parliament, and how far they shall do it
through the Federal Parliament. As it con-
cerns the public it is a question whether
elector Smith or voter Jones or constituent
Brown, being desirous of carrying some
measure or bringing about some political
end, shall give the necessary instructions
to his State representative Jenkins
or his Federal representative Robin-
son. At present, the Federal Parliament,
elected wholly by the people, has power to
legislate on certain matters, and the State
Parliament, elected partly by the people,
has power to legislate on certain other
matters. It is alleged by the upholders of
State Rights that the Federal Legislature
shows a villainous tendency to encroach
upon the domain of the State Legislature,
and the people are urged to resist this, and
to fight desperately for the preservation of
their liberties. The average elector Smith
has it pointed out to him that, in his Federal
capacity, he is showing a coarse and brutal
inclination to tread on himself in his State
capacity ; that he is jumping on himself
with the iron-shod hoof of tyranny ; that he
is stealing his own sacred rights and privi-
leges; and that if he doesn’t rise up and
sternly resent his own undue interference
with himself he will continue encroaching on
himself, till at last he finds himself a slave
owned by himself, and realises that he is
trodden helplessly under his own feet. That
is the case as regards the elector. As
regards the State Premiers, of course, it is
different. The maintenance of State Rights in
all their sacred integrity means that the
people shall, to a great extent, go on govern-
ing themselves through the medium of State
Premiers See, Philp, Peacock, Jenkins,
Lewis, and Leake. Therefore, the main-
tenance of State Rights is good for the
billets, dignities, and salaries of these per-
sons, and for the billets and salaries of their
colleagues and followers and possible suc-
cessors. But if the people, in their Federal
capacity, encroach on themselves in their
State capacity, a time may come when there
will be nothing left for the Philp and See
and Peacock crowd to do. That will be
bad for their billets, dignities, and remuner-
ation—also for their chances of getting
titles and other gauds. Naturally, they are
shocked at the idea of Slate Rights being
encroached upon, and urge the people to rise
against themselves and defend their liberties
to the last.
* • • *
In addition to the politicians whose State
jobs and dignities may vanish if State
Rights are lost, there are others whose
interests or prejudices point the same way.
The parochialists of the old .school—the
people to whom Australia is nothing while
their own State is everything-*-the Little
Australians who are not really Australians,
but only New South Welshmen, or Vic-
torians, or Queenslanders, or Tasmanians,
are with the State Rights people heart and
soul. The more power drifts into the hands
of the Australian Parliament, and the more
things are done from an Australian point of
view, the greater chance there is of the old
borders being obliterated, and the old dis-
tinctions, hatreds, prejudices, quarrels and
slanders being lost and forgotten. If the
Federal Parliament so far encroached on State
Rights that the railway systems were con-
solidated and new lines were built irrespec-
tive of State boundaries, the public debts
amalgamated, the State lands taken over by
the Central Government, the education
system placed under Federal control, the
civil and oriminal law made uniform and ad-
ministered by a purely Federal judiciary,
and the person condemned by that judiciary
put in a Federal gaol by a Federal police-
man, there would be so little use for the old
boundaries that the man on this side of a cer-
tain streak on the map might forget to have a
mean, unreasoning grudge against the man
on the other side. Then the old sacred
hatreds that Dibbs and John Robertson
spent half their lives struggling to keep alive
would be lost, and these and other persons
of their kind would have almost lived in
vain. In addition to the parochial clan, the
people who have axes to grind, and conces-
sions to angle for, and jobs to put through,
are mostly for State Rights, because the
State Parliaments are not so conspicuously
under the lime-light as the Legislature of
all Australia, and their moral atmosphere
seems more favourable to projects of Boodle.
And the Tories are for State Rights to a
man. The less power there is in the hands
of the Federal Parliament, wherein both
Houses are elected on the basis of one adult
one vote, the more power will remain to the
State Parliaments with their nominee Upper
Houses, or Upper Houses elected by a pro-
perty vote, and the better it will be for the
Tory interest.
• * * *
But to the mass of the people State Rights
are not worth the value of one long beer—or
half the value of the froth on the top of one
long beer. To the mass of the people it is
simply a question whether they will govern
themselves mostly through their State Par-
liaments or mostly through their Federal
Parliament, and their interests are in the
latter direction. They have absolute control
over their Federal Parliament, they elect
both Houses by the same suffrage and that
the most liberal suffrage on earth, members
of both Houses are paid, and a seat in either
House is open to any and every male elector
in the Commonwealth. And the people
have not absolute control over their State
Parliaments, where one half the Legislature
either consists of life nominees, or is elected
under a system which shuts out all save the
richest citizens ; and where even the other
half, in several of the States, is created
under restrictions and limitations. The cry
of State Rights is the cry of certain interested
parties demanding that the smallest possible
t/.are of political power shall be given to the
Australian Parliament, where the people are
absolutely supreme, and as large a share as
possible retained by the N.S. W. House of
Aged Nominees, and the Victorian Honseof 48
Rich Landlords, and the Queensland Nominee
Council and Property-vote Assembly. State
Rights, so far as the people are concerned,
are something to be carted away as refuse, or
burned by the common hangman.
• * * *
For the good of Australia it is to be hoped
that the Federal Parliament—the only
People’s Parliament in Australia—will extend
its powers by a vigorous process of encroach-
ment till it owns all the railways and all the
public lands of the Commonwealth, till it is
solely responsible for the public debt, till it
runs the police, the courts of justice, and
the schools, and establishes a uniform sys-
tem of law—in fact, till the State Parlia-
ments and State Governors are abolished,
and all their powers are centred in the Federal
authority. Then, for the further good of
Australia, it is to be hoped that the Federal
Legislature will abolish the old State
boundaries, wipe out the old State names,
and map out the Commonwealth afresh
into twenty or thirty divisions constructed
according to the natural features of the
country, and place each of these divisions
under a Local Government body of one
House—a body that will be more a county
council or divisional board than a Parlia-
ment—whose principal business it will be to
look after local works, apart from railways,
and to conduct itself quietly and orderly, as
a sober county council or divisional board
should. Then, the old States being gone, the
Senate, which is exclusively a House of
States, would naturally go also, and the
House of Representatives would constitute
the whole Parliament of Australia, to the
great acceleration of business.
* * « *
There is a long road to travel before that end
is achieved, but that, or something like it, is
the end that Australia should keep in view.
Australia has no room for State Rights now.
There is no reason why the elector, in his
State capacity, should become violently
hostile to himself in his Federal capacity
because See and Philp and Peacock tell
him to do so. There is no reason why he
should, at their bidding, seek to under-
mine or restrict the Federal Parliament,
which is wholly his own Parliament, in
order to preserve a large share of authority
for a State Parliament which is only partly
his Parliament, the other part being a body
of life nominees or a small clique of wealthy
property-owners. If the Legislature which
is wholly elected by the people can snatch
the authority out of the hands of the selfish
bigots who hold their places in the Tasmanian
and Victorian Legislative Councils solely
because the people’s choice is so restricted
by law that they have only a handful of
selfish bigots to choose from, or if it can
seize the power now held by the doddering
placemen of the N.S.W. and Queensland
Councils, there is no reason why the people
should object. So far from that, it is their
duty and their interest to stand by their
Federal Parliament all the time, to support
it in every encroachment it makes on State
authority, and to incite it to further encroach-
ments. Even if the State Parliaments are
reformed, this reasoning still holds good. ' It
is practically impossible, out of less than
4,000,000 people, to find enough good material
to make seven honest, capable and intelligent
Parliaments. It is probably possible to find
enough to make one. Therefore it becomes
a question of entrusting that one body with
a general supervision of all the country’s
important concerns, or giving it half the
power and leaving the other half to six sets
of incapables. The mental calibre of the
State Parliaments has slumped dismally since
the handful of leading men quitted them to
go into Federal politics, and there is nothing
in the quality of the six remnants to induce
the public to demand that any large affairs
be left to their management.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Municipal Convention.
The movement for the amalgamation of the 43, or
thereabouts, municipalities of Sydney makes a
little progress. Premier See has no plan himself
for placing the dishevelled city under a sane and
useful municipal government, but he has promised
to introduce a Bill authorising the -election of a
convention to draft the necessary scheme. It
remains to be seen whether the convention will be
elected on such a basis as to make the movement
a really popular one, and what energetic steps
Premier See will be prepared to take to carry its
decisions into law. So far as the general principle
goes, however, it has much to recommend it when
a tired and inefficient Government has no pro-
gressive ideas of its own. There is no reason at
all, save tiredness, why the Government shouldn’t
be its own convention, but being much exhausted,
the next best thing it can do is to delegate the hard
work to somebody else. It has taken years of
agitation and worry to get it to do even that. The
convention idea was necessary, in the case of the
Federal Constitution, where six States had to be
consulted, but in this case the matter lies wholly
within one State, and the need arises solely from
the fact that the N.S.W. Ministry has almost
given up tackling large, intricate, difficult sub-
jects. The new system is undoubtedly good for
the people. Things that the Government is too
debilitated to do can be thus rescued from
oblivion, for even a debilitated Government can
authorise the election of a convention and the sub-
mission of its scheme to the popular vote after-
wards especially as the old Bill which authorised
the Federal Convention will do with a few altera-
tions. The convention does the work—it may be
done well or it may be done badly, but it is done,
for it is the only business that the convention has
before it and thus something is kept moving.
The idea prevents the people petrifying;
it furnishes them with a method of attend-
ing to public business when public busi-
ness might otherwise fall down dead; il
prevents stagnation becoming general, and it
teaches the community to help itself. Conven-
tions elected for special purposes may yet become
one of the regular features of public life. At the
same time, Premier See has just been attending
a conference where he bellowed very loudly for
the maintenance of the rights of the State Parlia-
ment, and this tired way of doing things isn’t
calculated to make the public care much for the
rights of the State Parliament. He was assisted
in his bellowing by two other Premiers, who were
both lately pledged to call together similar con-
ventions for the. drafting of new State constitu-
tions because their Governments didn’t feel equal
to the work—the fact that they failed to carry out
their promises doesn’t alter the case. The Bulle-
tin is most cordially in favor of the convention
idea in the present case. It also desires to see a
convention elected to draft a new State constitu-
tion, and it would particularly like to see one
created to draft a complete scheme for keeping
this country going, without any more loans and for
repaying the existing, debts. But the whole thing
is a wild, weird joke in view of the complaints of
See and. co. about the Federal Parliament
encroaching on their political domain. Almost in
the same day See and co. have to agree to create
a special body to encroach on their political
domain, to prevent the domain going utterly to
seed.

The Mechanical Foreign-Trade Mind.
The most hopeless feature of the Freetrade party’s
mental outlook is that it has learned its case
parrot-fashion from books published in England
and dealing with English conditions, and it has
no knowledge, information or inference outside its
books. There is nothing in these English books
dealing with Australian conditions, and ,the book-
pedants can’t see these conditions because they
are not in its books. The two sets of circum-
stances are these:
(1) England adopted Freetrade when it was the great
creditor nation, of the world ; it had probably £50,00.1,000
a year coming in from foreign loans and investments.
(2) It adopted Freetrade when it was the great sea-
carrier of the world, and was receiving about £30,000,000
a year for carrying other nations’ goods.
(3) It adopted Freetrade when it was the greatest and
cheapest manufacturing nation in the world and was
ahead of all competitors.
England could afford, when it began its Freetrade
policy, to buy perhaps £80,000,000 worth annually
more than it sold because the outside world had
to pay it that amount. So a policy which
encouraged imports might suit it well enough.
And because of that the Millens, Goulds,
Conroys, Pulsfords and other men wise and
dreary in English book-lore are quite convinced
that the same Freetrade policy of encouraging
imports must suit Australia, which is deeply in debt
and has to keep its imports some £15,000,000 a year
below its exports merely to pay interest and avoid
getting further in debt. The unspeakably doleful
Pulsford recently quoted figures about England’s
prosperity for two solid days, ' just as if England
had started from the same place as we do, and as
if England’s example had any bearing on our ease.
The hide-bound Pulsford theory is that because
a man who lives in London can walk to Dover by
going south-easterly, therefore a man who lives in
Adelaide must also walk south-east and fall into
the ocean if he wants to go to Dover. England
can afford to import more than it exports, partly
because it receives £15,000,000 a year from money
invested in Australia ; therefore, in the Pulsford
philosophy, Australia is equally able to import
more than it exports because it has to pay that
£15,000,000 a year. The purely mechanical mind
is a dull, dry, soulless thing.

A Labor Programme.
The reform programme just put forth by the
Victorian Labor party—every party is putting
forth a reform programme just now—has many
good points, but on the whole it will do the party
as much harm as good. It shows, in one respect,
a curious inability to shake off old precedents.
The Labor manifesto suggests that the Legislative
Council should consist of 23 members elected by
23 constituencies, and the Assembly of 69, elected
by 69 constituencies, the constituencies to be in
each case on a population basis. Then it proposes
each House should be re-elected every three years,
be based on adult suffrage, with no property quali-
fication for members, and that members of each
House should be paid at the rate of £3OO a year.
Seeing that, under these circumstances, the two
Houses would feel exactly the same way and say
exactly the same thing, the glaring absurdity of
having two Houses is patent. The only possible
use of a Second Chamber which, unless there is
some swindle in the method of election, will
always say ditto to the first one is to pro-
vide 23 superfluous persons with billets. There
is an insuperable difficulty about the Second
Chamber idea. If the Lower House fairly
and honestly represents the people, then its deci-
sions must prevail. If the Upper House says no,
it has to be overruled at great worry and loss of
time; if it says yes, the writer Could sell the Vic-
torian Labor members for 10s. 6d. a small greenish
parrot which says the same thing, and which
would make an equally good Upper House. The
rest of the Victorian Labor programme is a curious
mixture of shrewdness and narrow-mindedness.
It includes an elective Ministry, reduction of the
number of Ministers to five, the initiative and the
referendum, “ rigid economy in all State depart
ments every programme includes that , and the
abolition of the State Governor, all of which ideas
are more or less commendable. Then it goes on
to lay it down that the Ministerial allowances
must not exceed £2OOO a year—and if that means
as it appears to do, five Ministers at £4OO each, it
can only excite pity for the party that expects to
buy brains enough to run the State for the wage
of a superior clerk. Brains must be paid for
like everything else, and five good Ministers
would be cheap at £4OOO each. It is also
recommended that all civil service salaries exceed-
ing £3OO a year should be cut down, which is also
an absurdly cast-iron way of doing things. There
are public service billets with salaries of far more
than £3OO a year where the pay ought be doubled
rather than reduced, and then the country ought
to make sure that it gets a man who is worth the
extra pay. The country doesn’t always get a
good man for a good wage, but it invariably gets
an * n * enor man for an inferior wage, and he
probably saves the country £5OO a year in salary
and loses it £50,000 in bad management. The
programme further proposes to repudiate, in part
at least, the unlucky bargain Victoria made in
the early garish days with its civil service pen-
sioners. It was a costly bargain—the pension
bill runs to something like £300,000 a year; but
still the pensioners are dying off, and the old
disastrous law has been long since repealed, and


a breach of faith, bad as the bargain may have
been, would be utterly unpardonable. And there
is not a word in the programme about making the
country independent of the foreign usurer, about
abandoning the disastrous theory that prosperity
may be reached by getting deeper into debt, or
about establishing a public works policy based on
revenue only. Apparently the Victorian Labor
party is no more aware that there is a financial
, problem that requires solution than it is personally
aware of the bunyip.

Barton’s Funk.
Alfred Deakin’s statement as to the defeated
attempt to increase Lord Hopetoon’s annual
salary by £BOOO “allowances,” shows how pitifully
Premier Barton funked his responsibility in the
matter until he was on the point of getting away
to the Coronation spree. On Jan. 11, 1901, Joe
Chamberlain wrote to his official friend, Hope-
toun, advising him to fall in with the resolution,
passed at the Premiers’ conference held early in
1900, to the effect that “ Governors should not be
expected to supplement their salaries from their
private means.” A previous Josephian despatch
had urged, without success, a more expensive
state of things. Finding that the original £lO,OOO
figure was the popular Australian idea of a maxi-
mum salary for the Gov.-General, the wily man
at Downing-street now asked his pupil to go
slow, and be guided by the “living within his
salary” principle. “I consider,” wrote J.C. on
Jan. 11, 1901, “ that this principle in which
the Premiers expressed their concurrence, and
which, I have no doubt, they would agree applies
to the Gov.-General as well as to State Governors,
is of the greatest importance, since it makes it
possible for the Crown to select a suitable person
for the high office of Her Majesty’s representative
without requiring of him a heavy pecuniary sacri-
fice. From the point of view of public interest I
consider it important that you should limit your
hospitality in capitals other than Sydney, strictly
to such official entertainment as you may consider
necessarily demanded, unless and until some fair
and suitable provision be made by the States or
by the Commonwealth for the expenses involved.”
Certainly there are no flies on Joe Chamberlain’s
social statesmanship. He told Hopetoun to cut
his pomp to suit his salary, and confine his enter-
tainments to Sydney (which Joseph evidently
thinks is the Federal capital), just as the Canadian
potentate restricts his little extravagances to
Ottawa. So much for the private instructions from
Downing-street. When they were passed along to
Premier Barton, by way of forcing him to approve
or disapprove, that clean-shaven procrastinator
replied (Feb. 21, 1901) that the Ministry had
agreed to submit a measure to Parliament which
would meet the situation, and this measure, says
Deakin, was decided on previous to the arrival of
the Chamberlain letter. “A Bill granting the
Gov.-General £BOOO a year allowances till the
Federal capital was in existence ” lay peacefully
on Barton’s conscience for fully 14 months before
he dared to plank it down upon the table of the
House. _ All that time Hopetodn— poor man !
was living with an accent on the first syllable of
his ancestral title. He gazed wistfully at Premier
Toby whenever they met at some “function” or
other, and Toby pretended to be blind to the
pathos in the vice-regal eye. The stout gentleman
was playing for safety—and a trip to the Corona-
tion. He has secured the outing, but the glare in
Hopetoun’s eye, when they next meet, ought to
make even him uncomfortable.

Bombala—the Federal Capital.
J cst now Sydney is close up to a water famine,
and Brisbane is closer still; in fact, it is reckoned
that unless there is heavy rain between now and
September the Brisbane River will run dry.
Most of the proposed Federal capitals are to-day
like the shifting wastes of Sahara or the hobs of
Gehenna. But when the visiting members of the
House of Representatives visited the Monaro
district of N.S. Wales the other day in search
of a Federal city, they found, even in this
season of desperate drought, enough pure
water flowing down the Snowy River to supply
the whole metropolitan area of Sydney 1\ times
over, and yet the Snowy was lower than it had
been for 35 years. This was at 3000 ft. above sea-
level, so that, if the Federal city was planted
almost anywhere thereabouts, abundance of water
for a population of 600,000 souls or more, could be
taken to it inexpensively by gravitation. Even
the wildly abusive language of Melbourne Age—
whose special axe, whatever axe it may be,
seems to be one that can’t be conveniently
ground up on Monaro —fails to disguise the
fact that the great New South Wales
tableland is the only place that has any
fair claim to be in the running for the
Federal capital. That city will, in time, gather a
large population round it, and Australia needs to
gather that population on the cool highlands,
where it is possible to breed a sturdy race, not to
concentrate more and more people in the sultry,
breathless plains where the community grows
tired and puts things off till to morrow. It needs
to put its new population alongside permanent
water, so that it can wash and irrigate, and the
present drought points to the fact that the Snowy
and other adjacent streams are almost the only
absolutely permanent waters in New South
Wales. The Bombala site is the only one yet
suggested which can have a good port of its own
■on Federal territory. It is easily defensible —
though it is, close to the sea, yet a mountain range
:3000ft. high takes a great deal of storming. The
soil is reasonably fertile. The population is thin,
which will make the process of resumption
cheaper, and the site appears to have the
venomous disapproval of the Age, which is,
perhaps, something to its credit. Among all these
recommendations it is difficult to say which is the
most important, but the water question is at least
a leading feature. Sydney and Brisbane, with
their comparatively small populations, yet on the
verge of water famine, have this year got some
slight indication of what may happen to an Aus-
tralian city of a million or two million people in
these lowlands, with an enormously increased
demand for water, and not one absolutely reliable
stream—one warranted to keep flowing in all
droughts between them and Bourke. The
necessity .of attracting population to the high-
lands, where there is permanent water, should
seriously occur to the Australian people now if it
never occurred seriously before.

The Plague—its Unmerciful Mildness.
Most unhappily the 1902 plague season seems to
be passing away without such a record as would
thoroughly scare the people of dirty Sydney into
sanitary whoiesorneness. It has claimed victims,
provided on some days mild sensations, but has
never come out with really startling figures ; and
already the brief spasm of cleanliness goes by,
and heads nod again preparatory to slumber in
the old filth. Sydney is afflicted with all manner
of dirt diseases —typhoid and dysentery, due
mainly to dirty milk; pneumonia and consump-
tion, arising from filth and damp; scarlet fever,
scarlatina, diphtheria, coming from bad drains
and rotten garbage heaps. All these, like the
poor, we have always with us, and are re-
conciled to having with us. Though the
last outbreak of pneumonia killed five times
as many people as the present outbreak of
plague, and maintained an appalling average of
mortality, it excited no scare, suggested no big
cleaning up. But the Blague, the Black Death,
the Pest, has a series of awe-inspiring names and
it frightens—frightens the individual into a bath,
and the town council into a hurried destruction of
aged accumulations of garbage. So, with its com-
paratively limited range of selection and its, after
all, comparatively low mortality rate, the Plague
really comes as a blessing. If this 1902 outbreak
had been severe, had for one month set itself to
establish a record, there would have been
some reasonable hope of a rat-proof, cleaned-
up and possible-to-be-kept-cleaned-up, properly-
drained, properly-scavenged Sydney. And the
result would have been such a marked diminution
in scarlet fever, consumption, pneumonia, diph-
theria, and typhoid mortality as would have
recouped the community a hundred-fold for the
lives lost whilst the Plague was raging. But,
unfortunately, it so far has not raged, and threatens
to flicker out —to come back next year, and next
year, and never to secure a real reform of Sydney’s
sanitation until it claims at least a thousand
victims at one fell swoop. The community forgets
scores and hundreds in a week or less. To be
heeded, the Pest must speak in big figures. Or,
perhaps, Fate will not be so merciful as to be con-
tent with Plague-warnings. The next visitor may
be Cholera, for which Sydney and Brisbane
offer the choicest of hunting-grounds, and its
victims would be counted not in one, but in many
thousands.

Appraisement—All or None.
Some remarks from a N.S.W. politician :
Not the least peculiar feature of the peculiarly-
managed N.S.W. Lands Department is the re-appraisement
scheme which now almost constitutes the sole business of
every land board in the Central Division. By allowing
re-appraisement the Lands Dept, practically admits that
it is a various kind of ass, because it sends one body of
“experts” to value the land before selection, and appoints
another body and a land board to reduce that valuation
after selection. The first thing the new selector does is
to demand a re-valuation, for the appraisement idea has
got such a firm grip on the “ man on the land ” that, no
matter what value is placed on a block, it is considered
too high, and the Lands Department is viewed in the
same light as an uncle from Fiji. Obviously, then, the
first valuation has no merit and can be dispensed with,
or guesswork would do equally well and save much need-
less expense. Now, there are two simple ways of
knocking out completely this appraisement folly : (1) a
measure to compulsorily appraise every holding
in N.S.W., so that the fellow who has his
land below its real value can come up a
few steps, and the high-priced one get on fair
terms by going down a bit. It’s a poor kind of scheme
which doesn’t cut both ways. But what sorb of a howl
would be raised if such a measure were proposed, and
what a chance it would have of becoming law 1 It would
terrify the bush M.L.A., who makes himself solid with the
new selector by yelling for appraisement—that blessed
word would lose its charm if there was any risk of it fall-
ing heavily on the old one. (2) Make every future selector
■ put his own valuation on the land, i.e., make so many
blocks available for settlement, and invite tenders for
each at so much per acre per annum, the highest bidder,
provided he satisfied the Department he was eligible, and
his offer wasn’t below the fixed reserve figure, to take it,
and at his own price. This would have the merit of being
business-like, and certainly would compare favorably with
the present clumsy method, which pries into an applicant’s
antecedents—his character, his financial position—neces-
sitating the appointment of a board, which generally ends
its inquiries by giving a block to the man with the largest
family ; or the ballot system, which, according to Senator
Drake, must come under the definition of gambling, and
is therefore a thing which no self-respecting Department
could tolerate for a moment.

The Two Judases.
Papers and politicians have tried to ram it down
Australia’s throat that it is the accepted rule for
the patriot who opposes a war to keep silent about
it once the guns speak. In England, however,
neither now nor hitherto, has such a rule been
generally accepted, though here and there it is
screechingly insisted upon as though it were
something not to be argued about. The modern
fods in whose interests the demand for gagging
onest (and probably right) opinion is made
never had any respect for this alleged
rule, themselves. Follow Judas Chamber-
lain’s career. Since he went into politics
the most serious wars up to this have been
with the Boers, the Afghans, and the Zulus. All
these he opposed. “ What is the use of being
great and powerful if we are afraid to admit an
error when we are conscious of it ? ” he asked in
’Bl, speaking of the peace with the Boers. “ Our
losses,” he went on, “did not make the original
cause of the war more just; they did not make
the prolongation of this miserable and inglorious
struggle more desirable and expedient.” And so
Chamberlain and his then party-fellows opposed
further bloodshed—“ the vain pursuit of military
glory ’’—and opposed it successfully. Lord Hart-
ington (now Duke of Devonshire) was leader of
the Opposition when the Tories plunged England
into the Afghan and Zulu wars. Hartington
moved a vote of censure, and incidentally referred
to this fictitious rule of silence :—•
If it were necessary to refer to precedents I could point
to the conduct pursued by the Opposition at the dose of
the last and the beginning of the present century in the
course of the War of Independence with the American
colonies and the Revolutionary War in France. With
respect to the first case, at all events, there is no doubt
that the Opposition took a wise and patriotic course in
continuing to oppose the Government during the war.
But it is unnecessary to refer to Precedent, for Reason
points to the same conclusion.
Chamberlain spoke to the motion and referred
to—
Those members who had invented a convenient theory
by which opposition was silenced in the presence of
foi eign complications. If we attempt discussion before
war breaks out, we are hampering the Govt, in negotia-
tions and endangering peace. If we wait till war is upon
us, then it is said that, in an unpatriotic way, we are
dividing the country in the presence of the enemy. If
we postpone discussion until the war is over, then we are
told we are guilty of futile fault-finding.
But there is hardly one first or second-rank man
who has not seen that the alleged rule of silence is
mere bluff, and that the patriot’s duty is clearly
to courageously attempt to save his country if he
believes she is being misled to ruin. Salisbury
(then Robert Cecil) saw it in the Chinese war *
Lansdowne in the Zulu war ; and Rosebery in
the Afghan war, against the continuance of which
he voted.

The Kyabram Sweaters.
The party with the “Kyabram programme”
continues to make a loud and insufferable ass
of itself in Victoria. The day after Premier
Peacock announced his programme at Clunes a
whole stack of M.’sL. A. were interviewed by the
daily papers, and their almost unanimous decision
was that “ tremendous economies will have to be
exercised in the public service.” And not one
blockhead of the crowd seemed to know where
they were to be effected. The short history of the
Kyabram-programme crowd is that it wants a
high civilisation at about half the price of bar-
barism. Its State has nearly six times as much
railway accommodation proportionately to popu-
lation as the people of Great Britain have, and it
has lower fares and freights in many cases than
they have, and it is always howling for more
railway and still lower charges, and it demands
that the lines be kept in better repair, which
means more employees and more cost, and it
writes to the papers when railway employees are
overworked, and at the same time it wants the
staff reduced, and the lines made to pay better.
It has free education, and it would raise Cain and
his dead brother, Abel, if it were made to pay 6d.
per week for each child so as to diminish the bulk
of the education vote in the national balance-
sheet. In fact, it is always demanding more
school accommodation. It howls for more police
protection, and less money to be spent. It
demands that expensive contingents be sent to
South Africa, and it approves cordially of old-age
pensions, to save it supporting its own aged
father ; and having driven the Government into
spending large sums on these items it asks
furiously why the expenditure is increasing, and
wants to have the country run as cheaply with
old age pensions and contingents as it was without
them. It raises 25 times more row over a local
rate of Is. 4d. in the £ than they do in London
over one of 6s. 6d. Among all the dozens of
reformers who have insisted on an instant cutting
down of something, there isn’t one who has shown
anything of any size that can be cut down. Vic-
toria is a very cheaply-managed and lightly-taxed
country—some of its services are going to wreck
through being run too cheaply. And a vast pro-
portion of the ignorant sweaters who run the
“reform” movement should paint themselves
black and take to the bush as aborigines. That is
the way to get really cheap government.

“Vulcan ” writes
It amuses (and galls) me to watch Australia in general,
lying in noisy and inactive admiration of itself, bragging
of its “ Democracy,” of its “ Socialistic Institutions,”
etc., resting under the fond delusion that it is a particu-
larly bright and shining example to “ Tory England ” in
the ways she should go, and slowly degenerating into
the backward and stupid pupil of the country it had
thought to teach. A case or two in point (to he read side
by side with the reports of the Snow Tramway Grab, for
instance !): —Hull (England) municipal trams returned a
net profit of £40,000 last year, and of this, after deducting
£lO,OOO for the relief of the rates, £30,000 was put by in
three divisions: (1) to a sinking-fund for the repayment
of the original cost of the system ; (2) for payment of in-
terest on the above ; (3) to a sinking-fund for the renewal
of obsolete plant, etc. Now, of the profits of the Sydney
tram system for the last 15 years, what amount
has been put aside for the redemption of the
original debt or for the substitution of more up-to-
date systems as they arise ? To record that
amount as nil is bad enough, but to that add this :
Having lately initiated a new system without having yet
paid for the old, Sydney, under the mismanagement of its
addle-pated bosses, will shortly be not only paying stiff
interest on the new trams, but an equally stiff interest on
those old ones, which the new have practically consigned
to the Moore Park dirt-tip ! . . . Melbourne and Brisbane
and Adelaide are not mentioned in this connection, for the
reason that, in allowing their city tramways to fall
into the clutches of private cos., they are beneath
criticism. Again, in Liverpool (Eng.) some .years
ago the local council took over the steam ferry service
running between Birkenhead and Liverpool, improved it,
ran it a few years with varying success, until, in 1901, it
at last showed a net profit of over £13,000. Now, the
Liverpool-Birkenhead service of to-day could no more be
bought for five times the sum given for the “ puffing
billies ” of some few years ago than the Sydney Ferries of,
say, 20 years hence could be purchased for ten times the
amount that would easily acquire them to-day. But
what effort has the Sydney City Council ever made to
become possessed of this magnificent revenue-producer
while it is yet comparatively cheap ? And having made
none whatsoever, why ? But perhaps the fact that writer
saw a bundle of Harbor-Ferries shares bulging from a
city alderman’s pocket t’other day answers the last
question !
+
The Federal Constitution declares that “ uniform
duties of customs shall be imposed within two
years” after the beginning of the Commonwealth.
The two years expire on 31st Dec. next, and if it is
held, as seems to be the case, that duties are not
legally “imposed” till the tariff is passed then
there are just seven months for that tariff to get
through. If it doesn’t get through it is alleged that
the old State tariffs will come in force again, border
duties included. Then there will be no legal means
of making a uniform tariff; and all the millions
collected in Federal duties must be handed
back—only, as they have been spent they can’t
be handed back. The Government made all
reasonable speed at drafting the tariff, but nearly
a year has elapsed since it was introduced, and
the dreadful Conroy has made something like
200 speeches about it, and every time the Govern-
ment tried to push things along a little, Sydney
Smith raised a stonewall. Every attempt to get
anything like a move on the business brought up
the gang of foreign importers with a lament about
“ stifling free discussion.” Now, after about ten
months’ talk, and with the measure about half
through, and the anti-Australian-industry party
still in full blast, and the biggest possible dead-
lock only seven months ahead, affairs are in-
teresting.
4
When the Australian democrat gets a little time
on his hands he will do well to use it finding out
how much democracy he really possesses. Hardly
a week passes, for instance, without a Premier in
a State or Federal House informing members that
such or such a communication from the British
authorities has been marked “private and con-
fidential” and cannot, therefore, be divulged.
After events show that in many cases the communi-
cations dealt with current questionsof local politics,
such as a White Australia or the emoluments of
a Governor. Only that they are meant to have
an effect on current or impending politics the com-
munications would be unnecessary. But if they
are so meant, is Australian democracy legislatively
what it claims to be ? Parliament controls the
Premier, the Ministry, it is true ; and the electors
control Parliament. But at many junctures the
Premier, in practice, controls the Ministry, and
the Ministry controls Parliament, and Parliament
laughs at the country. With a “ private and con-
fidential” influence at work, the measure of
democracy is consequently the measure of the
Premier’s democracy. An autonomous people
should discourage, condemn, penalise a Govern-
ment that accepts, on its current or impending
politics, “private and confidential” communica-
tions from any outside authority.

“ Glenaka ” writes from south-west Victoria :
Tired at last of the bitterly unequal struggle against
Nature the mallee settlers are leaving that woefully
drought-stricken region and are making for the plains in
the south-western corner of Victoria. Human wonders,
these men. Year by year they have battled on ; hoping
against hope ; waiting for the good seasons that never
came ; sowing but never reaping anything worth while.
Now, plucky as ever, with nothing but their teams and
battered machinery, they are clearing for new country,
hoping to “ make a rise.” Had Vic. Govt, attended to
its business properly they would undoubtedly have made
it.- The S.W. land—of medium quality, manurable,
reliable, well-watered, easily-worked and specially adapted
for wheat-growing —was offered to the Lands Dept, at its
grazing value, about £3 an acre, and the local press and
politicians strongly urged the buying of it and letting it
out again for farming purposes. But Peacock and Co.
were very busy at that time, endeavoring to get a “ dis-
loyal ” member fired out of Parliament. Anyway, they
said, the Govt, was only buying first class land ; and this
was ranked as third class. As if it mattered if it were
23rd class, provided it gave profitable employment to a
brave and deserving portion of the population and a
remunerative return to the Crown ! Now, the rush of
applicants has caused a land-boom on the Mt. William
and surrounding plains. The straight-out buying price
has been doubled already, and rents have been raised
accordingly. And the men who have faced so much
already will find themselves burdened with a heavy rent
or interest bill, and have to work for the benefit of the
squatter-owners. And for this the}' can thank Peacock
and Co.
_ 4-
It is suggested in Queensland that the State con-
trol of licenses may be worked to good purpose.
For instance, the Federal Parliament may take
the duty off foreign boots, and they will come
into the State free. But if the State enacts that
nobody shall sell foreign boots without paying
£lOO license, or that no foreign boots will be sold
unless they bear a ss. inland-revenue stamp on the
sole, the State will certainly be ahead —for the
time being, at all events. The idea contains the
germ of a larger reform than its advocates are
aware of. A time will probably come when all
shopkeepers will have to pay a license —ditto all
agents, brokers, merchants, dealers, lawyers, and
the like. In every country there should be just
the smallest number of traders and middlemen
and other non-producers that will suffice to
do the work, instead of the largest number that
can scratch an existence out of the business.
The wealth of a country is made by its producers.
Every needless middleman is a producer the less.
The more middlemen that intrude themselves
between the producer and the consumer, the less
the producer gets and the more the consumer
pays. When sixteen shops are making a pre-
carious living selling the goods that could be dis-
posed of in four shops, then 12 shopkeepers spend
most of their time loafing and dusting the stock ;
12 producers are lost; and 12 needless shop-rents
and gas-bills have to be added to the consumer’s
bills or deducted from the price the producer
receives. The superfluous shopkeeper, who spends
one-sixth of his time in actual work and the rest
in waiting for customers, is a sorrowful example of
the waste of human material.
. 4
Young man, go and be a schoolmaster —in South
Australia. South Australia (writes a Bulletin
correspondent)
has just about solved the problem of how to get its State
school teaching done efficiently at a sweating wage. The
youthful candidate who passes for pupil-teacher works
some time at a nominal wage, provided that he can teach.
Then two years are spent at the pupil teachers’ school,
two more in actual training, and, lastly, two years in
study at the University Training College. After these
six years, which brings him up to the minimum average
age of 22 years, he is, if in luck, classified F—the lowest
grade possible. Also, if he is lucky enough to get an
appointment as an assistant straight off—there is no
guarantee—he starts at £BO a year, and by seven yearly
increments he may, given good reports, reach the mag-
nificent salary of £l5O a year—aged, on an average, at
least 29 years. It is specially provided that “no one can
hold the position of first assistant till the expiration of
seven years at least after leaving the University
Training College.” After his training course, should
he elect to take a country school, he starts at £9O
a year, and in the seventh year, by way of increments,
he may, granted good reports, attain to £l6O a year,
“with £lO a year deducted throughout when living
accommodation is provided.” Further, in S.A. the city
head-master is a petty autocrat, armed with great powers
of report. Woe to the benighted assistant who knows
not how to grovel or gives evidence of vertebrae—he may
look to his portmanteau. Also, the assistant is expected
—another word for “must”—to control by personal
influence, moral suasion, personal charm, &c. Ye gods !
Think of it—thirteen years for best men to reach £l5O a
years—a whimsical head-master or inspector may extend
it to 20 years ! Thirteen years at least before getting a
first assistantship at £l6O to £2OO a year by increments !
That is 17 years, all going well, to attain to £2OO a year as
an assistant—this at an average age of 33 years.
Were evidence needed} f the curious state of the
Yic. Railways rolling-stock, the Parliamentary
committee appointed to enquire into the depart-
mental ways could supply plenty. Two foremen
engine-cleaners let in a flood of light the other day.
Said one, “He had not had sufficient cleaners
for 6or 7 years. He wanted 35 extra men at
least. The rule was that an engine should be
cleaned every 100 miles it ran, and this was
observed till Mr. Speight retired. Since then it
had been go-as-you-please and he cleaned the
engines whenever he could.” Another, “He had
not enough cleaners ; in years gone by he had 67
cleaners for his average 50 engines per night, now
he only had 14 or 15. The result was that engines
frequently ran warm, sustained damage and often
had to be laid up for a week or more. Owing to
the shortage of cleaners some men had had no
holiday for two years.” Two drivers corroborated
this evidence, one going so far as to state that
there was hardly an engine on the lines in a first-
class state. And a third-class engine running on
a second-class road often makes a first-class
smash.
