The “Reform Movement ” in Victoria has called
forth some rich gems of Tory utterance from the
local Fatman. For example, Capt. Pursey
Chirnside (salute !) wrote to his admiring coun-
trymen at Ballan, telling them that “Parliament
will never be reformed until electors place men
who have a stake and interest in the country
there,” which was Purser's way of explaining that
payment of members is the root of all parliamen-
tary evil. And yet, if Pursey were to take a
brain-tonic and lock himself up in his study and
brood upon wisdom for two hours before dinner,
it might dawn upon his foggy intellect that a sure
way of giving a man a stake and interest in the
government of a country is to make him a paid
member of the Legislature. The Labor member
at £3OO a year, who wants the money badly,
is very much interested in governing the country
wisely ; if he can’t show good results he loses his
billet. He has a much arger stake in the country
than the man who can sell his properties or float
them into a company, and put the draft for
£151,376 into his pocket, and take a saloon passage
for London never to return. The man with a real
stake in the country is the man without means to
pay his own and his family’s passage away from
it. The rich man, in many cases, only wants to
keep the country afloat till he has time to realise
his properties, or float his ca,ts, and after that it
may go hang.
The question of maintaining a little navy of our
own or contributing to the British one has been
well put in this fashion : Suppose Britain is
licked, badly licked, by a sudden attack—and
every nation is badly licked some day. The
victor will require a cession of territory—and the
territory required may be Australia. We may
db handed over like sheep to France or Germany
or Russia. Then we must rely on our own forces
to resist the cession. An Australian navy would
make a resistance. A subsidised British navy
would be bound by the terms of the treaty, and
would have to turn against us the very guns we
paid for to enforce the surrender. And that is
one good reason why we should have our own
navy—not paid for out of loans but out of taxa-
tion, as the British navy is.

A gloom was cast over Sydney Criterion
last week by the news that “ The Christian ” was
to be produced on Saturday before a heart-broken
public. “The Christian ”is the account of ona John
Storm who strove to be an ancient disciple in a tail
coat and modern boots, and came into collision with
a non-enthusiastic 19th century in consequence.
This stage sermon has been presented once or
twice in Sydney before, and Sydney was surprised
and pleased to find that the Criterion’s lady drama-
tist presented a brighter and brisker version than
it had hitherto seen. The Monastery Scene is
ejected altogether. This is possibly to be
commended. It was a large, dreary act in the
other version, and the moral it preaches against
celibacy isn’t required in this country. On the
other hand, its disappearance leaves a hiatus in
the gradual maddening of John Storm. Steadily
through the book John’s tail grows longer and his
eye wilder, and the monastery was one of the
influences which contributed to the lengthening of
John’s tail and the kindling of a flame in his
eye. He was the end of an ill-balanced curate
when he went in, and he was the beginning
of a deranged “Father” when he came
out, and the dropping of the monastery
disconnects the story of his mental decay.
The vacuum caused by the vanishment of the
Brotherhood of the Holy Gethsemane is filled up
with scenes of low-class London life, including a
picture of the Mission. Hall, where the children
are ranged under texts half a yard high, and sung
to sleep with a coon song instead of a hymn. One
of the Refuge girls interpolates the coon song,
which is of the “Sleep, Ma Honey” order. She
does it evidently to cheer up another girl, who
has lost her baby, and to remind her genially
that some women still have their children
though hers is gone. The first act, which
opens with the Isle of Man parishioners taking
tea and bun outside the village church, moves
pleasantly enough, and Alf. Woods performs well
his sad office as the religious prig who sheds moral
maxims all round to the expressed annoyance of
the feminine portion of the audience. When
later on, however, he takes to knocking people
down in order to help the good cause, his character
with the audience is retrieved. John Storm has
to show a considerable amount of muscular
Christianity in this version, and the manner in
which he knocks down Christians and pagans
alike is all that it should be. Later on, he is
knocked down himself, and married, not out in
the street as was the ridiculous custom in the
other version, but inside the drawing-room on a
chair, with the pagans he has denounced doing all
they can for him.
* * * *
The character does not show Alf. Woods
at his best. Miss Maud Williamson isn’t Glory
Quayle in appearance, and the red hair that
symbolised that erratic damsel’s revolt from the
humdrum is conspicuously absent. Nevertheless,
Miss Williamson, even with her staid black
locks, leaves the usual impression that Glory
sticks to John’s mission and the large texts,
and the melancholy coon song for one month after
John’s death, and then decides to marry Francis
Drake, who has meanwhile repented vaguely of
indistinct sins under John’s preaching, and goes
about looking like a large and conscience-stricken
ghost. The scene where John is asked to kiss
Glory and gives in after much persuasion, hangs
fire a little at the beginning, but grips well as it
goes on. Charles Woods makes a respectable
villain of the languid, gentlemanly sort, and the
remainder of the characters, with one or two ex-
ceptions, deserve no great reproach.
** * *
Frank Thornton, a crinkled little man of
mirth whom Sydney has badly missed for four
years, opens a season at the Palace on Saturday
with “Facing the Music.” As Thornton has
never brought anything dull to this country Syd-
ney will doubtless take the new show on trust,
and invest its two half-crowns with a light heart
and its shilling in a hopeful spirit. The bulk of
the present theatre-going generation remembers
Thornton best as the Private Secretary. He seems
to have dawned on most people in that charac-
ter, and though he has played many good
things since, the Secretary is a recollection
never to be effaced. Thornton came upon the
public as a humble, apologetic head and a
pair of goloshes looking round a door and asking
if he might come in (the above sentence is possibly
a trifle confused, but no matter), and the fact that
he did come right in is one upon which the city
has congratulated itself ever since. Theco. which
arrives with Thornton is new to the plague-
stricken city with the approaching water-famine.
The leading men struggle under the names of
Joseph Wilson and Alexander Bradley. Men
named Joseph and Alexander seldom prove quite
satisfactory, but possibly these two are an excep-
tion to the rule.
** * *
Williamson’s comic opera co. doesn’t stay
long in one place nowadays, and its visit to
Sydney Royal, which ends next week, is as tem-
porary as the rest of its doings. In the days when
Gilbert used to get up early in the morning and
write an opera occasionally things were different.
The co. which came along with a “Mikado” in
its bag could take a furnished house for six
months, with a feeling that it had come to stay.
Now its chief support consists of pleasant, bubble-
some trifles like the “Runaway Girl,” and how-
ever well they go for a little while, it isn’t
advisable to settle down and take root in the
community with such an unreliable means of sub-
sistence. So the co. will pursue the elusive copper
in new spheres of usefulness about the end of next
week.
** * *
J ust as The Bulletin was being printed
the World’s Entertainers were quitting Sydney
Palace Theatre, after the exhausting work of
putting on two four-hour programmes in one
day. American variety has won a high place in
Sydney’s regard and a warm welcome will be
given to the Entertainers when they come back
next year.
** * *
Sydney Lyceum is billed to remove the
large good brother off the stage this week and
produce another shock called “ In London Town.”
The convention that every stage city must be
London is a melancholy one. Why cannot the
heroine wail in a Liverpool snowstorm by way of
variety, or meet the kind-hearted dog-stealer or
baked ’tater man by a Manchester crossing?
Anyway, “In Sight of St. Paul’s,” is off this
week.
** * *
The bitterness of the inter-State net-ball
game is gradually eating into the soul of the audi-
ence at Sydney Tivoli. The net-ball game is
raging there and at Melbourne Opera House
simultaneously. Last week, the teams which are
supposed to represent Victoria scored best
at both houses, the N.S. W. team being
doubtless stiffened with public funds ab-
stracted at midnight from the Federal Treasury
by Deakin and Turner 1 . If this sort of thing goes
on, the Tivoli galleryites will march on Melbourne
and burn Fitzgibbon to the ground, and slay the
Yarra in cold blood, and lay waste the sewage-
farm with fire and sword and half-brick. Peggy
Pryde and some other cheerful people help to
distract public attention from the wrong done to
N.S.W. in the net-ball game. Blessed are the
peace-makers!

Mdlle. Antonia Dolores (Trebelli) will
resume melody at Sydney Town Hall for two
nights only—Tuesday and Thursday next—after
a three months tour of Maoriland, where she
floated in the wake of Amy Castles, and had over-
crowded houses. The Dolores smile made a tre-
mendous impression on one susceptible Maori
chief, and after rhapsodising in 15 dialects
of English and Maori, he rushed off in a state of
frenzy. Next morning, Mdlle. received a new
concert dress, a pin for her back-hair consisting of
a huge Maori god carved in greenstone, and
weighing somewhere about half-a-hundredweight;
and a huia feather. The above extraneous matter
is intended to imply that any remarks on the
beauty of Mdlle.’s singing are superfluous.

** * *
“The Thirty Thieves,” introduced to this
country for the first time at Melbourne Prin-
cess’s last Saturday, proved to be 13 gentle-
men in satin swallow-tails and knee-breeches,
headed by a baritone captain of commanding
stature whose fixings were even more “dossy”
than those of the gang. They wore black satin in
the first act, and red satin in the second act, and
there was no sense in them. The lady section of
the chorus also appeared to great advantage as
regards costumes. These ladies were described as
man servants and maid servants of the Lord
Mayor’s Household, the Lord Mayor being an
elderly exponent of an American accent, with no
other human interest attaching to him save a sub-
stantial young daughter (Miss May Beatty) and a
nimble secretary (Edward Lauri). Three other
people concerned in the well-dressed foolishness
were a wood-cutter, his son, and his hired gal, who
had found the hidden treasure of the Thieves,
Srevious to their idiotic burglary of the Lord
layor’s mansion. A £lO,OOO reward which the
Lord Mayor offered for the capture of the Thieves
on condition that he married the girl, or did not
marry her, was understood to be largely respon-
sible for whatever happened during the evening,
but the happenings lost all claim to intelligent
consideration when the triflesome character of
the show was fairly established. “The Thirty
Thieves ” seems a waste of splendour. The plot
is humorless in its efforts to be eccentric, the dia-
logue has not a spark of wit, the action is not
brisk. If the second act, as stated by the manage-
ment, is now brighter than its London original,
The Bulletin would like to know ex-
actly how dull the unimproved second act
could have been, and why a level - headed
manager like Musgrove thought it worth while to
import “Thirty Thieves” to these latitudes.
Williamson has never scored a success with any
musical show that was entirely lacking in literary
merit—and, indeed, some that possessed merit
were failures because the characters in the story
didn’t look sufficiently real. Musgrove will be
lucky if he gets a good five weeks out of this pro-
duction, notwithstanding its new company and
smart staging.
* * * *
The men are the most attractive items.
J. C. Piddock, the baritone captain of the Thieves,
is a little larger in all respects than Brownlow,
whom he resembles; also he shakes a looser,
lighter leg than our old friend, Henry of Navarre.
Edward Lauri, as the Mayor’s secretary, gets
along happily in a part which would crush a weak
comedian, and his versatile dance at the begin-
ning of the second act “knocks” the house.
Foster Courtenay, the woodcutter, shapes very
promisingly, W. R. Shirley appears to be just an
ordinary sort of new arrival, and the Mayor is
Charles Van Dyne, who supported Miss Stanton
in Sydney. There is not much to be said for
Van Dyne, except that he might have been a less
satisfactory struggler, considering the stuff he was
required to speak. The lady principals are only
two —Miss May Beatty, who is coupled with
Edward Lauri most of the time, and puts a fair
amount of vim into her frivol, and Miss Josephme
Stanton, hitherto a stranger to Melbourne.
Miss Stanton can sing nicely enough, though her
vocal gymnastics are not of a high order. She
has the further advantages of yellow hair, a plump
figure and a brilliant costume, but her American
way of speaking is much too casual for a comic
opera heroine. The music of the “ Thirty
Thieves ” usually suggests something one has
heard before, and these reminiscent melodies are
always pleasing, of course. The choruses, and
two or three of the other songs, notably the wood-
cutter’s ditty about the lost girl and her missing
eighteenpence, sound very bright amidst the
leaden grey dialogue, whilst the scenery and
dresses keep the eye contented all the time.
** * *
At Her Majesty’s the well-worn drama of
Napoleon and his Divorce, and the tableau that
befell him after he extinguished his Little Star of
Fortune came to pass again on Saturday. This
performance scarcely justifies the revival. It cer-
tainly doesn’t excuse the billing of Miss Waldorf’s
nameinlargertypethan otherpeople’s names. Miss
Waldorf was an uninteresting Camille on Thursday
and Friday, and now, as Josephine, she settles any
remaining doubt as to her want of force in specially
emotional characters. The least unconvincing
feature of H. H. Vincent’s work in the stagey
“ Boney ” part is the actor’s Napoleonic make-up.
Sundry other make-ups are effective,_ and the
company in general finds no difficulty in playing
up to the drama’s requirements when the scene-
painter doesn’t take all the trouble off their hands.
“A Royal Divorce” was relieved by a rather
audacious ballet on Saturday. One of the
girls who danced the quadrille in the Tuilleries
Garden was apparently out for an airing.
Every time she set to partners a broad ex-
E arise of skin-colored tights was displayed
y the slit in her loose gown, which garment
amounted to practically the same thing as a
night-gown when the lady lifted her right leg
heavenwards to give the boys a pale-pink treat.
“Much Ado About Nothing” is on the way
towards production at Her Majesty’s, but mean-
while “Twelfth Night” has earned a reputation
for being very much the best achievement of the
season thus far. Therefore, if the management is
wise it will revive “Twelfth Night” before
resorting to “Much Ado.” But managements
are rarely as wise as they think themselves.
»* * *
Buchanan’s “ Mariners of England,” now
afloat under the Anderson colors at Melb. Royal,
makes considerable concessions to common sense.
The breezy fable depicts nothing absolutely out-
side the pale of possibility. Its villains had sound
social and business motives for their crimes ; its
love-passages, enacted in sunshine and storm, are
brightened at the blackest by a ray of hope,
whereas in ordinary melodrama the lover and his
lass struggle and splutter, throughout in seas of
desolation, emerging only in the final scene, when
the hero invariably mentions that the clouds have
rolled away. All the same, “the Mariners” are
familiar enough with adversity to keep beholders
awake, and somehow the gallery on Saturday
night was more infuriated against the moderately
malicious villain than if he had been a fatal
chloroformer of the deepest Andersonian dye. In
Buchanan’s hands, the victims of vice, less apt
than some to tumble into trouble through their
own chronic idiocy, get more pity from beholders ;
hence, perhaps, the cat-calls of the gods.
* * * *
The plot meanders round the attempted
assassination of Nelson, who goes ashore to visit a
blind Admiral (Stirling Whyte), and is felled by
ruffians in the pay of Geo. Carey, a traitorous
naval person in pleasing clothes. R. Inman, the
Ralph Rackstraw of the period, gets wrongfully
acoused of the crime, and is court-martialled ;
but Inman’s innocence scores, as usual, for the
sake of Miss Duggan, and they live happily ever
afterwards on his revenues as the blind Admiral’s
long-lost son. Everyone in the piece who
doesn’t “ live happily ever afterwards ” dies
gloriously at the grand, spectacular battle of
Trafalgar, with the one exception of Carey.
Even Carey is becoming in the manner of his
decease, for he blows out his brains to save his
honor when his sin has found him out. Willard,
though got up to look shockingly old and decrepit
for a hero who died at 46, makes Nelson a satis-
factory blend of ancient ladies’-man, pietist, and
lion of the sea, and expires angelically in decollete
white underwear. The jingo Trafalgar tableau is
worth going to see “on its own,” and Willard’s
Nelson, though over etherealised, is better than
Barrett’s Christian Alfred, anyhow.
* * * *
Miss Duggan, the blind Admiral’s niece,
has quite a careless time compared with her
average stage lot. Her sailor-boy is tried for his
life, certainly; but, strange to say, no one murders
her blind uncle, assails her reputation, or casts
her to the mercies of homicidal maniacs. Ada
Guildford, deserted by the villain, dies of a
broken heart, a comparatively easy death. Bert
Bailey, as a petticoated fisherman, fools cheerfully
on dry-comedy lines ; while little Ethel Buckley,
as Poll of the Lobster Smack, suggests shrimps
rather than lobsters. Other features of the
“ Mariners’ ” pie are the picturesque Smugglers’
Cave, moonlight on the cliffs, fishing villagers
prancing on the shore, and a tuneful orchestra.

including Miss Nawn, and Mr. and Mrs. Sydney
Drew and Co.—who are respectively said to be
Irish comedians and sassiety sketch artists of the
greatest order. Also Peggy Pryde is due to sing
again on Saturday, and Peggy, like her aforesaid
partners in eminence, will appear at the Saturday
matinee.

Adelaide Royal, free for the first time for
two years from the rivalry of the Tivoli, has given
an enthusiastic welcome to the Broughs. Mrs.
8., as Lady Huntworth, has basted fowls and
gathered parsley for a week, and Leslie Victor has
scored heavily as the dissolute nobleman who
denies the right of any parson, being unmarried,
to talk about Hades as if he knew the country.
Ward and Brough are as sound and reliable as
ever. At present the uniforms of “The Second
in Command ” pervade the stage and thrill the
female heart, which has of late had to put up with
mere kharki.
The Wild Cat Column.
Just a few days after N.S.W. Treasurer
Waddell had assured the public that there was no inten-
tion of floating a loan at present, a loan of £3,000,000 was
dumped on the London market. (Treasurer Waddell
has since explained at great length that he was as
innocent as a fresh-laid pumpkin of any intention to
deceive, and that the discrepancy is not real, only
apparent). It is a 3 per cent, loan, and has apparently
realised about £92 10s. Therefore, between this and the
£4,000,000 loan of last September, N.S.W. has incurred
£7,000,000 of debt and got about £6,350,000 for it. That
is to say, its credit is good enough to enable it to borrow
at a little below 3£ per cent., but it borrows at what is
practically £3 6s. 2d. per cent, and adds something like
£650,000 of the interest to the permanent debt! The very
least that a Government with a fraction of honesty in its
composition could do is to establish a sinking fund
sufficient to cover the amount of the discount on the
loan—the amount which is really interest added to the
principal of the debt. As there is very little honesty about
Australian Governments or Parliaments, however, that is
too much to expect. Parliaments are mostly in the help-
less condition of James Ashton, M.L.A., who explained
recently that he had entered the Assembly with ideals,
but had speedily dropped them, realising that reform
must come from without. Which is another version of
the lazy and useless theory that the Government isn’t
expected to do right unless Parliament forces it to do so,
and Parliament isn’t expected to do anything unless the
people force it by violent and frantic agitation.
This loan makes a net addition of over £7,000,000
to the N.S.W. public debt inside 12 months. It raises
that debt, if all liabilities are reckoned, to about
£78,000,000— something over £57 per inhabitant. The
private wealth of N.S.W. is estimated at some £50,000,000
less than it was in 1890, and the debt is about £34,000,000
more than it was in 1890. Therefore, the boshy narrative
about the loans being wisely applied to develop the
resources of the country had better be dropped. The
question that is always waiting to be answered is—how
long can this process go on without something breaking?
The debt grows faster than the population. In 28 years,
N.S.W. has owed more per inhabitant every year than it
did the year before, and the other States have done the
same. Every year, too, it has more unproductive debt
per inhabitant. And, as the present loan has been spent
in advance, and more—it isn’t sufficient by fully £500,000
to repay the money borrowed from trust-funds to keep
the loan account going—it leaves N.S.W. with no money
at all to go on with, except by borrowing the trust-funds
again. The net railway revenue is falling off —for the
first nine months of this financial year it is £79,000 less
than for the first three-quarters of last year. And it is
announced that, despite all the extra revenue obtained
under the Commonwealth tariff, the year is likely to end
with a deficit of £150,000 or £200,000. This, it is ex-
plained, is owing to the drought—but, then, the deficit is
always owing to something. It will make the 16th deficit
in 23 years. What does N.S.W. think of the situation?
* * * »
One source of wealth which has dropped out
almost completely, and almost unnoticed, is that which
is derived from immigration. For many years, it was
reckoned that about £1,000,000 was brought into Aus-
tralia every year by immigrants—not the assisted variety,
nor Kanakas, niggers, or destitute Italian laborers, but
comparatively affluent settlers, mostly attracted by the
tail-end of the gold legend. N.S.W. gained a large share
of this, but now N.S.W., Victoria, and S.A. are all losing
by emigration, and money tends to go out instead of
coming in.
« * * *
During the last 10 years up to 1900 the N.S.W.
Government borrowed about £20,000,000 to “ develop
the resources of the country.” And this process of
development by getting further into debt panned out so
badly that whereas in 1890 the total private wealth of
N.S.W. was estimated at £410,500,000 (£368 per inhabi-
tant), in 1900 it was estimated by the same authority
(State Statistician Ooghlan) at £356,350,000 (£261 per
inhabitant). The people who died in N.S.W. during the
year ended 30th June, 1900, left less property behind
them than the people who died in any other year since
1891. And with this decrease of wealth the people had
to pay close on £500,000 a year more in 1900 than in 1890
for interest on loans. Where does the Government think
this kind of “ development ” by loans is going to lead to ?
The good old drought explanation doesn’t anything like
cover the downfall, for the value of live stock only
diminished by about £5,000,000 (from £34,000,000 to
£29,000,000), and the total downfall represents £54,000,009

Vanning Tables.—The very newest and what
gives exceedingly fair promise of superseding all other
tables is the machine known as the Wesley-Baird Vanning
Table. The table is of South Australian invention, and
has given excellent results on Broken Hill ores and ores
containing pyrites, carbonate of copper and tin. Like all
tables, the best work is done on “ fines,” and more espe-
cially when the product is properly classified. The
Central Mining and Tramway Co., of 424 Little Collins-
street, Melbourne, and 40 Hunter-street, Sydney, are the
sole agents in the Eastern States. The table has a capa-
city up to 30 tons per day.*

The Boulder mines have, on an average, 50 per
cent, of the shafts and pans of their Griffin mills broken
monthly by unexploded dynamite finding its way into
the mills. The wear and tear and loss of time for repairs
are enormous and must increase treatment-cost several
shillings a ton. If the new patent explosive compound
(Rendrock) were used this expense would be avoided
because even if this explosive got into the machines it
would not explode. Rendrock gives off no smoke or
fumes. Particulars will be furnished by the Sole
Proprietor, N. Guthridge "Ltd., Sydney, Melbourne,
Fremantle, Wellington.*

The Commercial World, August, 1901, writes
re The Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society, Limited :
“ It is primarily a progressive institution, its contracts
are liberal, and, moreover, its financial position is every-
thing which could be desired.”*
The Wilfley Concentrator, which was patented
and introduced to Australia some three years ago, has
met with most phenominal success, and is now to be
found in almost every up-to-date mill in all the States,
particularly Kalgoorlie and Broken Hill, where every
mine of importance is using them. Similar success has
been met with in every mining centre of the world, some
5000 of the tables having been sold. Its success has led
to numerous imitations and infringements of the Wilfley
patent. Lately the Company obtained an injunction and
damages against the Cammett machine and users thereof,
their patents being confirmed by the American supreme
Court, and other of the imitations have since been taken
off the market. The Syndicate owning the patents in
Australia has decided to prosecute all infringements, and
action has now been taken in this direction in Victoria,
users being equally liable with vendors of infringments.
They should exercise caution in dealing only with the
sole manufacturers, the Austral Otis Engineering
Company, South Melbourne, or their authorised agents. *

* * • *
Sydney Municipal Council, which put up its
rate for one year to 2s. in the £, reduces it again to
Is. lOd. It is reckoned that at this figure there will be a
deficit for the current year of £777, which is not very
much, but still it marks the beginning of a return to the
old, wretched practice of deliberately incurring deficits
for the sake of cutting down taxation. The estimated
revenue is £256,027 and expenditure £256,804. Instead
of allowing even a small shortage to arise, Sydney and all
suburban municipalities should just now be making an
extra special effort to wipe off their old debts. If a
Greater Sydney Council is established, as now seems pos-
sible, with large powers to effect improvements, it will
want more money, in order to start the work of making
Greater Sydney a clean, modern, up-to-date capital. Then
the city’s great unproductive debt made up through the
accumulation of bygone deficits will be found grievously
in the way.

» * * *
The recent fall in the price of even the best
securities seems to suggest that, despite the See Govern-
ment’s lavish foreign borrowing, a scarcity of money is
setting in. N.S.W. State securities have dropped. So
have such stocks as the Bank of Australasia, Commercial
Banking Co. of Sydney, National Bank, Bank of N.S.W.,
Union Bank, Australian Mutual Fire Insurance, Mercan-
tile Mutual Insurance, United Insurance, Tooth’s Brewery,
and other gilt-edged scrip. Bank of New South Wales
had no sellers in Sydney on April 7 below £45 17s. 6d.
On May 21 they were offering at £4l. Between the same
two dates the drop in Commercial Banking Co. of Sydney
shares (sellers’ quotation) was from £5l 12s. 6d. to
£2B 17s. 6d. There was a slight recovery after
the 21st, but the drop is still very obvious.
The N.S.W. Government is still selling stock daily
at the Treasury, but even at 3J per cent, it only
raised £108,233 during April. This ought to be.abundant,
but £1,300,000 a year of loan money to spend doesn’t suit
the principles and ideas of the garish O’Sullivan. No 3
percent loans seem possible in N.S.W. just now. The
great foreign borrowing policy has only resulted, as
usual, in a huge increase of imports—an increase that is
out of all proportion to the growth of exports. Here are
some significant figures from that valuable work,
Coghlan’s “ Wealth and Progress of N.S.W.” :
Surplus
available to

per annum. per annum. on foreign

1900-1901 27,244,645 27,757,820 513,175
Coghlan estimates that N.S.W. has to pay £4,655,000 a
year as interest on its foreign debts, public and private,
which means that its exports should exceed its imports
by that amount. Therefore the steady process of going
further into debt is painfully visible. Huge foreign loans
only tend to increase imports, and the more we import
the less show is there for employment for the local pro-
ducer, so the curse works both ways at once.
¥ * * *
The Foreign-trade person works in his little
advertisement everywhere. The Insurance and Banking
Record remarks of the Cascade Brewery Co. (Hobart)
that “ the brewery trade, since a Freetrade tariff has
been adopted, has increased enormously, and 6 per cent,
dividends are now assured.” And the Federal tariff has
been taxing imported beer in bulk Is. per gallon, and in
bottle Is. 6d. per gallon, while local beer brewed from
barley, tnalt or hops pays 2d., and other kinds 3d.—
protection to the local article 9d. to Is. 4d. per gallon.
As against his English or American competitor the
Federal tariff gives the Tasmanian brewer a lot more
Protection than he used to have under his State tariff,
the difference between the old Tasmanian customs and
the old Tasmanian excise being only 6d. per gallon on
bulk and Is. per gallon on bottled. And with this extra
Protection, which the Record calls a Freetrade tariff,
things have begun to look up in Tasmania.
« * » »
The abridged prospectus of the Canadian Real
Ice Skating Co. (Melbourne) is a humorous thing. It is
proposed to construct a skating rink with a real ice floor,
and also to sell ice to the public, or, in the prospectus’s
own words : “ The company is to be formed for the pur-
pose of purchasing from the vendor and promoter, for the
consideration hereafter mentioned, all options, rights,
contracts, or arrangements obtained or made, or to be
obtained or made, by him in connection with the acquisi-
tion of a site for a skating rink, the erection of a skating
rink with an ice floor, and for the sale of ice, and cold
storage, if necessary.” The promoter (the prospectus, as
published in Australasian of 17/5/’o*2, doesn’t state who
he is, but one Maurice Goldburgh, Esq., appears to be
indicated) doesn’t seem to put in anything except the
idea and the option of buying or leasing a piece of ground
in Flinders-st., Melbourne, on which to build the real ice
rink. That isn’t much ; writer could get similar options
over nearly half Melbourne or Sydney if he wanted
them. The “ consideration hereafter mentioned” isn’t
mentioned at all in the advertisement above quoted,
so what the promoter is to get for his option
remains a mystery. The value of an option de-
pends largely on the price or rent to be paid for
the property optioned, or opted, or whatever the word
should be; some options are valuable and some
can hardly be given away. The prospectus alludes to
the alleged immense takings at some American real ice
rinks, which casts very little light on probable takings in
Australia. It also alludes to the large profits to be made
out of the sale of ice, which, as the Melbourne Fresh
Food and Frozen Storage Co. proposes to get rid of its
business at a huge loss, partly through inability to make
money out of ice, is less reassuring than it might be. It
is promised that the rink will be cool in summer (ice
generally is) but “ in the corresponding depth of winter
the necessary comforts and warmth will be maintained,”
which is a rather remarkable statement. What will
become of the ice if “ warmth is maintained ? ” It is also
stated, as if it was a powerful argument in favor of the
speculation, that the site over which the promoter has
the option of lease or purchase has been valued at £lOO to
£llO per foot, which only seems to suggest that the co.
will have to pay an awful amount of money for its
premises. And the co. asks for £50,000 capital!! There
are many interesting points about this ice business.

that. It depends on whether you are going to be a
sleeping or active partner, and if latter, on the value of
your services, also on how long business has been estab-
lished, how long it has been returning a good profit,
whether profit is increasing or diminishing, and various
other things—including character of proposed partner.

* • • *
The bald announcement in Melbourne last week
that a well-known firm, name withheld, was about to be
prosecuted for alleged income-tax evasion, seems to have
created great unrest in many reputable business quarters.
The taxation officers were besieged all day by furtive
scouts of commercial houses, uneasily seeking to discover
the firm implicated, and had it not been that the name
was disclosed that afternoon, some highly respected
business men would have passed very sleepless nights.
As it is, the income-tax people have directed their
suspicions upon more than one esteemed person whose
word has hitherto passed as law.
» » *
Chief-Justice Griffith decided for the plaintiffs
in the big cyanide-patent case. According to His Honor’s
interpolations, it looked that way from the start. £5OOO
costs were asked for and £1250 given ; but the legal light
was a very expensive flame, and even if the case stops at
this point Gent One, &c., will collect two or three
thousand.
* • • •
Mr. W. J. Hogan, of Cobar, writes with refer-
ence to a paragraph from “Cobarite” (8., 24/5/’O2), to
state that it is not correct, as represented, that the In-
spector of Mines for the district has called upon the
Occidental 00. to brace its open cut with timber, and
that the charges assumed tc have been made against the
Inspector (who, he remarks, is an experienced mine-
manager) are altogether baseless. Mr. Hogan also in-
forms us that the allegations with respect to the Great
Cobar mine (that the Inspector has refused to allow the
pillar-system and favors the disused workings being filled
with slag) are false, and that the comments in the para-
graph, as a whole, are quite unwarranted. The Bulletin
regrets that it has been misinformed. It had no
desire to cast any reflection on the officer referred to,
and it did not, as Mr. Hogan asserts, allege that he had no
practical mining experience ; this denial of the statements
made is willingly published. With reference to the Occi-
dental, it must be stated that, for some time past, it has
been common talk on Sydney Stock Exchange and in
mining circles, that the slump in the value of the shares
was solely attributable to orders which had been given
by the inspector with respect to timbering. As Mr.
Hogan is chairman of this co., it is satisfactory to have
from him a contradiction of the report, which has
evidently been put in circulation by “bears.”
• • • •
B.H. Block 10 has made a profit of £5386 for
the half-year—not very much for a co. that made
£970,000 in the previous nine years, yet a good deal better
than the loss of £129 in the first half of last year. But
even the £5386 is not all mine profit ; that figure has been
reached by tacking on £2114 over-charged income-tax for
1900 and 1901, £4lO interest on reserve funds, £336 un-
claimed divs., and other small amounts. The actual
profit on working was £2519, allowing nothing for depre-
ciation. Allowing a fair thing for that, the profit was a
bit less than nothing. So far as shareholders are con-
cerned their one consolation may be that a good deal less
ore has been taken out of the mine than in any recent
period. The history of the last four half-years can he
fairly well shown like this :

There are several satisfactory items in this. The most so
is the increase in the extraction of the metals ; though
still nearly three-fifths of the silver and a quarter of
the lead go over the dump. Next is a reduction of
costs, especially as it apparently has not been accom-
panied by a reduction of wages, poor enough for such
work in such a place. Miners on contract averaged
10s. 6fd. per shift; all miners, 10s. 2d. Costs, however,
are still very high. The South mines and mills for
14s. 9d. ; and 16s. is reckoned a fair thing elsewhere.
There is much room for labor-saving on surface by the
rearrangement of plant. As to outlook, it doesn’t dazzle
the eye. The ore-faces are, on the whole, better than
they were a few months back, but the shafts are taking a
lot of patching up to keep them at all workable, and day
and night, Sunday and every other day, the co. pushes
down its new main shaft. In ore-treatment, too, nothing
except of a negative character has happened to the co.
lately. The Odling magnetic processes are taking a rest
till Seddon, or somebody, puts up the price of lead and
zinc. “ Captain ” Warren, who has helped the mine to
pay £920,000 in divs. and bonuses (£9 4s. a share) in 10
years, leaves it this month. Stanley Low, a young Vic.
product, takes on the stiff contract of succeeding him.

A gentleman from way back in N.S.W. is the
proud owner of a gold-mine, reef 2ft. 6in. thick, ore said
to be worth from 2 to 6oz. per ton ; shaft down 145 ft. ;
and although it will be necessary to drive a crosscut 20ft.
ere the reef is intersected and the quality proved, he is
now in Sydney trying to sell, not the mine, but 20 tons
of ore at £3 per ton spot cash, goods to be delivered when
the reef is reached 1
* * * *
Day Dawn, Braidwood, N.S.W. : Floated a
few months ago by Austin Chapman (Federal Government
whip). Local reports state mine fabulously rich —12oz.
to the ton. If it goes even 12dwt. it ought to be a good
thing. Battery now being erected. Development work
allegedly being done to a great extent by Govt., which is
said to be contributing £2 16s. per ft. towards cost of
sinking shaft. A few pertinent questions put to the
Mines Minister in Parliament ought to elicit some in-
teresting information—particularly so to struggling mine
owners, who, not knowing the run of departmental ropes,
are refused a share of the prospecting vote.
* * * *
Mt. Lyell half-yearly report shows that on
working account there was a credit balance of £87,660,
but after deducting £19,000 for depreciation and other
items, a net profit of £53,000 is shown. The amount paid
in divs. out of the half-year’s earnings was £75,625, so
that the writing-off of £19,000 for depreciation is a mere
book-keeping entry, which means taking it out of one
pocket and putting it into another. The report gives no
information as to the profits made from smelting ore for
other co’s. It is stated that 32,411 tons of public ore were
treated, but beyond this there is studied evasion, no data
whatever being given on which to make any calculation.
But assuming that the Mt. Lyell charges were modera-
tion itself as compared with other co’s.’ tariffs, there
would have to be gross mismanagement somewhere if
this work did not return a net profit almost equal
to the whole amount credited to working account after
making the deductions indicated. It is quite certain that
the ordinary operations of the co. on its own ores were
carried on at a very small margin of profit, and now that
the outside source of income is at an end through the
termination of the N. Lyell contract, it cannot be said
that the prospects of Mt. Lyell are extra rosy. _ Although
copper has shown an inclination to rise within the past
fortnight, the present price is 30s. per ton lower than the
average price ruling during the half-year under review.
The future of the mine itself is purely a matter of con-
jecture. Mr. Sticht is without question the ablest ad-
ministrator who has had charge of any Australian mining
enterprise. He is noted for being as cautious as he is
able, but, allowing for this, can anyone read this extract
from his general report without coming to the
conclusion that he entertains some doubts as to
what will happen, as it must be always borne
in mind that the success or failure of the co. will
depend on developments in the deep levels ? “ While the
open-cut ore smelter per day for the half-year has aver-
aged 712 tons, the underground ore has averaged 35.4
tons daily— the small quantity of the latter being due to
the erratic nature of the enrichments, together with the
depleted condition of the stopes. At present there is
still a certain amount of ore left standing in the stopes at
No. 7 level and No. 5 level north drive, but these are old
workings and probably nearing early exhaustion. Momen-
tarily the downward continuations of the No. 7 level stopes
towards No. 8 level are the only known reserves of rich
ore of any promise, but there are no data to surmise the
extent from. There is general geological evidence in
favor of the occurrence of similar enrichments at No. 8
level and below. Exploration work is not yet, however,
sufficiently far advanced to afford grounds for the assump-
tion of the certain presence of the same.”
Up to the end of April last Mt. Lyell had pro-
duced 39,698 tons of copper, 3,268,0160 z. silver and
125,4980 z. gold, the approximate value of which would be
£3,500,000. The amount distributed in dividends was
£869,386, or about 25 per cent, of the product. The sub-
scribed capital of the co. was £825,000, added to which
there was £191,000 premium on shares sold, making the
total capital practically over £1,000,000. Assuming that
divs. are continued at the same rate as for the last half-
year, it will take the co. another year to return the
original capital.
* • * *
Western, Zeehan : History of this co. shows
necessity for making it compulsory for mining co.’s to
establish reserve funds. Out of product valued at
£428,000 co. paid £102,000 in divs. Development work
became necessary ; not a penny to credit; calls made and
paid on only 17-60ths of shares ; overdraft, £2500 ; mine,
which has fair prospects, closed down. Had 5 per cent,
of the profits, as earned, been set aside for progressive
work shareholders would not have noticed it, and work
would now be proceeding with almost a certainty that, in
a short time, the producing and profit-earning stage
would again be reached.
* » * *
New Churn Railway (Bendigo) : Work at this
mine should command attention of mining men all the
world over, “ Most encouraging indications ” met with
3700 ft. from surface. No doubt about reef-channel
having been intersected, and a few weeks will determine
whether or not there is pay ore. This is the greatest
depth at which prospecting for gold is being carried on
anywhere. Victoria Quartz, on the same line, driving at
nearly the same depth ; but should they find a forma-
tion there it would, stratigraphically, be fully 1000 ft.
above that which N.O.R. has met with, as, in addition to
V.Q. being at a higher altitude than N.0.R., the ore-
bodies pitch from the latter northerly towards the former.
Discovery of pay-ore in N.O.R. will cause a big revival at
Bendigo, and will probably induce Geo. Lansell to deepen
his 180 shaft to 5000 ft.—a work which he has frequently
expressed a desire to accomplish. The long-standing
drainage trouble having been amicably arranged, there is
now no obstacle to block the ambitious enterprise.

Tartana (N.Q.): “Now reluctantly admitted
in high quarters to be the biggest, withal richest, copper-
ore deposit known in Australasia. The tangle is out and
the inevitable is happening—the mine is talking. Enor-
mously wealthy men handled relatively poor mines in
Chillagoe and Mt. Garnet. There is only a change of
words to illustrate the Tartana proposition—a poor man
is handling a relatively enormously wealthy group of
mines.” Thus writes the sec., and he ought to know.

SOCIETY
Loch (Yic.) was the first Governor who
demoralised Australians by a too lavish expendi-
ture at Govt. House. It was his first important
appointment, and he was anxious to make a
success of it. When Hopetoun followed, feeling
that he had a record to break, he spent £22,000 a
year on the guzzlers and gorgers who infested his
halls, giving them frozen fish and pheasant from
’Ome, as well as the best brands of champagne.
That was a long jump from old-time Gov.
Hotham’s historic sandwiches and she-oak, but
the sassiety loafers only looked upon it as a right.
Poor Hopetoun pathetically refers in his cable to
the Secretary of State to the “extraordinary re-
sponsiveness of the people throughout Australia ”
when he landed as Governor-General. The “re-
sponsiveness ” was mostly a lively sense of favors
to come. The frantic cheerers did not expect him
to ask for an increase of salary ; they were stimu-
lated by the prospect of additional outlay from
his private purse. There will be sorrow in some
quarters now that the goose with the golden eggs
has been slain. Anyway, a salary of £lO,OOO a
year is ample for a Governor-General who does
not consider it a part of his duty to gorge the
swell loafers of the dominion over which he
presides.
* * * *
Lord Hopetoun’s resignation will hasten
the end of the present farcical State-Governor
system. At South Melbourne, last week, the
local councillors were bewailing the Hopetoun
affair in loud ungrammatical language, when a
comparatively thoughtful member of the crowd
suggested that “a re-adjustment of the Gov.-
General’s allowances could have been achieved by
a retrenchment of the minor offices of State
Governors.” This was at least better than a plea
for the Bill which Parliament rejected, though
the speaker didn’t try to show reason why the
major office of G.-G. may not be kept up
very decently on a definite £lO,OOO a year.
The most desirable way of enhancing the
value (in “dignity”) of the chief vice-regal
position is the economical way. Let the
State Governor be a local man, performing the
simple political duties of his position for the same
A CHANCE YET.
HED P A: “ And so you sold that for thirty guineas! My friend, if you will abandon Art and
join our firm as a salesman it will do much to mitigate my hitherto rooted objection to
you as a son-in-law.”
salary that a Speaker receives. The Chief-Justice,
with £IOOO or £ISOO a year added to his plump
stipend, would be a suitable Governor, provided
he were forbidden to cultivate a large circle
of friends and make himself less exclusive than
every other judge ought to be. Then the Gov.-
General at £IO,OOO a year would loom larger than
he now appears in comparison with minor Gover-
nors. He’d have no rivals near his throne.
Better to be the only imported social star than the
brightest star among seven. All actors will agree
with that doctrine. And what is a Hopetoun but
an actor ?
» • * *
To celebrate the Coronation—feed the un-
employed. Heaven knows they will be plentiful
all over Australia this bitter winter. With the
drought murdering the pastoral and agricultural
industries, with big mines closing down, and with
a financial crisis threatening the various Govern-
ments and limiting the expenditure of money on
State works, there will be, indeed,a pitiful crowd
of them. Let, then, what money is to be spent go
in food, not gas and fireworks.
• * * •
Sydney University Union has done much
to retrieve the reputation of the students of the
institution by carrying (by a large majority and
amidst much cheering) a resolution protesting
against the agitation of the stupid, Tory, Mary
Jane papers, such as S.M. Herald and Sunday
Times, for the punishment of Professor Wood
on account of his anti-Gore views. This agita-
tion the Union condemns as “a serious
menace to liberty of speech.” Mr. K. Swan-
wick, in moving a resolution on the subject,
put the position in a nutshell when he pointed out
that there was no agitation against Professors
MacCallum and David for publicly expressing
their pro-Gore views, nor against Professor Pitt-
Cobbett for having taken an active part in the
Federal controversy. The resolution arrived at,
and the free acknowledgment of Professor
Wood’s patriotism, does honor to the University
Union; its members may not be able to see the
rights and wrongs of the war clearly, but
they have, at any rate, the instincts of free men.
* * * *
The cohentingenb craze begins to provide
a luxuriant crop of divorce proceedings. A typi-
cal case came before a court the other day.
One hero, having led an ordinarily peaceful and
domestic life with his spouse and children, was
suddenly smitten with the desire to go away and
help in the great cause of Beit and Cohen, and fell
away from grace under the seductive influence
of much delirious cuddling by miscellaneous
maidens. And, when he got back, covered with
glory and a great reputation as an expert farm-
burner, the hero, probably as the easiest way of
getting rid of wife and kids, confessed that he
wasn’t fit to associate with them because he had
lived the same kind of life as eight-tenths of his
fellowcohentingentershavebeen living in S’ Africa.
He had committed adultery there many times, and
“a kaffir woman was good enough for him,” and
likewise he had acquired drinking and gambling
habits, and committed other acts which he could
not mention to his wife. In virtue of these and
other merits of the same kind, the boss warriors
gave the gentleman an Imperial commission ; so
he is an officer and gentleman, even if, on his own
showing, he isn’t fit to be the husband of a decent
woman.
* * 0 *
Poetry and prose. Melb. Leader, in Feb.,
1901, printed a pathetic snapshot, entitled, “The
Soldier’s Farewell,” in which a departing cohen-
tingenter was shown kissing his sweetheart, just
before the sailing of the boat. The interesting
picture turned up in the District Court, t’other
day, as evidence in support of the claim for main-
tenance put in by the girl on behalf of the infant
which subsequently transpired. A letter from
the soldier “at the front” was also put in, in
which the writer said : “I hope the kid is like
me. ... If the war lasts long enough, I’ll make
him a soldier of the King.” The “ kid ” may see
service yet.

Brisbane Public Library was opened by
the Society gang, the other day. The Library is
evidently intended for Naice people, as it is to be
kept open only from 9 to 4 daily (while their un-
washed brethren are at work humping coal, &c.),
and closed during the hours that Dan, Jim, and
Mick are off the chain.
The proposal to build a consumptives’
hospital near Hornsby, Sydney, arouses bitter
opposition from residents of the district, many of
whom are themselves weak-chested. The Hornsby
hills, 600 to 700 ft. high, are excellent abiding-
places for phthisics, but the man who has gone
there with “ only a patch,” or “just a little weak-
ness,” doesn’t want to see 1000 or so desperate
cases quartered near by to prejudice his chances
of life. It is a selfish but entirely natural
feeling. Even the Picton (N.S.W.) people
are now protesting against the Thirlmere
Consumptives’ Home near by; and it is plain
that, to avoid discontent, the refuge for the
phthisical will have to be far removed from every
centre of population. There should be no diffi-
culty in securing a big, healthy area 10 miles from
everywhere; and a large community of consump-
tives, under proper management, could amuse
itself without requiring much intercourse with the
outer world. Medical men are endeavoring to
allay the fears of the Hornsby people by stating
that there would be “absolutely no danger ”from
the hospital. Yet one doctor, at the deputation
to Treasurer Waddell asking for the site, said that
two of the medical staff at Prince Alfred Hospital
had died from consumption, probably contracted
through the admission of phthisis cases to the
institute ! If the doctor, with all his precautions
and knowledge, is thus attacked, there is good
reason for public alarm. On the whole, N.S.W.
Gov t. had better place the consumptives’ hospital
on some isolated site, if only for the valuable edu-
cational effect such a precaution will have in
teaching the general public to beware of tuber-
culous infection.
The latest feature in Sydney suburbs is
the lady bottle-’o.

It has been discovered that one-tenth of
the alleged property-owners who have
applied for compensation over the
Sydney land-resumptions have no
documentary title to the properties
they are supposed to own—ares imply
in possession through the death or
disappearance of the real owners.
In most cases these real owners
dropped out very long ago. It would
be interesting to find out what they
died of, or if the bodies were ever
found. Ten per cent of the titles is
a huge proportion to be based on the
dropping-out of former possessors—
especially in a suburb so conveni-
ently adjacent to the harbor.
.Possibly, when the Works
Department starts level-
ling the ground and
digging new founda-
tions it will find the
bones of some of these
lost proprietors, and
some may be dredged
up from the ooze of
the harbor when the
old wharves are shifted
and the mud of ages
disturbed underneath
them. c
* m
Years ago, when
the Mormons were
sending mission-
aries out here, a
i northern N.S.W.
resident got rid of
• a troublesome wife
rather neatly. Pro-
fessing conversion
to the Mormon
doctrines, he sold out
his possessions, and
with wife aforesaid, went
on board the steamer with
the other converts. Just as
the moorings were being cast off,
however, hubby, with all available
cash, rushed ashore on plea of having
forgotten something, and disappeared in the
crowd on the wharf. He didn’t come back, and
the lady went off in the steamer.
• * * *
Justice Williams must have established a
record in Melbourne last week when he put
dissatisfied couples through the divorce mill at
the rate of ten a day for several days. Most of
the cases were of the penny plain variety, but a few
were colored. One gentleman, who had “gone out
West,” and had been a trifle dilatory in sending
his wife the gold he didn’t get, finally broke off
relations in a letter informing the lady that—“l
feel I cannot care for you as I used to do. When
you would not write I wrote to a young lady in
Melbourne, and sent her money to come over to
me. She will live with me as my wife. She is a
respectable, well-connected girl. My mother has
seen her. To the best of my belief, she is abso-
lutely virtuous ; so you see she is not a bad
character.” Another respondent declined to
continue being a loving husband, just on
the spur of the moment as it were, but, to
fill the void thus created, thoughtfully brought
his wife some sea-gulls, dogs and a cat, and
desired her to waste her young affections on
them. Finally, he left her because she wore
a black dress. Another husband first roused
his wife’s suspicions by boring holes in the ser-
vant’s bedroom door, so as to watch her undressing.
He must have been a member of the Gimlet
Brigade. An extra special sample, found kissing
a lady friend, candidly owned up and requested
his wife to allow the other lady to come and live
with them. Also a really interesting person who
had made £BO,OOO on the Westralian goldfields,
took three several business trips to England, each
time bringing back a fresh “ companion,” who
had consoled him in his wife’s absence. It is not
surprising to learn that a few years later he was
a “broker,” and actually looked up his wife, who
was by then in “service,” to borrow money from
her. But all these giddy males were balanced by
the long-suffering person whose wife contracted a
confirmed habit of running after strange gods. He
possessed an infinity of patience, but when his
wife had run away 18 times with a large number
of different men, he said forgiveness had its limits,
and closed the door for good. Truly, they are a
quaint lot, these divorcees.

Despite the order issued by Maoriland’s

tunic, &c. —were to attend a recent Govt. House

by the G.0.C., and now the highly-exclusive Ist
Battalion of Auckland Infantry, which hasn’t any
gory togs, is clamouring for the blood-dyed sesame
to the sacred portals of the vice-regal ’Ome.

W.C.T.U. people have taken up a “drink
cure.” Same old apo-morphia which you can
“ put in his tea ” when “ he” is misguided enough
to venture on tea. The cure, of course, will no
more cure a drunkard of drinking than a dog of
eating, and its free use would be likely to cause
more domestic unhappiness than even drunken-
ness.
WOMAN AND MAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I, that ache to he kissed,
And yearn for your strongest embrace,
You, who go where you list,
And leave neither shadow nor trace.
Woman! watching the years
Kaleidoscope vapid and slow;
Man ! with no time for tears,
Have you no heart for woe ?
ECHO.

AND Troubles
Practically, Dr. Neild’s evidence acquitted
Claude Lomax Stowers of the alleged murder of
i. his baby, That entity was proved to have been
just four, months old, whereas the doctor swore
positively that the dead infant—whose body was
found encased in some of baby Stowers’ clothing—
could not have been that age, and was more prob-
ably only about two months old. Meanwhile the
dead baby lies in a nameless grave, the Stowers’
baby has disappeared, the State has spent a large
sum of money over the three trials; and the whole
trouble would never have happened if a Foundling
Hospital for the receipt of all undesired babies
“andHo questions asked” had been available
in Melbourne. The cost of those three trials
would have reared a dozen foundlings.
• * * *
Unconsciously humorous were the York
business-advisers in turning the Ophir library into
cash. The directories of the various towns visited
are, for some unknown reason, stated to be “the
most interesting feature of the collection ” —which
they really might be if they contain full and candid
marginal notes by the visitors. Thus: Smith,
“Mayor, wants title; avoid carefully.” Jones,
“Coach-driver, send for, remind of former visit.”
Brown, “Squatter, shooting good, whisky very
good.” But it seems too much to hope for. And
among the great authors represented are “Kipling,
Rossetti, Tolstoi, and the Duchess of Leeds ! ”
* * * *
In the wild hurly-burly of the Sydney
artistic row, one fact seems to stand out plainly :
That the students who hold aloof from both art
societies, and demand a State school of art, with a
thoroughly competent instructor, from Europe or
elsewhere, are on the right track. If N.S.W. is
to spend any taxpayers’ money on art, let it be
spent in securing a strong, sane teacher-painter
full of sound knowledge. Then these young or
old gentlemen who are afflicted or blessed with
the insanity of genius can get up, if they please,
their own private art asylums, and pay for their
own cat-fights.
* * » •
The proposal made by a committee of the
wise men of Auckland (M.L.) to roof-in with
canvas a whole block of Queen-street for the pur-
pose of forming a dining-room to accommodate
10,000 school children at a monster bun-worry on
Coronation Day was voted pure lunacy by the
city dominies in public meeting assembled. With-
out the aid of the teachers, the advocates of the
scheme saw that it was impracticable, and it had
to be dropped. Probably Wirth’s Circus will
benefit by the change of programme.
, * * * *
M'r. Justice Stephen (N.S.W.) giving judg-
ment in a very bad Gent. One case in Sydney last
week:
I cannot understand why it is that a person found
acting in this way is not prosecuted.
But isn’t it the judge’s own fault? If in Bank-
ruptcy and other jurisdictions judges may send
suspected thieves and perjurers for trial, why not
send embezzlers when the criminal act is so clear ?
If the case is plain enough for the judge to de-
prive the man of his living, it is surely plain
enough for him to send him on to a jury lor trial.
It is liot flittering to judicial intelligence that
that is not perceived.

HIS WORSHIP: “I have
been 30 years silting on this Bench, and”.
THE DEFENDANT: “ It’s just about time yer give
it a rest.”
There, has been a great to-do in the prim
establishment of two well-known maiden ladies in
an Australian city. The wife of a friend paying
a duty-call was taken unexpectedly ill, and the
hostesses were thrown into consternation at the
revelation that a little stranger was on the point
of arrival in their drawing-room. Doctors, nurses
and neighbors were collected wholesale and a little
girl was safely ushered into this world. The
person least disturbed was the mother, who her-
self made a sudden start on life’s journey from a
railway station. Is, then, miscalculation heredi-
tary ?

The filthy plague-stricken Chinese quarters
at Alexandria (N.S.W.), burned recently by the
health authorities, were, it has transpired, used as
a “benevolent asylum’’for aged Chinese by their
countrymen. Charity is very cold spelt in
Chinese—and not much warmer spelt in
Australian.
THE UNLIT LOVE CANDLE.
"I want to get a divorce from my wife on the ground of not sufficient warmth and love on
her part. How do I stand in the matter if I prove my allegation? ”
CANDID ATTORNEY : “ Not the remotest. With that face, it would take you all your time
to convince the jury that any female ever consented to be even reasonably civil to you;
and as for expecting said female to show any warmth, I personally consider that
utterly preposterous. ”
Two strangely ambitious suicides recently
passed away in Victoria. One hung himself from
a tree 20ft. high near Brighton. The other evi-
dently heard of him, and promptly hung himself
40ft. high from a big Moreton Bay fig at the
corner of Lansdowne-st., Melbourne.
* * * *
The Bulletin lias already told the story of the
Gippslander who hanged himself merely because a
tree overhanging a gorge near his camp “ offered
such splendid facilities.” There is also on record
the case of a Melbourne storeman who was heard
to say half-a-dozen times in the Course of a year :
“Wouldn’t this lane be surprised if it found a
man hanging there one of these mornings!”
pointing to a beam where hung the lift-pulley.
One morning he fastened the rope about his own
neck, and swung himself from the landing. “No
reason could be assigned for the rash act.” A
man’s ambition counts for nothing before a
coroner’s jury.

The Martinique disaster
is making the people of Mount
Gambier (S.A.) very thought-
ful. The two celebrated lakes
are obviously craters, and the
mount itself an evident vol-
cano—technically “ extinct,”
no doubt, but so was the
West Indian one. Also,
it is a matter of common
knowledge that the water
in the lakes occasionally
sinks or rises some feet
without any above-
ground cause.
The publication in
a Sydney daily of
what purports to be a
likeness of a man un-
der committal on a
charge of assaulting
children should be re-
sented in the interests of
justice. At best of times
the processes of daily
newspapers do not favor
credibly-accurate portraiture,
and to employ them in the case of
a merely accused person is sometimes
as serious an injury as the publication of a dis-
torted version of the evidence.

A recent case in Vic., when a woman got a
decree of nullity because she had married her
deceased husband’s brother, suggests that the
Deceased Wife’s Sister Act needs to be supple-
mented with a Deceased Husband’s Brother Act.
* * * *
A small robbery being reported at Castle-
maine (Vic.) lately, the local sergeant was given a
description of the suspected offender. Being a
worldly individual of keen intuition, he wasted no
time picking up clues, but went straight to the
crowded tent where the Simultaneous Mission was
being held, and almost the first man he set eyes
on was the culprit, with the stolen property still
on-him. A day or two later, at Melbourne, Chief
Justice Madden released, during good behavior,
a couple of small boys who had broken into and
robbed a Presbyterian church. One of these
youngsters—whose blood he froze by first telling
them they would formerly have been burnt at the
stake for such a heinous crime—was a model boy
and prizetaker at the church Sunday-school.
There seems to be a moral about these items'
somewhere.
The table on which the late Queen Victoria
signed the Commonwealth Constitution Act now
rests in Melbourne National Gallery, creating a
hideous discord among the pictures with its
garish vulgarity. There is only one thing in
Melbourne like the Queen s table, and that is the
ghastly.hearse adorned with golden angels which
is occasionally seen about the streets. That table
should stand for all time in the Gallery as a
shocking example of the degradation into which
art and beauty fell during the Victorian era.
It and Herkomer’s picture of the Queen
should be placed in a room by themselves. They
are objects too sacred—and too awful—to be asso-
ciated with the Gallery pictures.
* * * *
A Melbourne hearse-factory has quite a
collection of unconsciously-humorous customers’
letters. One, from a country undertaker, urges
the factory to hurry up with the gorgeous meat-
waggon, ordered and in course of building, as the
local mayor is sick, “and not expected to last
more than three days,” and the rival ghoul
may get the job of “ planting ” him “if delivery
is_ not made at once.” The factory sat up all
night and finished the vehicle within the time •
but the mayor recovered, and both ghouls were
disappointed.

Residents in the wealthier Melbourne
suburbs have been having an anxious time of late
as the result of the depredations of a clever and
audacious band of balcony-thieves. In conse-
quence of the rigid patrol system upon which the
police are worked the thief knows exactly where
any policeman will be at a given hour, and by
timing the peeler on his beat, and keeping five
minutes behind him, the balcony-thieves may go
right through a suburb without disturbing the
constable or causing him to mSss his official ap-
E ointments. In some of the swagger suburbs the
alcony gang have fossicked over a whole row of
houses in one evening; and now the authorities
are considering the advisability of overlooking a
little irregularity m the patrolling of the regular
police. °

“Oktis” writes:—
In a N.S.W. country town a tailor employs eight hands
mostly boys. The workroom is about 10ft. x 12ft and
air space is considerably reduced by the walls being
thickly hung with used paper patterns. Light is admitted
through a small, firmly closed skylight. There is no fire-
place. Into this workroom all the employees are crowded
a L K it. fl ' l i qUently h , ave t 0 "'ork overtime and at night. One
of the boys employed developed consumption, and died
quite recently. The workrooms in country towns are
becoming as insanitary as many in Sydney.
Those interested should petition to have the Fac-
tories Act extended to the town.

The cheerful readiness of people to swear
things in court was manifested again in a recent
Australian Police Court case. A red-headed man
was arrested with a mat in his possession, evidently
because the police vyere of opinion that a man
with red hair could not possibly be the rightful
owner of a mat. Prisoner insisted that he did not
steal the mat, there was no visible proof that he
had stolen it, and the case was further complicated
by the appearance of two claimants for the mat,
each solemnly swearing - that the mat was hers,
and identifying it positively by certain marks and
brands common to 10,000 mats. Prisoner was
given six months for being found in possession of a
door-mat above his station in life. The claimants
were still wrangling over the property, however,
and as he was being removed the culprit called
out to the court: “X may as well tell you the
mat doesn’t belong to either of these people,”—
and he gave the name and address of the party to
whom it did belong, and went to gaol affirming
his innocence.
* * * *
Already some results of the religious
hysteria, introduced lately into Melbourne by
American missioners, are being felt. The other
night, a well-dressed young woman went to the
South Melbourne parsonage, and, kneeling down,
prayed loudly for the vicar, Canon Dickenson
who openly disapproves of the ways of these mis
sioners. She was promptly handed over to a
policeman, who locked her up for “ insulting be-
haviour.” A professional man, noticing in his
toddy a bitter taste and a white sediment, dis-
covered that his wife, hitherto most exemplary,
felt it her duty to poison him, because he was in
the habit of taking each night the drink which
leadeth to hell fire. From religious hysteria to
religious mania is a short step.

Male witnesses of recent murders in Aus-
tralia never, according to their own accounts, run
away ; they invariably set out in search of the
police. One such, not so long ago, with a
realisation of his duty thrust on him, set out to
find a policeman—leaving a lot of women at the
mercy of a violent man armed with a pistol.

Arm Inn,” was the district home of cock-fights

Therefis a story going the rounds of Mel-
bourne afternoon-tea circles that a small school-
boy lately assisted at his parents’ wedding.
Which recalls the musical comedy “In Town,”
wherein Maud Hobson and Grace Palotta (then
playing her first Australian engagement) figured
as members of the co. at the Ambiguity Theatre.
Behind Graces back the.other girls in the mimic
green-room were speculating as to whether she
was French, or otherwise. “ Oh, she’s verv
French,” said Maud Hobson; “her father and
mother are about to get married, and she’s going
to keep house for them.”
The Victorian Government recently ap-
pointed a commission to inquire into the state of
its railways. Phis is one of the conclusions it
came to :
It must be admitted that the Competition of the tram ;
ways has seriously affected the suburban railway returns
which, when compared with the electric tram system in
.Sydney, may well cause regret that the Melbourne tram-
ways were allowed to go outside the control of the State
Government.
Adelaide will also be sorry long before Snow’s '■
monopoly expires, and will probably shoot rubbish
on Premier Jenkins’ grave by way of expressing
its repentance. And Perth and Brisbane will be
sorry also. Ditto Hobart, when it wakes up. .
A creeping paralysis has attacked the
public spirit of Victorians latterly. Apathy and
indifference are displayed by Melbourne writers,,
municipal reformers, and public towards the rapid-
ly-maturing assault of a private electric tramway
and power co. upon the public road rights of
several municipal districts between Wyndham
and Melbourne. Practically no opposition has
been made to the proposal, except by some of the
Port Melbourne councillors, although not only are
. hueaments of the Adelaide Snow scheme
visible in the Melbourne road-raid, but also the
value of the existing tramway system, when it
reverts to the people, will be materially damaged
if the plot succeeds. Yet even now, whenthematter
has reached the referendum stage, and when the
three-vote elector is going to endorse the “con-
cession ” of the people’s rights, press and public
scarcely know that there is such a scheme.
