A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, May 27, 1902.
Mt Dear Arini,—
There is something of the pathetic and the
comic in the attitude of mind with which a new
Governor comes to us. I fancy I can see Sydney,
as the grinning Little Boy at Manly placing a com-
fortable chair for the new ruler, then pulling it
away at the instant that he is about to sit down.
The new Gov. comes here a very Etna of good
intentions, but Sydney blows him cold and
extinct. Sir Harry Rawson, caught at Auckland
by the interviewer, said what all the new Govs,
say in different phrases : “Both myself and wife
have come out intending to make our home with
the people of New South Wales, and to do every-
thing we possibly can for their happiness and
prosperity.” That is the big-chief-and-loving-
father spirit in which every Governor enters Farm
Gove. In fancy, Sir H. already sees well-worn
tracks diverging from the “Cranbrook” wigwam
to the wigwams of his people; and in
fancy he and they foregather to smoke
in peace, and “ponder how the tribes of
men might prosper ” ; after the manner of Pau-
Pak-Keewis in the Hiawathan legend. Soon he
finds that “his people” look down upon his
patriarchialism and say, “ He’s got a terrible lot
of flies ! ” They gaze on his feathery sceptre and
think ‘ ‘ what'price the crowbar ? ”
Large, heavy, hairy, iron-grey, short-necked,
with the humorous eyes and beaming, polished face
that betoken a sound digestion, and a voice that
you could hear through rolling drums, and a here-
upon-my-vessel gait. That’s an impression of
the new State Governor as he stepped ashore at
Farm Cove, prisoned in a tight uniform, bur-
dened with gold bullion. The landing was the
usual military-naval-Church-and-State affair.
Lady Rawson is a plump, serene-faced dame
with a fine head of greying hair. She wore
silver fox furs, a heliotrope gown, and a black-and-
pink toque. Miss Rawson would be a pretty girl
even if she were a simple A.B.’s daughter; as the
Admiral’s lamb she is a marvel of good looks, with
a nice expression, tan and ruddy coloring, and a
lot of warm brown hair. She left a dress memory
of blue and black and brown sables. The swear-
ing-in ceremony at Govt. House was as simple as
noughts and crosses, and short as a yawn. Miss
Darley and one or f wo other official ladies were on
the platform.
The most striking feature of Space Governor
Rawson’s arrival was the procession of dingy,
shabby and nasty carriages hired from the
undertakers ? in which the local Assembly
and Legislative Council outraged the public
eyes. Compared to these conveyances the
much-despised robbo was an ornamental creation.
If members cannot afford to each hire a decent
horse and dray on his own, they ought to club
together and do the thing decently in a drag or a
better-class keb off the rank.
The Gov.-General will, fill our vacuity
for two months or so before he departs for Lon-
don. We should be very glad to have him even
as a grass-widower and not long to reign over us,
for the G.-G. isn’t cross-cornered, or egotistical, or
solitary, and he can foot Scottish dances as well
as any Rob Roy that treads a measure for prize-
money at a Caledonian sports meeting.
Dear Lord*Beauchamp is engaged, so the papers
say. Lady Lettice Mary Elizabeth Grosvenor, a
sister of the Duke of Westminster, is the elect
one. What nice, old-fashioned names she has ;
they recall mignonette and sweetbriar. You
don’t hear of many Gladys, Muriels and Rubys
among the coroneted class. Lady Lettice’s father,
heir to the dukedom, died some years ago and her
mother re-married —with a Mr. Wyndham,
a political light, man of letters, and grand-nephew
of Lord Edward Fitzgerald, the Irish “Rebel.”
At present he is Chief Secretary for Ireland.
The Grosvenors are immensely rich ; one of
them could afford to marry a penniless
Teck, so good little Willie will be related
to royalty. A month hence we shall see Lady
Lettice’s portrait in all the illustrateds amongst
the contingent of high-toned English brides,
mostly very plain ladies who don’t seem to know
how to do their hair. Our ex-Gov. isn’t the man
to marry one who isn’t Lady Something or other.
A while ago a group of titled women tilted
with their pens in the Lady’s Realm at the
subject of unequal marriages. I remember nothing
of the articles save that one of them remarked
upon the “ repulsiveness ” of the marriage of men
of rank to actresses or barmaids. That was one
for the peerage and young millionaires !
Concerning barmaids : I see that the W.C.T.U.
is tucking up its sleeves for a fresh attack on the
girl entrenched behind the bar. It avers that her
hours are too long, that she ’tices men to drink,
and that the conversation addressed to her is not
on the spiritual or intellectual plane, but savors
of a ribald version of the Song of Solomon ; also
that she dyes her hair, drives about with men
wedded elsewhere (and frequently ends by marry-
ing a doctor). Antagonists don’t want to throw
her out of work ; they say “ there are far better
avenues of employment open to her, as lady clerk,
typist, tailoress, dressmaker, domestic servant,”
&c. So the female clerk, typist and (average) dress-
maker has a better “ avenue ” than the barmaid ?
Some of us believed de Rougemont, but none of
us believe that! Better in a certain sesthetical
sense, perhaps ; but, financially, infinitely worse.
Looking at the woman-worker side of the ques-
tion only, a crusade in favor of waitresses and
shop-girls is much more urgently needed. I don’t
know any barmaids (save a few who are married
and in sooiety, and their memory is a blank as to
the period before they crossed the bar), but I am
acquainted with some hack music-teachers, and if
the barmaid has a harder, more nerve-wrecking
life than hers 1 hope the W.C.T.U. will win.
Glasgow is doing away with its barmaids, but is
finding work for them in its new tram offices as
ticket-takers, money-checkers, book-keepers, etc.,
and in its municipal meat-markets. Glasgow
gets drunk, according to the arrested cases,
twice as often as London, and it doesn’t
expect to get more sober because a man instead of
a maid is behind the bar. But, as an old bailie
said, “It’s no seemly fer a young woman tae
listen to the talk o’ men in their cups.” The
Scotsman just wants his whisky for whisky’s sake,
and he would as soon be served by the Jabberwock
as by someone like Grace Palotta, if the service
were as good.
A sad life’s litany was recited in Melbourne
Divorce Court last week when Maud Williams
asked a divorce from her husband on grounds of
desertion. She swore that she married Williams
12 years ago. He made £30,000 in W.A., went to
London to float a mine, and came back bringing a
strange woman with him. A second and a third trip
were made, and each time a different woman livened
his voyaging. The letters of these persons were
mostly demands for money, with incidental
references to the kisses they wished they could
bestow upon their dear Williams. For five years the
wife had had no money from the man, but when he
was stone-broke and his boots had gone back on
him, he found her out where she was at service and
sought money and sympathy from her, and got
both. Morant, “ The Breaker,” has a set of
verses, “At Last,” which might have been written
on that story. Some disconnected lines from the
set: “When I have found life’s garlands
rue.” “When all the joys and all the
zest of youthful years have fled.” “In
the sad twilight of my life I will come
back to you.” I have a vivid recollection of a
dove-eyed district visitor trying to soothe an out-
raged and deserted slum wife with exactly those
sentiments, and the wife arose in wrath, her eyes
blazing, and answered : “So Bill will come back
to me when what you call ‘the fire of life’ is
burnt out ? Will he ? Well, d his soul! who
wants him then?”
Amy Castles and co., all fresh from touring that
long, straggling M.L. province, which may be
blown up or sucked under, some volcanic or earth-
quaky day, re-appeared at Sydney Town Hall at
a concert, 13 numbers strong, increased to 23 by
encores. The singers were all in improved voice,
but the star soprano herself put the harmonious
roof on her previous Sydney work. There is no
reason why the musicianly architecture shouldn’t
have been accomplished altogether on her last visit,
but a Voice, like the ways of Providence, is an
inscrutable thing. Amy’s principal contributions
were Gounod’s cantata, “Jerusalem,” and the
Traviata aria, “A Fors e lui,” given with the
power and sweetness that such splendid melodies
deserve. For the Irish Folk Song, which the
soprano gives so often as an encore, I cannot get
up any enthusiasm. Foote composed it after
being serenaded by his family banshee. Another
encore was a winsome little ballad, “If no one
ever marries me ” a child’s reverie of how she
would fill her spinster life with a squirrel, rabbits
and a lamb, up to the doddering age of 28, at
which time she would buy a little orphan girl.
It was sung with a dreamy playfulness, and got a
treble encore. Dani’s amorous tenor roused the
usual enthusiasm amongst the huge audience.
The “fortunate soprano,” as the Herald always
calls Amy Castles, had several pleasant frisks
given in her honor during her short stay in
Sydney. Mr. and Mrs. Meagher, with whom she
stayed, took her in the Govt, launch Eva to
Riverview College, where the students gave her
three cheers, and she got a holiday for them to
come to her matinee. Minister for Works and
Mrs. O’Sullivan, Mrs. J. C. Williamson, Mr.
Kingsmill (the Westralian Commissioner for Rail-
ways), and Mr. Kidd were also afloat, with a stray
barrister and brewer or two, and some Young
Things, amongst them Miss Dolly Castles, with a
cascade of golden hair and a voice the twin of
what Amy’s was three years ago. (By the way,
the Minister for Works says he is going to make
a railway to the foot of Mt. Kosciusko and
straighten the paths to the top. Doesn’t it
freshen you to think of the terminus and
the edelweiss on the Mount ?) On Saturday, Mrs.
J. T. Toohey gave an “At Home” to meet Miss
Castles. The decorations, the browse, the pro-
gramme, dramatic and musical, and the guests,
were of the best, and had it not been for the lack
of a steam merry-go-round (without which no
“At Home ” is now really up-to-date) the function
would have been perfect.
The Sonoma had a musical as well as a vice-regal

In glancing over the list of six or eight hundred
wedding-gifts to the Hornabrook-Sargood pair I
noticed letter-scales. The gift stood out in high
relief from the flat, monotonous reduplications of
silver photo-frames, egg-cruets, and bangles. A
literary pair gave it. You’ll see now that the next
fashionable wedding-present list will contain
several letter-scales.
The mind of a dog 1 A Gordon setter friend of
mine had two pups which worried the life and
lovo out of her. They refused to be weaned, and
no matter how she objected, insisted upon accom-
panying her on her walks. Her owners had left a
bush and wild-flower district seven miles up the
northern line, and just come to live near town.
On an afternoon lately Mrs. Gordon Setter went
out for her walk but, instead of going by stealth
to evade the pups, she deliberately took them with
her and went down to the railway station which
is the terminus from the bush and wildflower
district. From that point she walked along the
line towards her former home. Witnesses saw the
trio at various stages of the journey and at the
seven miles the mother and her footsore and
exhausted offspring were seen to disappear in the
dusk into the bush. Late the same night the
setter came back, fearfully tired, but without
encumbrances. She must have reasoned out that
manoeuvre, of taking her pups to the bush and
losing them, even to the degree of leaving no trail
by walking along the metalled railroad.
Miss Maggie Moore, of “Struck Oil” fame,
was married to her late leading man, Mr. Harry
J. Roberts early last month in New York. A
civil marriage before a justice of the peace in
Jersey City was followed a week later by a
sacred ceremony performed by a Dissenting
clergyman. Then the bridegroom hastened
to the theatre to play his part in the matinee
of “Du Barry.” Mrs. Roberts will sail for
Australia some time in July to fill an engagement
here. Her husband will continue to play under
Mr. David Belasco.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“There are people, who lay some of the blame
for the Gov.-General’s flit on the shoulders of the
haughty Countess. She has never liked Aus-
tralians, say these wiseacres, and now ( she fumes
at the idea of impoverishing her sons for the sake
of the beastly colonies. Very rich folk are easily
‘impoverished,’ you know. A boy, reared at the
rate of say ten thousand a year, is a wretched
pauper if pa can’t leave him more than a beggarly
hundred thousand or so. Hopetoun’s trek
is a bit of bad luck for his suite and household.
A viceroy doesn’t throw the handkerchief at
the same set of pals more than once in a lifetime,
Wally always excepted. Mrs. Corbet deserves
something of her hostess, anyway. Since she
came, people pronounce the countess not so bad,
after all. It is surmised that Mrs. Corbet may be
an heiress, which would account for much. Her
husband’s brother married a Scotch heiress, so
moneyed wives may run in the family.
“An extra number of Sherlock Holmeses were
switched on for a certain fashionable wedding the
other week, and the arrangements, this time, were
simple and direct. The ‘officers’ were stationed
round the big room where the presents were spread
forth, like the lamps on a suburban station. There
they glimmered, silently, effectively. Once or
twice, corpulent legislators, hustled by the fashion-
able crowd, collided with one of these light figures,
begged pardon breathlessly, looked a second time
for a civil answer or protest, saw the Sphinx
stare, damned softly, and turned away in wrath at
tilting against a detective —or something of the
sort. Heretofore the rule obtained of scattering
two or three of the ‘ watch-and-catch-’em ’
brigade among the wedding-guests, but a wise
host conceived the sensible idea of letting those
with thieving propensities be warned off present-
lifting by that ominous row of Standard Police
Lamps.
“ They are a bit particular in ‘ dear dirty
Dublin ’ as to the status of the lady visitors to the
race-course. One of the rules laid down for the
new Phoenix Park Club members is that an
application for any half-guinea ladies’ tickets
must be accompanied by the names and addresses
of the fair ones. And this rule was passed. Not a
solitary member was there to veto it. What
model men ! How would that rule fare in con-
nection with Flemington or Caulfield course?
Imagine the names and addresses of some of the
golden-hair flashlets being handed into the club by
various respected members ! Of course a lawyer
has always the excuse of pressing claims of a
client, a doctor of a patient—a bootmaker of his
last.
“ Men are just now being bribed, and coaxed,
and terrified into sporting medallions of wifey and
the little chips of the old block. Two or three
woman-fearing and peace-loving husbands have
the framed baby’s' photo, hanging honestly from
the rolled-gold chain. Others dangle them till
out of sight of home, when they are ruthlessly
stowed into an inner pocket. The man who
absent-mindedly hands you the family ‘On Our
Selection ’ medallion for a match-box is the most-
to-be-pitied of all. He wears such a wretchedly
imbecile, hang-dog expression of a ‘ couldn’t-help-
it-had-to ’ air. Surely women needn’t hobble the
poor paymaster like that J
“A strong, racy dame of exalted associations
was being lunched luxuriously at Toorak t’other
day. A swagger party it was, but muzzled.
Things were carried out in much yellow-plush
style. When the adjournment to the boudoir
came on, the hostess proffered, somewhat timidly,
a tray of highly-perfumed cigarettes. ‘Will
you?’she asked. ‘Thanks,’ said the racy dame,
‘ I have my own smokes with me.’ She had. She
drew out a cigar—maduro—from a case, lit it, and
settled down to a snug yarn.
“The cream voile and white cloth costume is
to be the toniest turn-out of wintery house
gatherings. The choice, but not profuse, de-
corations of an art-brown velvet on the cream
gown is to be the crowning feature of the
toilette. Lady Chermside wore such a frock at
her Bananaland reception. At an At Home given
by that languid, dreamy-eyed, ivory-complexioned
dame, Mrs. Arthur Moule, no less than six cream
gowns tried conclusions, but only one—and this
not the smartest—boasted the subdued nut-brown
relief. By the way, Mrs. Moule, who has seen
several summers in India, is one of the few clever
conversationalists in Sassiety equal to the playful
wit of professors and book beaux.

“ A medico on the staff of a foreign man-o’-war
visiting these shores, got into hot water through
his amorous overtures and most promiscuous and
daring flirtations with girls he met or merely saw
in the street. One man threatened him with a
thrashing, another sent a warning in a caf6 through
a waiter, and a third laid in wait and scattered
the foreign devil out for winking at his lady-love.
“The gentleman in kharki ordered to Africa
has lost his early glamor of romance. One pf the
best-looking and most petticoat-loving of the
crested warriors left Melbourne last week for the
third time since the war commenced. His depar-
ture on this occasion proved the anti-climax to the
former glories of hero-worship. It was evident to
him, and to all at the feeble farewelling, that
the mustard uniform has lost its power, and fallen
into disregard. His first departure from Mel-
bourne, when the war-lust was in its first hysteria,
was wept over by many charmers—bona-fide
charmers. All the other officers ground their
molars with envy; they had only two or three
apiece. The second time, one girl wept and waved
him off. The third time, there wasn’t even one.
“ During the first act of ‘ The Thirty Thieves’
on Saturday there was a painful sensation in the
d.c., near to the manager’s box. A stout gentle-
man fell from his seat in a fit, and had to be
carried out to the foyer by as many strong arms
as were necessary to manage the heavy weight.
The case—it was Mr. Pollock, the now hotel-
keeper, formerly of ‘the profession’—turned out
to be not so serious as it had looked, for presently
the word went round that Mr. Pollock was all
right again. Then the people who had been
witnesses of the depressing side-show recovered
their appetite for frivolity.
“ ‘ Orpheus and Eurydice,’ a spectacular, classi-
cal drama in two acts, will be performed at St,
Kilda Town Hall on Thursday, June sth, in the
sacred cause of charity. The cause is the City
New Boys’ Society. The author of ‘O. and E.’—
Mr. Arthur Seymour—will play the male name
part; Miss Eileen Buckley is the lady who married
Orpheus, and a good deal of the music composed
by Mr. Gollmick will be sung by Hector Lightfoot.
At classical items like this, Hector is a highly-
appropriate Christian name.
“In a quiet way, old man Parkinson, the most
successful of living Melb. trainers, is doing more
to develop an opera school than either of the
conservatoriums. A finished little performance
of the ‘ Faust ’ garden scene, the other evening,
followed on similarly successful excerpts from
‘Trovatore ’ and other operas. His pupils do not
rush into the theatres to perform, but give their
shows in a quaint little theatre in their tutor’s
pretty back garden, the audience sitting on the
greensward on summer nights, after Italian
provincial fashion. Mr. Parkinson, who figured
as leading tenor of fifty-three grand operas in
London and the provinces from the early fifties to
the late eighties, taught most of the Melb. singers
all they know. One of his earliest pupils was
Florence St. John, the buxom English burlesque
singer.
“The sad death of the Septimus Millers’only
child, a girl of 13, draws attention to the fact that
big families of bouncing boys and romping girls
are no longer common, at all events in wealthy
homes. Sons are especially scarce and prosperous
parents lean all their hopes on the frail shoulders
of one or two delicate girls. Note the leaders of
fashion ! Mrs. Baronet Clarke and Mrs. George
Chirnside have each two daughters. Mrs. Browne
(nee Chirnside) has one. The nurseries of more
recent households, of Mesdames Gordon Lyon,
H. M. S. Cox and Vallange are, so far, tenanted
by a solitary girl baby.
“Youthful bridegrooms are the order ot the
day. Last week, one of the early editions—
Augustus T. Hiskens—took unto himself for wife
Miss M. K. Macdonald—a tall, dark, willowy
girl, whose foreign air and style of dress are only
found a delusion when her attractive Scottish
accent falls on the ear. Miss Macdonald is a smart
gowner, and her bridal turn-out was one of the
most fanciful and well-thought-out of any recently
on show. A feature of the dress was its wide picture
frill sleeves, its handsome yoke of pearls, its wide
belt, and its much-laced and festooned-skirt,
flowing wide, in the fashion of all French and
American toilettes. Jessamine was worn in a
corset with orange blossom. The two sisters of
the bride were also foreign in their style, and
somewhat original in their fashion. French frocks
of white soft silk, with long Beaufort coats of
brocade, and rose chiffon vests, were an effective
foil to the two brunette dazzlers. The bride-
groom is the son of a clergyman, and looks about
21. His dark, handsome bride is taller and more
notable.
“Tea-talk now at Toorak is mostly in the
direction of private hospital treatment. So many
women go regularly to the sawbones’ mills for the
usual fashionable operation that the comforts and
luxuries of each expensive institution form the
staple subject of Toorak’s tea-tattle.

N.S.W. Speaker Abbott, and a member of Sydney

S.A. Parliament only possesses one man
with a title—Speaker Coles. The Council, barring
the conventional “ honorables, ” are all plain
Misters, although President Stirling is in danger
of being adorned with a Coronation tag.
Squatter Robert M’Farland, of Riverina,
who introduced the Vermont merino to N.S.W.,
and ruled as a pastoralist king from the early
days, has died on boardship the Konigen Luise,
on what he planned as a pleasure trip to England.
Rev. J. E. Mercer, of Gorton, near Man-
chester, has been nominated Bishop of Tasmania.
He is more “low-church” than any previous
bishop of that English isle, and there is grave fear
in Hobart that he won’t be sufficiently con-
temptuous towards “Dissenters.”
Milman, the retrenched (Q.) Stamp Com-
missioner, was the official who collected the evi-
dence in the notorious Hopeful case —all too con-
scientiously for some people. The revelations in
connection with the Hopeful’s blackbirding
methods were about the nastiest knock the traffic
has ever had.
At Bendigo, a few days ago, an applica-
tion for an old age pension was granted to a miner
over 70 years of age, and totally blind. Ere the
welcome dole of Bs. per week was granted, it was
elicited that the cheerful veteran was working
(unsuccessfully enough) a 40ft. shaftdaily, and unas-
sisted —by maans of ladders. He lost his vision
years ago.
By the deaths of John Rounsevell, J. T.
Fitch (a wealthy draper), and J. Angas Johnson
(a grandson of the original millionaire Angas), the
S.A. Treasurer will realise about £25,000 in death-
duty, and he is worrying now lest the estates
should not be ready to pay up before the end of
the financial year on J line 30. All three capita-
lists died in one week.
One present Queensland Minister first
struck his present electorate in the capacity of
cook to a travelling hawker. When he first came
to Parliament he was arrayed in bell-bottomed
pants and high-heeled elastic-side boots. People
wouldn’t remember these things if his present
aggressive attitude towards his own class didn’t
perpetually remind them.

Professor Towar, the new chief of S.A.
Agricultural College, is a man so well accredited
thiit folk are wondering how the Wheat State
managed to tempt him from America. He is most
distinguished in appearance and much resembles
Wilfrid Laurier. The Professor was much struck
with Sydney on his way through the States : “It
is suggestive of Boston,” he said—and what
higher praise is possible from a citizen of the
Great Republic ?
The death of “ Father ” Kenny, of Gran-
ville (N.S.W.), is announced. The thousands of
people he baptised and married and prayed with
in the last half-century of Primitive Methodism
in and around Sydney called him “ Father, ” be-
cause, although he had given up the R.C. priest-
hood, he kept a lot of the old manner among his
flock. He held himself clear f religious broils.
Printed that Cardinal Moran is in high
favor at the Vatican, and will be appointed to
direct the mission operations of the R.C. Church,
in succession to Card. Ledochowski, now in his
80th year. Sydney’s Cardinal probably knew
what he was about when, two years ago, he was
slaving over those beautifully imaginative “his-
torical” accounts of the South Sea Islands mis-
sions !
In the first flush of their new-found
“ patriotism ” several officers of the earlier Vic.
cohentingents declined to take Imperial as well as
State pay. The red-tape war officials, however,
refused to be aw..re of this bashfulness and for-
warded their pay with the rest. Now their
patriotism has melted away, and they are reaching
for the coin with both hands. The Vic. Govt, is
also reaching, so there is a coolness between the
parties.
The late Mr. James Dick, of Glasgow,
well-known in Australia as a fortunate mininer-
speculator and open-handed man, in disposing of
his fortune of about a million-and-a-nalf, left
something like £750,000 to Scottish charities It
is understood that in his will he gave the refusal
of the .well-known and now splendidly-equipped
Mitchell’s Creek Freehold (N.S.W.) gold-mine,
which contains perhaps the longest gold-shoot in
Australia, to the present manager at a very low
price.
England, so very anxious for Australians
to take the nigger to their bosoms, doesn’t take
any more nigrur herself than she can help. For
instance, the Stamford' Earldom ! This, by right
of primogeniture, belongs to some half-caste Zulu
or other, the last Earl having married a Zulu
woman, and lived as a Zulu. But his coffee-
colored progeny was quietly passed over by the
Lords’ Committee of Privilege, who chose a white
man far outside of the direct line of succession, to
be the new peer.
Related of a N.S.W. back country M.P.
(noted for his baldness and his rakish wig) that
coming across a large body of aborigines, evi-
dently bent upon mischief, some years ago, he
got behind a tree, put his head out to the right
and showed his well-wigged skull, then as quickly
withdrew it, pulled off the wig and placed his
bald head out to the left. He executed this per-
formance rapidly for a few minutes. The dark
warriors stared, then with a wild yell of “Debbil-
debbil ” made off through the scrub.
Rumored that N.S.W. Premier See will
resign very shortly. He and his family are
arranging for a trip to England, and his retire-
man t from politics is looked upon as certain if a
title comes his way Coronation time. The un-
interesting Mr. See is a man of many interests,
and he is said to be tired of lots of things ; tired
of keeping pp with the O’Sullivan; tired of rastling
with the unemployed ; tired of asserting his status
as a State Premier ; tired of the whole game, and
eager to retire from it all as—hats off ! —Sir John

Albert Le Soeuf, aged 74, recently deceased
director of Melb. Zoological Gardens, was, for 35
years, usher of the Black Rod in Vic. Leg.
Council. He was the happy possessor of shapely
calves, and when he arrived in the Assembly with
a message, he made the courteous six bows, coming
and going, in a manner which invariably drew the
attention of members from the business in hand to
himself. He had lived in the State since ’4O, and
knew a lot about aboriginals, kangaroos, snakes
and native birds. Two of his sons have positions
in Perth (W.A.) Zoo.
Tommy Marks figured in a law-case at Bal-
larat a week or two back, and the case recalls
Tommy’s history. Marks is a Ballarat celebrity.
He was one of the men who helped to get Lalor
away after the Eureka fight, and he it was who
nursed Peter throughout the journey to Geelong.
Tommy got on to some good things after, and
made a pile, but has since lost it all. He has a
big claim on true Australian sympathy, a claim
that the S’African swaddies, whose tombs,
memorials, and bean-feasts are becoming so
expensive, never can have.
Death of Abby Booth, of Gobbagumbalin,
Wagga, N.S.W., at 87, is another proof that the
hardships of the settler in early Australian days
interfered very little with his chances of long life.
He went to the Riverina 60 years ago, when the
country was in a state of savagery, but he stuck to
it till he made Gobbagumbalin not only a profit-
able speculation, but a home having all the solid
comforts of civilisation. Booth was an English-
man, and fraternised for many years with Henry
Parkes ; both were born in Waterloo year.
Poor Alfred Austin, poet laureate, is 67
this week (May 30), yet he will have to strain his
alleged intellect by writing a Coronation ode of
some kind. How the Royal Family must miss
Tennyson, who, while always eager to sing their
praises, usually managed to sell his loyal poems
at a good rate and to secure them a wide publicity.
Austin, on the contrary, earns nothing but ridi-
cule for himself or his patron. As Austin is
Setting on in years, there is still a chance of
talonel Neild being offered the laureateship.
The Lady’s Realm (London) for Feb. tells
this of the Yorks’ visit to Melbourne:—
A novel incident occurred during the great Trades
Procession. A blacksmith jumped from the symbolic
lorry on which he was riding, and, deftly removing a shoe
from one of the horses, boldly ran towards the royal
visitors and laid the emblem of good luck at the feet of
the Princess. The loyal spectators were shocked at his
audacity for the moment, until they saw how genuinely
pleased she was with the blacksmith’s humble though
significant gift.
There is no explanation why the guard of honor
did not prong the Bold Blacksmith with a bayonet
before he could explain that he meant kindly
with the horseshoe.
PASTOR RONALD , M.H.R. FOR SOUTH
MELBOURNE.
Meteorologist Clement Wragge is now in
his 50th year. He came to reside permanently in
Australia 19 years ago, but had previously twice
visited South Australia, doing surveying and
other work. When S.A. parted with him as a
meteorologist he offered his services to other
States, and was accepted by Queensland.
Clement Wragge has issued an “ appeal ”
to the Australian public to keep his Weather
Bureau going by private subscriptions. It reads
like a royal proclamation demanding tribute :
We mean to save the Weather Bureau as a great private
concern to the Australian nation ; and the thing can be
done, if dogged will and enterprise can do it. We are not
so easily suppressed as some people imagine, and time
proves all things. We refuse to be kept longer “on the
string,” self-respect and British pride alike rebel, and so
we mean to launch out boldly and fearlessly, and if our
scheme does not succeed it will be the fault of the people,
to whom we directly appeal, not ours '
The outbreak of Dillon, M.P., in calling
Chamberlain “ a damned liar ” in the Commons
is nothing to that of Mr. Martin, member for
Galway, who in 1789 referred to a fellow member
and his sister, who were sitting in the gallery,
thus: —“These Ponsonbys are the curse of my
country. They are (unprintable), personally and
politically, from the toothless old hag who is now
grinning in the gallery to the white-livered scoun-
drel who is now shivering on the floor.” Even
Australia’s own John M ‘Elhone at his best (or
worst) never excelled that.
John Rounsevell, brother of ex-Treasurer
Rounsevell, who died in Adelaide, May 15, was a
crack whip in the early days. On one occasion he
came to a bad bit of track with a full load of
passengers and asked them to alight to save the
strain on the horses, but they said they had paid
to ride and declined to descend. So he calmly
remounted the box, drove the coach over a log,
and capsized it. Then they all got out. John,
who was reputed wealthy, sat in the Assembly for
some years, but never took much interest in
politics. He made a record by marrying three
sisters —of course, one at a time.
Demise : Capt. Machell, perhaps the
smartest man ever known on the English turf.
He brought off the Hermit coup, with which
Chaplin ruined Lord Hastings, by winning the
Derby, after Hastings had bolted with Chaplin’s
fiancee, daughter of Lord Anglesey. This lady
afterwards married Sir George Chetwynd, a par-
ticular friend of Chaplin’s, and the three became
cronies in their whist-table days. It recalls Haw-
thorne’s fanciful ending of the “ Scarlet Letter,”
when he speculates on Chillingworth becoming
the friend of Dimtnesdale in heaven, after his im-
placable enmity on earth —over Hester Prynne.
Love is akin to hate.
Dick Wood, best-known and most in-
dependent of the defeated Adelaide Labor
members, has taken to life-insurance canvassing
as a temporary occupation until the next election
comes round. Before he entered the Assembly he
was the best blacksmith in the Govt, workshops,
and he was once employed at Woolwich Arsenal.
While there he put a chalk mark on the present
King’s boot on the occasion of a Royal visit, and
Albert Edward paid up his drinks like a man.
The Prince rendered himself liable to the fee by
picking up one of the sledges to test : ts weight.
One of the foremen showed an inclination to
punish young Wood for his “ irreverence,” but
was told by the lad that in that case he would
report the matter to the Prince. So nothing
happened, and Richard was th.e hero of the shops
for weeks afterwards.
From D.T. (7/5/ 02)
Adaminaby, Tuesday.—Mr. Henry West, of Oabramatta
Station, died yesterday, at the ripe age of 91 years.
Deceased had been for over 50 years a resident of Monaro.
This good-natured man, Mr. Henry West (writes
a correspondent), arrived upon the scene just as
my father —who has himself, this month, entered
upon his 84th year—had been severely injured by
a kick from a bullock. This occurred over 40
years ago, and far from reach of medical aid. Mr.
West, who was travelling in a cart with another
man, on a cattle-trip, at once unloaded his cart,
-and, leaving the man with the goods, kindly eon-
veyed my father 50 miles, to Hay, to a doctor ;
and had then to return that distance ere continu-
ing his journey. A hundred miles in a cart to do
a good and kindly action !
N.S.W.’s ex-Sheriff Cowper’s letter on the
remount question in London Times leads off with
the personal confidence—“ when I was in the
Government.” Charles Cowper was 40 years ago
Clerk of N.S.W. Leg. Council. He drew a salary
for that office and ranked as a member of the
Government, but had not a seat in the Cabinet.
His father, “Slippery Charlie,” was Premier at
the time. The ex-Sheriff was in early life a fight-
ing politician and it was believed he would fill his
father’s boots in time, but something steady with
a fixed salary got the better of his political
ambitions. While he was a public servant he
often frightened domineering Ministers by
threatening to resign and oppose them on the
hustings, and when finally he was retired he tried
to rekindle the old fire, but it would not burn. So
he started a private business, married, and went
to see the world.
A personal note of especial interest to old
Sydney University men :
The two James Patersons lived together during the ’7o’s
in Woolloomooloo, being known as “ The Twins." James
Stewart Paterson, M.A., LL.D., who died last week, and
the sparkling M.D. (deceased many years ago), class-mate
and pal of the Prince of Wales (now King), later idol of
the sick poor in Lower Woolloomooloo, when it was
the breeding-ground of pushdom and the training
school of larrikins, where none save those to
whom “the talent” gave the “freedom of the city*
dared tread after nightfall. Barrister Paterson was a
silent man, ever fiercely absorbed in gleaning up the
fossil remains of the dry bones of antiquity, radiating
little back, not through greed but from inability to
dispense. Concerning men and their devious ways he
knew little ; of women, less than nothing, and cared
still less. This soul, badly nutritioned in sameness
of diet, was but imperfectly protected from the
ravages of a physical infirmity to which he owed
his eclipse from the meteorlike brilliancy of his career
during the ’so’s and ’6o’s. He was very absent-minded.
One day the doctor picked up a dead fowl, which he
brought home for the purposes of some experiment, but
his housekeeper served it to Professor James as a lunch'
dainty. ‘ * Man, it naturally deceased ! ” exclaimed the
horrified medico. “ I picked it up coming home."
“ Weel, and ye might ha’ passed sooner or it might ha’
died later with advantage,” and he calmly finished up
the bird. The Professor possessed, but used
• seldom, a smile which made him the adored
of women and women-children. Vague, evanescent, un-
directable, it wandered round till it lighted by chance on
some soul that understood, and appropriated it. Present
scribess, 35 years back, had one smile gleamed on her,
and would identify it from a thousand others to-day.
The girl who waited on him at the last said : “ Did you
ever see the doctor smile ? How we shall miss him 1 "
And she wept sadly.

N.S.W. Full Court had the courage, the
other day, to boldly upset a bylaw, under the
recent Pharmacy Act, providing that, to be
registered as a chemist, a man must pass certain
tests and produce a certificate of skill from his
previous employer. The Pharmacy Board re-
fused to register a man because he couldn’t bring
along the employer’s certificate; the employer
was dead or something. It takes a very solemn
and technical view of its responsibilities, N.S.W.
Pharmacy Board.
The United Typewriter and Supplies Co.,
one of the largest concerns manufacturing type-
writers, seems to be getting more of its share of
the world’s business. One of its machines, the
“Yost,” was favoured recently by the Indian
Government with an order for one hundred and
twenty machines. In addition to this their
Australian representative now reports the sale of
five hundred “ New Century” Typewriters for use
on the Government Railways. An important
fact is that these two are record orders for the
world, and, what is more important in the eyes of
the Company, were obtained in competition with
all the leading makes. Australian buyers can
obtain both of these machines from the Company’s
office in Sydney, Melbourne and Perth.*

a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they-will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.

“ THE AUSTRALIA ” is loftily situated ; from
its dome, the Pacific Ocean can be seen, and the
whole of Sydney.
To its favorable and airy position, “ THE
AUSTRALIA ’’ adds every comfort and appoint-
ment. It is fireproof from tower to basement;
and an efficient staff patrols the building day and
night. It possesses the most elegant of drawing-
rooms, handsomely appointed dining-rooms, cosy
bar lounges and winter gardens. The system
and attendance of the hotel are unsurpassable.
J. URE SMITH (Late of Menzies’), Manager,
Castlereagh-st., Sydney.
Aboriginalities.
To Contributors. —In the case of ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
“B.B.” : Recently crows have made their
appearance in the orchard districts of Central
Cumberland (N.S.W.) and turned their attention
to oranges from which they pick and eat the pips.
Have crows ever before
been known to turn vege-
tarians? Certainly a crow
looks like a bird that is
capable of turning
anything.
IN PUSH SOCIETY.
SNUFFLEBUST: “ Wilt thou take this woman to be
thy lawful wedded wife? ”
GROOM: “My colonial oath!”
“Werrimee”: A Mr. Bowman, of
Taroom(Q-), claims, at this late date, to have dis-
covered that the bottle-tree is a valuable fattening
food for stock. Now, the nourishing qualities of
the bottle-tree (for beast and man) were fully ex-
pounded in these oolumns by writer and others in
’97-8, and its fodder value was known to bushmen
Heaven knows ho.w many years before that. Mr.
Bowman certainly didn’t blaze the bottle-tree
traok.
It is not the foliage but the wood of the bottle-
tree which is given to sheep and cattle. The tree
is cut down, chopped into lengths, and chips
either wrenched off with an axe, or stripped off
with a bar. The cattle eat them greedily, young
and old, and fatten. The strips of timber can be
put through a cutter and utilised as chaff.
•*. * *
Blacks in the Macleay district, after suffer-
ing more than seven lean years in their maize-
growing, are having such a fat season, and are
getting such fat prices, that a number, of them
now drive about in sulkies and dress in tailor-
made clothes. The sulkies are always well-stocked
with “wobble Charlie” (rum), and the gins now
spend their time on a steam merry-go-round in
tine township.
MORNING TEA AND BUSH MODESTY.
(Dawn of Day—station burnt down—manager living in tent.)
NEW COOK (with cup of tea on fire-shovel): “Here ye are!” (Shoves it through tent-flaps.)
Owing to drought and the high price of
meat it is seriously proposed, in several districts
of southern N.S.W., to petition Parliament to
suspend the operations of the new Rabbit Act so
that bunny may be preserved as food for the
people. Yet we have persons howling for the
introduction of some foul disease amongst rabbits !
* * * •
Taking into consideration the statement of
the leading butchers that meat will yet reach a
shilling per lb. on account of the drought, what a
harvest there will be for anyone with rabbits to
sell at 9d, a pair ! Australia may yet eat up its
rabbit scourge.

Re beer-drinking. When the N.S.W.
Lancer Regiment was ’Ome at Jubilee time,
the men received many an invitation to
“ ’ave a sup.” At first they were raw to
English drinking habits. One day four
or five were lined up at the bar by
invitation, when a tankard was pro-
\dded, in cockney fashion, for all to
drink from. The first lancer didn’t
know the “game,” so when the
vehicle—containing two quarts —
was handed him, he didn’t bring it
down until he had drained it !
Then, how the degenerate London-
ers cheered him ! The prestige of
the N.S.W. Lancer Regiment in
beer-drinking was afterwards un-
challengeable.
“ Brumbie ” : I know a far-
mer in Burrowa (N.S.W.) dis-
trict who gives one of his cows
skimmed milk daily. She waits
at the dairy-door every morning
for the milk to be skimmed.
When the milk, often a large
dishful, is put out to her she
greedily drinks it and goes off to
feed. This cow yields much more
and richer milk than any of the
others. Is this sort of thing eommon ?
« » * *
Recently a traveller, representing a Melb.
produce-export firm, journeyed out Orange
(N.S.W.) way to buy rabbits. He came back to
Sydney rabbitless —prices were prohibitive. Crops
have failed ; stock is dying wholesale ; there
is no water, grass, confidence, or credit between
the mountains and Bourke. Yet the trapper who
can snare a dray-load of rabbits with a stick put
up the price so high that the rabbit business fell
through.
• * * *
While Queensland Govt, legislates against
prickly-pear as a pest, Queensland farmers are
finding it a veritable stand-by in drought time. It
is boiled in big tanks and mixed with a certain
amount of bran and served to cattle. In India
they don’t call it a curse but a blessing.
* * * *
How things are inN.Q. ! The manager of
a station recently told a shearer, waiting for the
shed to start, that he would either have to pay
cash for the grass his horse ate, or do fencing as
an equivalent.
• * * •
A Vic. female rabbit-trapper, who applied
for a pension, was refused on the ground that she
made a living by trapping. Two little boys at
Flinders (Vic.), aged five and six years, have for
about a year been making two and three shillings
each week by trapping bunny or running him
into logs with dogs.
“T’othersider”(W. A.): Travelled recently
from Northam to Perth (W.A.) in the same
carriage as three cohentingenters. They had
landed, at Albany a couple of days before. We
asked one of them what he thought of the Hand-
cock-Morant affair. “ There’s no points in taking
prisoners,” he said, reflectively. “ You know we
only get four biscuits a day for rations, and our
officers tell us if we take prisoners we’ll have to
feed ’em out of that. So, when we do take ’em —”
he paused and laughed. “ I suppose you let them
go?” said my mate. “Let ’em go,” growled
another soldier of the Empire, “put a bullet
through the blankers.” They said a good many
more things concerning the conduct of this
“humane war” which, if true, would make the
allegations of the continental newspapers appear
but mildly censorious.
“ Monowai ” :
Be animal suffer-
ing :
Descartes held that
animals did not feel,
and were merely cun-
ningly constructed
automata whose move-
ments we naturally
but mistakenly inter-
pret in terms of feel-
ing. Then, according
to Shakespeare, the
“poor beetle” we
tread on experiences a
“pang of sufferance”
as great as “when a
giant dies.” Between
these extremes there
is room for an infinite
variety of opinion ;
and science seems to
have said little except
that both extremes are
to the last degree im-
probable. It seems
pretty certain that
animals do not feel so
much as sentimental
ladies (if there are any
in Australia) imagine ;
but, on the other hand,
no one who knows
aught of the nerve-
structure of the higher
animals can doubt that
the volume of suffer-
ing during an Aus-
tralian drought must
be appalling. And the
normal squatter’s cal-
lousness to it is yet
more appalling. I
know of one who philo-
sophically observed: “Well, it takes 20,000 sheep
to manure my run.” “Steele Rudd” affords a strik-
ing instance of callousness to animal suffering. How
many comic incidents in “Our Selection” turn on
the torture of some poor beast, for which the -author,
whether by art or nature, betrays no sympathy.
Now, is this callousness normal or abnormal. Is the
Australian on the land forced into brutality by circum-
stance ? I put this question to a Riverina squatteuse or
squatteress the other day. “ Well,” she said, “ you don’t
expect men to be tender-hearted.” One excuse plainly
will not serve : “Nature is so brutal that we learn the
trick of her. When we see her starving millions of sheep
in the hush, how expect us to be particular about driving
a sore horse, or about being merciful in our necessary
slaughter of rabbits and wallabies?” This will not do.
Nature produced no sheep or cattle in Australia. They
are a human importation, and man must bear the blame
for their suffering. Would not a law against overstocking,
i.e., stocking without some water-provision for bad years,
be both economically and morally justifiable ? At present
squatting seems to be a wild gamble on the off-chance of
a good season. And I pity the poor counters, who haven’t
the excitement of the gamble, more than the poor
squatters, who have.

Quirk, McLeod & Co., of Wellington,
N.S.W., have the season’s record for a stack of
wheat. At their sheds they have stacked 53,000
bags of wheat, weighing 5,800 tons.
* •
At one station between Rockhampton and
Mackay, (Q.) 35 men called for rations in one
day. Men are making north in droves from all
drought-stricken districts. Apropos, a P.M. in
a Q. town told writer t’other day that lie issued
orders for rations in one day to 30 men and 10
women. Happy Queensland !
“T.L.”: Re “J.A.C.’s” (8., 26/2/’O2)
method of finding the compass-points with a
watch, let him try it at 9 a.m. and at 3 p.m. and
he will find a big difference. The true North can
be found by his method at 12 noon exactly, given
the watch is correct.
* * * *
What is described as a simple and in-
genious method of making a common tin-billy,
with the addition of a strip of mathematically-
ruled paper, serve both as a clock and
compass is explained in an article con-
tributed to W.A. Education Circular by
Govt. Astronomer Cooke. The idea is a
capital one, and if the explanation, which
is a newspaper column long, could be grasped by
the average swaggie the adoption of the billy com-
pass and sundial might save life and much
blasphemy. But until the stroller Out Back learns
enough “elementary spherical trigonometry” to
wrestle with a formula like “ tan a equals sin 1 tan
h ” he will probably continue to carry his gold
repeater, or, in emergency, ask the nearest police-
man.
• • * »
Q. Labor member “ Bill ” Hamilton tells
a woeful tale of the drought in his electorate.
Said electorate, by the way. is nearly as big as
Victoria, and almost as large as Southern Queens-
land-being 84,300 sq. miles in area. At Boulia
township the inhabitants have had fresh meat
twice in twelve months, and this only when a
neighboring squatter found a couple of beasts
worth slaughtering and generously distributed the
beef all round. At Warenda Station (a run of
4000 sq. miles), which used to muster 50,000 cattle,
there is not a single beast fit to kill, and the
station people are living on tinned meat. Very
few hands are employed, and the majority have
drifted “ out where the dead men lie.”
• • • *
“ Indra ” : The back-country sheep are
eating dead rabbits. I didn’t believe it till, the
other day, I heard that sheep on a station out
from Darren were dying through eating poisoned
bunny. They not only eat dead rabbits, but
charred bones, bits of paper, fur, and wool; the
latter they are constantly to be seen pulling off
one another's backs, presumably for the clover
burr tangled therein. Truly, the poor “monkeys”
are having a bad time, and when rains and frost
corps the mortality will increase.

Tale of woe told by the father of a re-
turned Vic. colientingenter :—“ My son got on
grand for awhile, and was gittin’ a splendid lot
o’ things together. He sent us over some months
ago, a bee-utiful tablecloth, worth about eight
pun’, and a lot of bee-utiful wall-pockets. By
gum, they Boer women mus’ be pretty smart with
the needle! Well, he got enteric, and’ad to go
into horspital, and his blanky mates shook every-
think he had collected, and even got at ’im for his
own watch. Fine lot o’ cows they are ! He sez
he wouldn’t trust ’em further ’n he could throw a
blanky bullock by the tail.”
OVER THE STOCKYARD RAILS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
N.S.W.
Hard by the bells are jingling,
The nags are feeding near,
The quaint bush sounds come mingling
With memories that are dear ;
The warm night-wind caresses
As o’er the grass it sails,
Like Nelly’s soft brown tresses
Across the stockyard rails.
She was a “ free selector,”
Her beau a station swell,
And “ Dad ” was the objector
When I went courting Nell;
But Nelly met me slyly,
Though ha was hard as nails—
And, oh I she kissed me shyly
Across the stockyard rails 1
Now, many years have vanished,
And many girls I’ve kissed ;
Some long-from memory banished,
Some dear ones sadly missed ;
And these' come gaily trooping
From ’yond the yester veils,
And once again I’m stooping
Across the stockyard rails.
Ah, girls will love a rover—
And pretty lips are met,
On every track a drover
Has ever wandered yet.
So Love must wait and languish
Where wand’ring life entails
A parting kiss of anguish
Across the stockyard rails.
Now in the autumn mellow
Sweet faces come in train,
All smiling round a fellow
Who may not kiss again !
But, oh ! the fire burns brightly,
And Memory gladly hails
The kisses Nell kissed nightly
Across the stockyard rails.
EDWARD E. SORENSON.

4000 ft., has not met any artesian water. Work is
now stopped. The cost, about £12,000, would
have given the town a water-supply from one of

“Itftick” tells a
story of White upon
Yellow -
Sing (under-porter at
the hotel I stay at in the
far North-West) is a
particularly ugly China-
man. When I asked him
why he had married a
white girl he became
frantic with excitement.
“Why me mally white
girl ? Me no want to
mally white girl. Me
wanted to go to my
countly and mally China
girl. But me rich. Me
pearler. Me say to white
girl—‘ You no mally
Chinaman, you mally
white man. ’ Then she
cly, cly, cl.v, and say me
made her heart shy, me
made her heart ashamed.
Then me c 1 y and go
away. Me hide out in
my boat—me tink me go
away to my countly and
mally China girl. Me
clawl in. No one see
me. Then when night
come, me come up for
wind to blow on me. Me
see boat come, and me
hide again, quick, quick,
but they find me. Girl’s
mudder come, say ‘ Sing,
you mally her. You bad

“ Omicron ”
tells how one
debbil - debbil
was started :
In ’72 I was
boundary-riding on
Tarrawingee Sta-
tion, N. Q. The
back of the run was
then inhabited by
a tribe of blacks,
who gave comidrr-
able trouble by
spearing cattle.
Visiting one of the
huts, my first
night’s rest was dis-
turbed by the noise
of a corroboree in
t’’e black camp;
ditto next night.
I determined to
remove the cause.
On asking German
Bill (the regular
tenant of the hut)
for some gunpow-
der to make a
flare-up, he entered
into the plot, and
agreed to make a
fine rocket. Dark-
ness came, and I
crawled to a knoll
about 50yds. from
the bi’llabong,
struck a match and
lit the fuse. It had
been insufficiently
damped, and, in-
stead of smoulder-
ing, flashed. A
sudden flare-up
made me spring to
my feet in surprise,
and the blacks saw
a great blaze and
the figure of a man
with arms out-
stretched. Then
came a bang, and
with a great hiss
he seemed to go
skywards with a
huge trail of fire.
In reality I turned
and ran. But a
shriek of terror
went up from the billabong, and then not a sound was
heard. To this day nearly all tribes near there repeat
a ] e 0 f the debbil-debbil, and it is quite impossible to
get one near the billabong at night,

WHEN MONARCHY IN GENERAL COMES TO THIS IT
WILL AT LAST BE IN ITS PROPER CONDITION.
This is a photo, of the king of the Nogoa River district, Queensland,
taken at Wharton Creek station. His Majesty is supposed to be over
100 years old. He is quite blind, cannot stand, and voice and hearing
are both nearly gone. Blacks’-protector Meston was written to, and now
the Government supplies the king with rations.
“ F.A.8.” to The Bulletin :
Re M'Cooey’s explanation re earless pigs on Buckyinguy
Station, Macquarie River (B. 17,5/’O2). What is he giving
us ? Pigs’ snouts were paid for by the Stock Board a few
years ago ; never heard of them being scalped. Buttabone
Station was the first to thin them out with phosphorus,
which killed them in thousands and possibly accounts for
the earless pigs. (Rabbits lose their ears very often after
recovering from phosphorus poisoning).
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in ; and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of Uny State) accompany.
Elo: The differenoe between the orthodox be-
liever and the unbeliever is mainly that the former is of
opinion the Deity works the universe by hand and the
latter thinks it is done by machinery. The unorthodox
reckon that there is a perfect, elaborate and unalterable
series of laws which keep the universe going smoothly
towards its destined end, whatever that end may be. The
orthodox hold that the Deity is always (at the instigation
of the orthodox, expressed in prayer) lifting it by hand
over the big ruts where the imperfect scheme of things
has got bogged (the ruts being pointed out by the ortho-
dox). The unorthodox hold that the Great Builder, who-
ever He might be, constructed the mechanism of the huge
vessel so well that it can voyage without him in absolute
safety. The orthodox hold that He constructed it so
badly that He has to run bellin'l all the time and push.
The explanation is that orthodoxy . was invented
at a time when there was practically no machinery,
when almost everything was done by hand, and
when the builder always ran behind and pushed....

“ Alba ” tells The Bulletin he has solved
what is known as the “Great Pig-feeder Mystery”:
He is a stout elderly gent, who has dined at a certain
Sydney restaurant for two years. When he enters, and is
seated, the waiter, without taking his order, calls gently
to the cook, “ Pig’s fry without! ” and brings it chortling
hot to the stout gent, who sits bolt-upright, glares at the
plate, curses the waiter’s boots, eyes, and hair, but
finally gulps down the fry. This has been going on for
nearly two years. Meeting him one day at a club, he ex-
plained the mystery of the fry: “The cursed farce is
the result of a wager made two years ago. A wealthy
friend of mine observed my tendency for pig’s fry, and
offered to bet me a big sum that I couldn’t take it for
dinner every day for two years. I took up the bet because I
had a genuine liking for pig’s fry then. The keeper of the
restaurant I frequent is stake-holder and referee. Well,
for the first three months, and up to the sixth,
I only thought eating fry a bore. At the ninth
month the sight of fry made me feel that my
eyes were changing color; my tongue used to
go dry ; I had to stiffen myself with brandy before I
could face the plate. Now, in the 18th month I can hear
pigs squealing when the mess is put before me. The cook
is an inhuman beast, giving me the same shaped curly bit
of liver, the same bit of white, underdone bacon every
day, not a scrap of potato, not a scrap of meat to help it
down. The referee is not so hard ; he told me that I
might have a week’s rest from fry, but that I’d have to
make it up at the quarter’s end. So Igo on a burst of
curry and rice and stewed tripe for a week, but it only
prolongs the agony ; I have yet to make up my deficit of
pig’s fry by the end of next week.” He sighed wearily
and took out a green cigar. “I avoid the streets
where butchers hang out pigs. I compel my family to
eradicate pig from their conversation. All pictures
and books in my house containing suggestions of hog have
been destroyed. I lightened things last week by going on
a burst of pickled herrings. I am subject to attacks of
pig-delirium. White pigs creep into my room at night
and sit in the corners watching me. At midnight they
make noises. The doctor has ordered me a diet of poultry
and green vegetables. I have six months to go before I
have completed my 730th plate of fry. And after
winning the money thej' will call me”—he wiped
his hot face—“ The Pig’s Fry Martyr.” He stole
into the street sorrowfully, his eyes fixed on the pavement.
At that moment, several wild-looking men rushed from
the direction of Sussex-street, shouting furiously at a herd
of small black pigs, that squealed and ran between horses’
legs and under passing carts, fairly blocking traffic. The
martyr looked up suddenly, covered his eyes, and ran
blindly into a pub.
Debility and Dyspepsia.
SWEPT AWAY BY
DR. WILLIAMS’ PINK PILLS.
“It is as far back as 1893,” said Mr. Matthew
Abberton, of Mummell, near Goulburn, “that
the pains of dyspepsia first became severe. For
some time previous I had been gradually becoming
debilitated. Then my liver became congested,
and this‘’brought on dyspepsia, kidney troubles,
and severe internal complications. As a result
I was afflicted with sleeplessnes, and heart palpi-
tation on the least exertion. I was restless all
night, and rose in the morning almost as tired as
when I had gone to bed. Although my appetite
became poor, and I did not eat much, still I felt
excessively full after meals. Thus, what with
want of sleep and proper nourishment, I lost flesh,
and grew weak and dispirited.”
“Did you have medical attention?” the inter-
viewer asked.
“Yes, four doctors attended me during my
nine years of suffering, but their treatment did
not mend matters.”
“ Did'you try advertised medicines ? ”
“Yes, I did, but without effect, until a friend
persuaded me to begin taking Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills for Pale People. From the first these pills
appeared to suit me.. After taking two boxes I
was vastly improved ; I slept better, and the
lassitude and disinclination for work totally dis-
appeared. I felt brighter and more hopeful than
I had been for years. Five more boxes completely
cured me. Now I eat heartily, and sleep soundly,
and you can see by my appearance that I am in
excellent health. Those who suffer from dys-
pepsia or debility will, I believe, in most cases,
find a cure in Dr. Williams’ pink Pills for Pale
People.”
Thousands of cases of obstinate liver and
digestive troubles have been cured by Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills. First, move the bowels
freely with a simple aperient; then take one of
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills after each meal. For the
second week take two after each meal; and in
severe cases three may be taken. A cure will be
sure to result. By their combined action in build-
ing up the blood and strengthening the nerves,
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills cure neuralgia, St.
Yitus’ d'ance, hysteria, nervous exhaustion in men
and women, rheumatism, lumbago, bronchitis,
chest complaints, anaemia, diarrhoea, etc. Ladies
will find them of inestimable value at all times of
life, whilst their health-building properties induce
a beautiful complexion. They are obtainable
from chemists and storekeepers, and the Dr.
Williams’ Medicine Co., Queen’s Place, Sydney,
*2/9 per box, 6 boxes 15/3, post free. When pur-
chasing be sure to ask for “ Dr. Williams’ ”—imi-
tations are useless.

How to File up the Centuries.
This is a feat best known to cricketers, and of
all cricketers probably those of Australia most
frequently perform the trick. But the best cen-
turies are those which represent years of life. For
a man or woman to become a centenarian is in
itself Nature’s own certificate of a well-spent life
no less than of a sound constitution. Of this, the
better kind of century, Australia has also had a
few notable examples, and we will hazard the
prophecy that she will have yet others. Mrs.
Elizabeth Fuller, of No. 44, Ryder-street, Surry
Hills, Sydney, N.S.W., now 83 years of age, is
still hale and hearty, and, we believe, good for a
further indefinite term of life.
Anybody who lives to the age of 71, as Mrs.
Fuller did, without knowing what it was to be
seriously ill, and then to become ill only as the
result of accident, has something substantial to
boast of. “I was7l,” says Mrs. Fuller, “when I
lost my footing while ascending the stairs, sus-
taining a fractured arm and broken collar bone.
I became an inmate of the Sydney Hospital for
two months and was discharged as cured. My
worst trouble, however, was now about to begin.
In my fall I must have injured the kidneys, for a
dull continuous pain set in in that region which
gave me no rest by day or night. Obstinate
constipation and urinary difficulties were soon
added to my troubles, arising, I believe, from the
same cause. I visited the leading hospitals as an
out-patient, and was also under treatment at my
own home by a lady doctor; but none of the
plasters, liniments, lotions, or other medicines
they gave me, proved of the least use. As time
passed the pain in my back increased so much
that the agony was intolerable, and I felt as if I
could have welcomed death. Such was my con-
dition for two years, wh.m my daughter, Mrs. W.
Johnson, of Arden-street, Waverley, gave me
three of Mother Seigel’s Soothing Plasters, a box
of Seigel’s Operative Pills, and a bottle of Seigel’s
Curative Syrup. The plasters, used in conjunction
with the Syrup and Pills, at once reduced the
backache, and the bowels and other internal
organs, were soon brought into proper working
order. I could eat, sleep, and get about again,
and, in three months, was as well as a woman of
my years could hope to be.”
For many years physicians were of opinion that,
when the human body had reached the age of
three score and ten, it was no longer responsive to
even the most appropriate remedies. That opinion
is now no longer held, and the case of Mrs. Fuller
again disproves it.
A Hopeless Outlook.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh, fetch along the washing-tub,
The bucket and the pail,
For I would weep a woeful weep
And tell a tearful tale.
I’m not an educated man,
Nor soulful nor refined,
Yet shed a tear
Because I hear
Lord Hopetoun has resigned !
A tear, said I ? I’ll weep for weeks,
Like every working-man
From Marrickville and Newtown out
To Coonabarabran.
In plagues and droughts and tariff tricks
No cause for grief I find,
But ’tis, you know,
An awful blow,
That Hopetoun has resigned !
“This world is full of blasted hope,”
Our teachers used to say,
But now our one and only Hope
Declares he will not stay !
He only asked for twice the screw
At which he took the job,
So me and you,
And others, too,
Can merely weep and sob !
’Tis true we never blew the froth
Off Hopetoun’s pint of beer,
We only had to ante-up
Ten thousand pounds a year !
These politicians really seem
To be devoid of sense,
Or he would stay
At double pay, ,
At common folks’ expense.
Of course, Lord Hopetoun thought the men
Who guzzled his champagne,
Would raise his screw to any sum
And make things right as rain.
He thought they’d square the Parliament
And make it all the same ;
But half the band
Was frightened and
The other half not game !
Thought he : “ The Guv’ment will resign
Unless the House consents ! ”
Which shows how much he knows about
Australian Parliaments !
Though statesmen dote on banquets and
Delight to dine with nobs ;
The man’s an ass
Who thinks they’ll pass
A Bill to lose their jobs !
You see, Lord Hopetoun only knows
A fat selected few,
And doubtless he was guided by
These bounders’ point of view.
They’d laugh and chaff and say : ‘ ‘ Ha, ha !
What ? Not sufficient screw ?
Please make no fuss,
Leave that to US —
Will Thirty Thousand do?”
And then he’d pour the sovereigns out
In quite a regal way
As Scotchmen very often do
When someone else must pay !
Benevolence—by deputy
Has ever been the pride
Of clansmen keen
Who dwell between
Auld Reekie and the Clyde !
But now he’s spent his blooming splosh
(A pure Australian phrase)
Thev’ve gone and left him in the ditch—
It’s mean in many ways.
It is in fact enough to break
A Caledonian heart!
They will not pay
And yet we say :
“ The best of friends must part ! ”
I would respectfully suggest
(And pray my Lord agree)
A National Testimonial;
The limit, one bawbee,
And then Australia’s working-men
Will raise a mighty cheer
And cry : “Once more
We pay the score
Who never drank the beer ! ”
Alas ! I fear he will not stay
And that is why I weep.
(The buckets and the washing-tubs
Are several inches deep !)
Oh. Barton! keep Lord Hopetoun here,
Oh, do ! for goodness sake !!
For if he goes,
To crown our woes,
Our bursting hearts will break !!!
W. T. GOODGE.

There has not been a more interesting
Australian book published for a long time than
“Bushman and Buccaneer”—giving the life and
writings of Harry Morant (“The Breaker”).
Every bush man will want to buy a copy for the
sake of the complete collection of “The Breaker’s”
verses which it contains. The book gives a photo-
graphic reproduction of the “ copy ” of Moran t’s
last verses, written in gaol, and many illustrations,
from authentic photographs, of the Bush veldt
Carbineers’ operations. Price one shilling.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Has it been called Astray-lia !
A cold truth from Sydney D.T. :
Britain has absolutely no friends among the nations
except the colored peoples of China and Japan.
Britain has so far spent £225,000,000 on
suppressing the Boers; and how much that money
is required now to suppress Uncle Sam !
If the Indian residents of the Common-
wealth are given—as they request—a special
member of their own, it will, at any rate, give
Australia’s Noblest Son a chance of getting into
next Parliament.
The Australian Governments entrusted
with choosing Australian teachers for the S’African
concentration camps, have been told to remember
in the selection that their work will be of a
“semi-political nature.”
Outside Sydney, N.S.W. has 44 towns of
over 2500 inhabitants. Of these ten —Goulburn,
West Maitland, Grafton, Orange, Liverpool,
Penrith, Dubbo, Hamilton, Lambton, andBourke
—show a decreasing population.
Joseph Chamberlain now calls the Free-
trade doctrine “an adherence to economic pedan-
try and old shibboleths.” Within the last few
years the British person has begun to east quite a
large number of strange bricks at his old god.
SOYLLA.
Peace, posing as virgin however she may,
Is forever a-chiid to War ;
His progeny bark from her womb o’er the bay.
Where Charybdis engulfs the Poor.
Alciierinoa.
See’s Conference of Premiers was very
“ social ” indeed. It was a conference of Premiers’
and Attorney-Generals’ wives far more than one
of male politic ians only. It would be interesting
to learn the total cost, and out of what fund the
money was taken.
There is an. impression now in Federal
political circles that Lord Hopetoun’s resignation
was at least as much due to constitutional as to
financial reasons. Following its usual policy, the
Barton Government is said to have suppressed a
portion of the correspondence relating to the
resignation.
A TIP FOR TOBY.
Visitors are requested to be wary of the lion.
The departmental minute issued by Labor
Minister Perry, intimating that the terms of
N.S.W. Shearers’ Accommodation Act may be
temporarily relaxed or waived by the inspectors,
should refresh the mind of Parliament on one of
its main functions. Whether the minute referred
to be justifiable or not, the fact remains that many
Acts are, from time to time, emasculated by regu-
lations and departmental minutes, and the emas-
culation in almost all cases takes place where em-
pioyers’ interests are concerned. Where em-
ployees complain of the incidence of an Act they
are invariably met with the official information
that while the law is as it is tbe.Government must
do its duty. The employers obtain a relaxing
regulation, or departmental minute ; the em-
ployees must agitate for a repeal of the Act.
Philp talks of reducing the number of Q.
members. But Q. Labor party consists of 24
members, or one-third of the Assembly, and if th e
Laborites object, Philp will need every man-Jack
of his followers and all the six Independents to
get the necessary two-thirds majority—a somewhat
remote prospect.
The chief waverers in the Senate tariff
debate are Simon Fraser, the Vic. Rich Landlord
relic, and Dobson from Tasmania. Simon, at the
elections, was claimed by both high and low
tariffists, and he tries to fulfil the expectations of
all his constituents by voting alternately with the
Government and the Opposition.
Whatever faults and weaknesses they have
seen and deplored in Peacock and his Ministry, it
behoves Vic. democrats to line up in a phalanx
behind him now, for he has definitely put forward
as an essential of his policy the first determined
assault on the Leg. Council stronghold made by
any Ministry since Berry’s disastrous Embassy
failure.
The Defence Department makes a be-
ginning at treating Australia as an entity by wisely
forgetting the N.S.W.-S.A. boundary line and
working Broken Hill in conjunction with northern
S.A. But it is not consistent; it still sends a
Sydney officer all the way round —an unnecessary
2000 miles of travelling—-to put the reservists
through their drill.
Out of 22 Polynesians recently brought to
Bundaberg the local Health Officer refused to
pass 21, on account of defective physique. Here-
upon the gay blackbirders immediately shipped
the whole batch of 5.9. wasters North and landed
them at a port where no exacting official existed.
They’re all grafting now in the cane and getting
ready for the Kanaka cemetery.
Some of the wild curses of N.S.W. anti-
Billites during the Federal campaign are coming
home to roost. To Sydney D.T. (appropriately
enough) falls the task of recording this :
Some members from the other States are beginning to
lay great stress on the need for the Federation having a
port of its own. The peculiar argument is used that the
Federation may at any time be in danger. of civil war
through a rebellion in N.S.W., and that it is imperative
that the Federal territory should be so situated that
communication with it could not be absolutely cut off. by
the insurrectionary State. The idea of a possible rebellion
in N.S.W. is, indeed, entertained by some Federal Repre-
sentatives as a contingency that must certainly be
guarded against.
Sydney Telegraph suggests that it is
only necessary to reduce N.S.W. Assembly—not
the Council; the Government is merely to cease
making new appointments, and the dying-out
process will do the rest. But if the Assembly is
reduced to 80 members, the Council is supposed
to come down to about 40. That means waiting
till about 25 members die off, and by that time the
40 survivors will be of the average and toothless
age of 75. No State can afford to be governed by
men who are so old that it doesn’t matter to them
what happens. Even a Vanderbilt or a Rocke-
feller has no stake in the country worth mention-
ing when he reaches 75.
The cheering news is cabled from China
that war between Russia and Japan is considered
certain and not far off. Japan, apparently, is
hustling for that war with all her might. If it
can be brought on, and if another Power, however
small, can be drawn in along with Russia, then
Britain, by the terms of the treaty which the
Salisbury Government has made, must join in
and lick Russia on Japan’s account. And all this
trouble was brought about to secure British trade
in China, while under the Freetrade system no
trouble at all is being taken to secure British
trade'in Britain’s own ports, and every year a
smaller proportion of the shipping that goes in
and out of Britain’s own ports carries the British
flag, and a larger proportion carries the flags of
other nations.
