“ Micro ” to The Bulletin :
Now that his baptism of malignant criticism has brought
Labor member and Minister Trenwith somewhat to his
bearings and to a sense of his duty to the workers, would
it not be wiser of the workers to drop once for all their
insane jealousies with respect to him, and use him as the
Socialists of France used Socialist Minister Millerand— i.e.,
for the enactment of measures and the carrying out of
administrative policies, which only the tenure of a
Cabinet seat can secure ?
The John See proposal for a referendum
on reduction of members cannot well be opposed
by the N.S.W. Democratic party, but prompt
steps should be taken to put before the voters the
true facts of this alleged “ reform ” movement
—that it is largely Tory, and that local govern-
ment and financial reform combined with the
abolition of the useless Leg.-Council are the real
remedies for present maladministration.
“ Democracy ” puts it very straight :
According to a recent correspondent of yours, Mr.
Trenwith is the ne plus ultra of the Vic. political world.
Aforesaid writer says that only envy or animus could
account for the opposition of Democrats to Mr. Trenwith.
Let us see. Mr. Trenwith voted for the expulsion of
Labor-member Findley, and thus secured a seat to the
Conservatives ; Mr. T. voted against a minimum wage of
£2 weekly to all P.O. employees over 21 years of age ; Mr.
T. voted" for the reduction in old age pensions ; Mr. T.
was a Jingo of Jingoes at Melbourne Town Hall “ Pa-
triotic” meeting, etc., etc.
The Victorian alleged “ reform ” party has
met in public meeting at Melbourne and rejected
Premier Peacock’s reform programme with much
indignant blather. Peacock’s scheme embodied
all the essentials of the Kyabrarn platform, but it
went further and proposed to make both Houses
of the Vic. Parliament thoroughly democratic in
addition to reducing their numbers. That made
the disreputable Tory “reform” party madder
than a wet hen, and it repudiates Peacock neck
and crop.
“ Myall ” to The Bulletin :
Much dissatisfaction is expressed in many parts of the
Central Division, N.S.W,, at the manner in which the
improvement-lease provisions of the Land Act have been
stretched during Minister Crick’s term of office. On one
station in Brewarrina district 80,000 acres were secured as
improvement leases by the pastoral lessees out of an area
of. 100,000 acres of expired leasehold. In Coonamble dis-
trict recently 36,000 acres of some of the finest grazing
land in the Central Division were similarly disposed of to
the station owners. The derhand for land for bona-fide
settlement weekly increases, and the niggardly manner in
which the Dept, offers land for that purpose, lias rendered
the possibility of winning a block at the ballot as remote
as that of drawing first horse in one of “Tatt.’s” big
sweeps. If the improvement-lease policy is pursued much
longer the chances will be even more remote, as the whole
of the expired leases in the Central Division will be
secured by the pastoralists and financial corporations.
Manager Badger (Brisbane Tramways Co.)
thus orated at a recent employees’ banquet
(Courier, May 8th):
He thought Queensland now had a very good Parlia-
ment and a very good Ministry. If he did not think so,
he would stop the trams running within the next 24
hours.
Doubtless Manager Badger, who is being allowed
to cut the throats of State suburban lines by
running parallel tramways wherever he wants to,
has good reason for thinking the Philp-Leahy
combination a “good Government.” What sort
of a despotism, however, exists in the city of
Brisbane when a Yankee hustler can threaten to
stop the trams running in “the next 24 hours,”in
case he doesn’t think the Government a good
Government ?
All the clamor about the necessity of
having two Houses in the State Parliaments is
based on an absurd misconception of their posi-
tion. The Federal Parliament
is the Parliament of Australia
now. The State Legislatures
are only something half-way be-
tween a County Council or
Divisional Board and a Parlia-
ment, and nobody dreams of
demanding that a County Coun-
cil shall have an Upper and a
Lower House. Certainly the-
English people, when they es-
tablished their London County
Council, did nothing so foolish.
This gross neglect of a good
English precedent is going to
make trouble in Australia.
Cohen, Broughton, and the
rest of the “Reduction”
M’s.L.A., keep on crying that,
as the See Government did not
table a Bill to reduce the num-
ber of members in N.S.W. As-
sembly, they, though full of good
intentions, could do nothing
during the last session. The
disingenuous assertion deserves
squashing. It is true that they
could not reduce members on
their own motion, but any one
of them could have moved :
“That it is desirable that the
Government shall bring in a
Bill to reduce,” &c. By
carrying that, they could
have forced the hand of the
Government, accelerated re-
form, achieved fame for them-
selves, and avoided the sus-
picion of being a time-serving
crowd, hastening to grovel </,
public opinion. Yet, not a Cohen
moved, and Broughton might as well
have been glued to his seat.
Colonel Lassetter, back after a long experi-
ence in South Africa, gives a report on the Boer
burgher, the general effect of which is that he is
an intelligent, well-educated and courteous gentle-
man, and that the Australian might do worse
than learn from him in some respects. The men
who have met the Boer appear mostly to talk of
him in that strain. It is the men who never went
within some thousands of miles of him who supply
the information about his ignorance, dirt,
cowardice, and cruelty.
The good old Braddon Blot is the only
thing that stands between Australia and financial
wreck just now. It provides that for ten years
the Federal Parliament may only retain for its
own use one-fourth of the customs and excise
revenue, and if the anti-Australian-industry gang
managed to get the tariff revenue cut down to
£2,000,000 the Federal Government’s share would
leave it so short, unless it levied heavy direct
taxation, that it couldn’t pay Sydney Smith’s
salary. Braddon blotted better than he knew.
Seeing that steps have been taken by the
Presbyterian Assembly to secretly organise the
Protestant vote in Victoria in connection with
religious education, and that there is said to be a
R.O. Defence League in active existence sub rosa,
and that the Yellow Pup is always with us, there
should be a fair supply of red herrings in the not
unlikely event of an appeal to the people over
constitutional reform and Factories Act re-enact-
ment. Breeding red herrings seems to be the
modern rendering of the old maxim of reaction-
aries—“ Divide and conquer.”
The sexagenarian question is being re-
vived in N.S.W. Public Service circles. When
the P.S. Board assumed control it was stated that
a clean sweep would be made of all men over 65
in the service. The Public Service Act practically
**throws the responsibility of retaining the veterans
on to the shoulders of the departmental heads, and
they are loth to turn adrift men of capacity, whose
only offence is that they have grown old in the
service. But the younger men, tired of waiting
for the shoes of their seniors, are always anxious
to put the 65 years’ limit regulation into force.
Perth Morning Herald is crusading for
the abolition of the Westralian border customs on
the ground that
The people are being taxed out of all reason. . . . The
State is getting no advantage in the shape of reproductive
public works from the extra revenue.
Of course, it would be quite as easy to get it
enacted that the proceeds of certain taxes shall be
paid direct into a Public Works Fund for the
building of railways, water-works, telegraphs, &c.,
as to procure the abolition of these taxes. But
where is the Australian paper (The Bulletin
excepted) that will tackle that end of the financial
problem ?
The Kyabrarn crowd all over Victoria are,
judging by the reported names of chairman,
movers, seconders, &c., precisely the crowd that
f§ted and hurrahed J. B. Patterson as saviour of
society a few weeks before the awakened people
of Victoria, with its hob-nailed boot in the shape
of Turner, kicked him and his policy into well-
deserved oblivion. Shire councillors, elected on a
limited franchise to make roads and generally
making them badly, are the chief feathers in the
Kyabrarnapootra. A list of the metropolitan
members of the Reform Citizens League was
rather foolishly printed by the Argus, and it con-
tained a greater proportion of rabid Tories, bowel-
less sweaters, blatant opponents of every demo-
cratic movement, and general enemies of the
human race, than even the average Birthday-
levee list.

--EXACTLY how many homes in this country possess a piano we are unable to say—certainly
many hundreds of thousands. We may assert, however, without fear of contradiction,
that only one piano of every three is opened oftener than half a dozen times in the course
of a year. There must be some reason for this, and it is no-t difficult to seek. Nowadays one
cannot afford the time necessary to learn the technical, or linger work- part of piano playing.
Long years of constant practice and drudgery must be undergone before one acquires but
moderate proficiency. Even then one's repertory is confined to very few pieces, and every
addition to it means more practice and hard work. .
THE PIANOLA is an instrument which enables one to skip those years spent in over-
coming the physical or finger playing work, while it allows one to have full mental control over
one’s playing ; that is, to infuse just as much individuality as one possesses into one’s efforts, and
thereby create true music. The Pianola is not automatic, and that is the secret of its success,
and why the world’s greatest musicians appreciate it.
FAULTLESS TECHNIQUE.
1. J. PADEREWSKI writes: “ It executes the masterpieces of pianoforte, literature with a
dexterity, cleaiwss and velocity which no player, however great, can approach.”
HAURICE riOSZKOWsKI writes: “ Anyone hidden in a room near by who hears the
Pianola for the first time will surely think it is a great virtuoso playing; but after a time he will
perceive his error, because your instrument never plays false notes.”
A FACTOR IN MUSICAL EDUCATION.
V. DE PACHHANN writes : “ I wish to add my testimony as to its excellence, both for
professional use in reading elaborate piano compositions and for private use in educating the
musical tastes of people who have pianos and are unable to use them for want of a thorough
musical education. ... ,
JEAN DE RESZKE writes : “It will certainly have the results of popularising good music,
of giving us more educated listeners, and also probably fewer indifferent performers.’’
LENDS ITSELF TO TRUE ARTISTIC EXPRESSION.
EfllL PAUR writes : “ Nothing of the kind I have heard before can approach it. In the
hands of a competent player all the shading, crescendo, as well as modification of temfa are
remarkably accurate.”
LUIGI MANCINELLI writes : “It is a wonderful instrument. Nothing I have ever heard
before could be called artistic in the true sense of that much abused word. It will have a great
influence on the future of piano playing.
The above extracts are sufficient to indicate the nature of the reception which the Pianola
has met with among leaders of the musical world. Those who possess Pianolas, or have heard
them in use express similar opinions. Have you heard the Pianola? If not, and you will favour
us with a call, we shall be delighted to demonstrate the instrument to you. We do not plague
you to buy. Should you, however, not be able to visit us, please write for fully illustrated and
descriptive catalogue, which will be sent post free on application.
It may be had on Instalment System.

VITADATIO
Frank-street, off Munro-street,
Coburg, 27th March, 1901.
To Mr. S. A. PALMER, . T
Dear Sir,—For the benefit of suffering humanity 1
make this statement. Five years ago I was very ill and
was reduced to a shadow, the doctor who was treating
me said I was suffering from Consumption and he could
not cure me. In the beginning of the year 1898 Mr.
Thompson, who was for many years overseer m the
Pointing Department at Pentridge, who had to give up
that position on account of suffering from Consumption
and who had derived great benefit from VITATA.DIO,
advised me to call at your Institute, 47 Bourke-street and
consult you. I did so and you advised me to give
VITADATIO a fair trial, as it was a sure cure for me, I
commenced with the medicine and in a short time my
disease began to pass away from my system. I persevered
with VITADATIO for some time, gradually getting
stronger, and eventually I became quite well and gained
(14) one and a half stone in weight. It is now three years
since I left off taking VITADATIO and it is a proof that
the disease has completely left the system, as I have not
had any return of my old trouble. I shall be pleased to
give any further information about my case to anyone
calling at my address.—l remain, yours faithfully,
ELIZABETH J. WILLIAMS.
For further particulars,
s. A . PALMER ,
184 Pitt Street, Sydney.

The Melbourne dailies never fell in so
badly as in their attempt to whoop-up the alleged
Reform movement. The accounts of “great”
public demonstrations in the country are un-
paralleled for their impudence. One “great”
meeting at Kyneton was attended by just 17
people. Even the Kyabram storekeeper who
started the agitation, when he wanted to demon-
strate some more, and got a special train put on
to convey the people to that one-horse place from
far and near, found it necessary to fill up a truck
or two with cattle in order to comply with the
requirement that there should at least be some
pretence of a reason for putting on the train.
And the Age and Argus gravely chronicled next
day that the “ special ” had conveyed innumerable
persons to the hallowed shrine of Ben Goddard,
and the wishy-washy proceedings which really
happened under the auspices of that renowned
patriot were blazoned forth untruthfully as a
howling and prodigious success.

Missing Friends.
STAHN HEIRS SOUGHT.— KARL EMIL STAHNisdead,
aged 85 years. He was a native of Gratz, Prussian
Posen. His half-sister, Paulina Steinbach, married Henry
Franke, and they went to Australia about 1848, and
settled in some Australian seaport. Their children were
Lydia, Paul, and Karl Franke. Such children, or their
descendants, should write Darwin C. DeGolia, Esq.,
care Harry Monson, Esq., 27 Mercantile Square, Bangor,
Maine, United States of America, at once, and authorise
him to represent them in the matter of Karl Emil Stahn’s
estate.

Words to the Wide World
THERE ARE MANY REASONS why we pose before thepublioo! Australasia as the Leading'
Pathologists and Medical Experts of the Day. One of them is because we do more
practical good and create more permanent physical benefit than any other association of
medical men in Australasia. We are not bound down by any specified rule, our views and our
principles are as wide as the Continent over which we practice. Our systems, methods,
practices are new; there is no room for obsoletism in an Institution whose patients are
as the sands upon the sea-shore.
EMINENT PHYSIOLOGISTS from the World’s scientific laboratories admit Electricity Is the
source of Life. It is the creative and sustaining element in all animated things. Our new
methods prove that Electricity can be applied judiciously! but to be effectual the system
must be prepared by a course of special renovative medicines. Then a great and remarkable change
occurs. The worn-out, physically enfeebled system becomes strong in strength, palsied
reproductive systems become obedient to the powers of volition and sensation, functional
disorders are remedied, the nerves strengthened, the circulation equalised, and Health
is assured.

AN OPEN INVITATION.
JUST because we have a Splendidly Stocked and Handsome Shop, and because we have sense enough to
know that well-made and well-fitted Windows and Show Cases serve to keep the Stock in A 1 condition,
is no reason why you should think that our prices must be high. Don’t for a moment think that our
shop is too grand for you or that you can get more for your money in a smaller place. If you do think so you
make a big mistake, and the sooner you give us the opportunity to prove that this is so the better will it be for
you. Does it not stand to reason that we, with our six popular Australasian stores (two more will be opened
in a couple of months, one in Brisbane and the other in Dunedin, N.Z.) can buy in larger quantities and sell at
lower prices than single shops ? Is not the possession of these six stores proof that our prices please the public ?
Next time you want anything in our line, come in and see how well and how cheaply we can supply your
wants. It will cost you nothing to look round and get all the information you want, and even if you don’t buy
we won’t be offended. Let the purse be fat or lean, the same careful and courteous attention is given to all.
If you are out of town your name and address on a post card will bring our 1902 Catalogue, and you will see
that we can serve you just as carefully by post as if you were standing before the counter.

insists that they shall tell only what they know,
not what they think or believe. Time and again

the wandering wit-

we are not interested

we want jto hear
what you actually
know.” There is no
such trouble experi-
enced with the
worthy witnesses of
the cures wrought by

GROSVENOR STREET
W. H. Traill
THE LATE W. H. TRAILL , FORMERLY
EDITOR OF “ THE BULLETIN."
William Henry Traill, journalist, who died, at
Brisbane, on 22nd inst., was born at Westeve,
Orkney, in 1844. He came of a race of freeholders,
bolding their own at Westeve through the quiet
and the troubled centuries. Orkney was too
narrow for him at 17, and he came to Australia,
jaokarooing first in Queensland; then a return to
the Old World, to find Orkney narrower still.
Australia again; a turn at station-managing,
marriage, and a tentative settle-down in the
Mines Dept, of Victoria, under that merciless
autocrat Brough Smyth. Young Traill endured
two years there ; got back to Brisbane, found a
place in the Lands Dept., and did some good
work. Gresley Lukin was his colleague there, and
when Lukin was established as chief of the Courier
and Queenslander, Traill said good bye to the
oivil service, and entered on his life work as a
journalist. It was not altogether a bad life then,
for Lukin knew that big papers were only to be
made by able men, and gathered a ring about him
such as could hardly be found m any Australian
city to-day. There were Brunton Stephens and
Carl Feilberg, “ Redspinner ” (William Senior),
Dick Stewart, Dick Newton, Ernest Favenc, and
Traill—“Old Man Traill,” as he was first called
then. The Queenslander was a paper to read in
those days, and the Courier too good maybe
for the little northern capital. Traill showed what
live journalism might do when, as special com-
missioner for the Courier, he burst up the dum-
mying gang on the Darling Downs. Next change
came with a call to Sydney. John Fairfax and
Sons invited him to edit Sydney Mail, and he
accepted. That did not last long. Ho took over
Reuter’s agency in Sydney, but continued to
write for the three papers of the ancestral firm till
the big change came.
Traill had not lived in a mean or a narrow
school. The tough Orkney breeding had been
followed by large and varied experience in Aus-
tralia. In his civil service years he had seen the
seamy side and the multiplex humbug of official-
dom. As representative of the Courier and
Queenslander he had felt the potentialities of
robust and earnest journalism. Reuter’s work
had given him a good grip of the large facts of the
world’s life, and when in the hurly-burly of The
Bulletin’s affairs of that date an opening appeared
he stepped in and filled it. It was a fighting time,
and Traill carried on with an enterprise which
verged on recklessness. Amongst his best services
to The Bulletin, to journalism, and to Australia
were the engagement of Hopkins and Phil May as
artists. “ Hop ” came when the old threepenny
edition was enlarged to the present size. In view
of that development Traill went to America to
find a man. It will be admitted that he succeeded
in the quest. Phil May was secured in the same
way on a subsequent visit to England. It is
not difficult to trace the influence of Phil May to-
day in every illustrated periodical of the world.
With those two men, girding together at all sorts
of Australian humbug, and opening out new
sources of Australian humor, “Old Man” Traill
was seen, perhaps, at his very best.
As Editor of The Bulletin Traill began making
footprints in the paths of Democracy- The paper
gave him his opportunity and he gave it a plat-
form and a policy. Up to that time politics had
not been a marked feature of the paper, but from
his accession it impressed his intellectual
strenuousness and courage upon the disorganised
democracy of the day. Sectarian animosity, at
that time sedulously instigated for party purposes,
was the subject of his constant attack, vigorous
to the verge of ferocity, for it was clear to him
that the path of reform could not be trodden till
this obstacle was removed. The visit of the
Redmonds in the Irish Home Rule interest
enabled him to show that a Scotch Protestant
could consistently demand redress for the wrongs
of Irish Roman Catholics. He strove to elevate
the question at issue from a sectarian to a political'
plane. That he did not wholly succeed in doing
so is accounted for by the spirit of the times. His
effort was made at a time when, to refrain from
denouncing the cause the Redmonds advocated,
was to invite detestation deeper than that which
centred round “pro-Boers” twelve months ago.
There were some prominent politicians of that
day whose views, nationality and faith should
have compelled them to the Redmond platform,
but who preferred to let the Orkney Islander
fight the battle while they retained their pro-
minence and popularity by reason of their caution
and unbroken silence. They were counted wise
in their day, but their wisdom in saying nothing
and Traill’s courage in saying a good deal and
saying it at the top of his voice, were long
remembered by the party which always polls
heavily in the K to P booth.
The despatch of the Soudan contingent gave
Traill another eagerly-seized opportunity of in-
curring popular displeasure. The Bulletin stood
alone in opposing the hysteria of that occasion,
and Philip sober reversed his own decision in
due time. But Traill’s most enduring journal-
istic and political monument is not to
be built from mere occasions. These and
other like particular incidents and occasions
illustrated his courage and principles, but his
best political service to N.S.W. is to be found in
the series of articles in which he hammered at the
abuses of the time, and gave direction and added
force to the party of reform. Progress was then
deemed incompatible with political or journalistic
decency, and it is impossible now, with the altered
state of popular opinion, to appreciate the bitter-
ness and vehemence of opposition and denuncia-
tion that then met any voicing of Radical views.
But in spite of property votes these views pre-
vailed. Payment of members came in 1891, and
paved the _ path to Parliament for the Labor
Party; bricks and mortar ceased to have any
representation after 1893, and the facilities for
roll-stuffing were then abolished ; land and income
taxation followed, and Protection was achieved in
1901. Traill’s efforts for Federation gave him
a claim to rank with the earliest and strongest
of its advocates.
• As one of the promoters, and as editor of the
Star, Traill continued to assist the unpopu-
lar cause, and made a further effort in its behalf
by asking East Sydney to elect him to the Assem-
bly. Of course it enthusiastically refused ; but
later, after giving the Attorney-General a very
close run for South Sydney, he led the Protec-
tionists who won the constituency. But he had
none of the arts of a politician. He did not assist
those of his constituents who wanted billets, and
failed to take part in local agitations, and he was
much too candid—an unpardonable fault in elec-
tioneering. In the House his ability was acknow-
ledged, but he was a rebellious partisan, believed
in his own opinion, and showed that he meant to
be guided by it. He was always too strenuous to
be politic ; too firmly convinced of the import-
ance of the principle to be willing to qualify it to
placate others. The man whose mind does not
first of and all the time, consider persons can-
not hope to be a Premier, or even a leading
member of the House.
Pity perhaps that such a man as he was ever
entered politics, or meddled with commercial con-
cerns at all. The humbug, the tergiversation, ■
the chafing restraint, the humiliating associa-
tions of political life worried and wore, even as
business sacked and spoiled him. His later
return to journalism was not altogether a
success. Men of his mould find it hard to weave
unless the pattern be of their own designing and
the thread of their own spinning. Amongst the
directors of half-hearted daily papers he was the
devil amongst the tailors, and as the tailors were
on their own board the devil had to get. Late
work in Brisbane is not to be taken seriously.
The man was dying and knew it, and he wrought
not in fulfilment of his life’s purpose—only to satis-
fy the exigent demand. But Australia will not
remember him so, but rather as “Old Man”
Traill, of the old hard-working, fighting days, and
to all worthy to hold it, that memory is good.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
Big thing of the day, Moorefield Handicap, 1£
mile, fell to Wootton’s Prodigal-Nursegirl gelding, Soldier,
who started among the outside lot, and won handily.
The Maine, boomed for weeks, hot at 6 to 4, ran a poor
second. Up-country top-notch performer, Winoline, ap-
peared to be backed well, but manner of her running—
with crack rider Barden up—if correct, was very ill-
judged. In face of Soldier’s capital track-work and con-
sidering the fact that he beat Horace fairly easily, before
latter recently lost his maiden certificate, in a good
“ field,” after years of “ going to do something,” punters
marvelled why he carried so little (if any) stable money.
Wootton is evidently not the bettor he was.
* * * ■
Stewards at Moorefield gave some signs of life
Saturday. Much-talked-about and waited-for Valente—
tipped for long as sure the first time of asking—was, to-
gether with her jock and trainer, “sent-up” for 12
months, through an “unsatisfactory ” display. Rider of
Oin, in the same race (Flying Handicap), was “ severely ”
cautioned, though how this is going to affect him isn’t
too clear ; and owners of Loch Lotta and Chippy Norton
were mulct in sums of £5 each for late scratching. There
were other cases that called for drastic treatment, but
probably the stewards thought they had done enough to
start with. Result: A mild panic among the ever-in-
creasing army of constant crooked goers.
• * * *
Kogarah (Sydney) trainer - owner, Hughie
Roarty, nicked Kogarah Stakes (6 fur.), Moorefield,
Saturday, with Rock Harbor—a colt by Gibraltar-Miss
Hawthorne, who finished admirably under punishment.
The “ good thing ” was confidently anticipated too, for
as little as 2 to 1 in a field of 14 “ novices ” found ready
acceptance. Rock Harbor is a finely proportioned, well-
grown three-year-old, bred by Mr. A. Houston, of
Singleton, who got the colt’s dam for six guineas, and
has since sold three of her progeny for 255 guineas.

Clever jock M‘Curley died at Braidwood
(N.S.W.) recently from consumption, cetat 22. The dead

Hurdles from which he never properly recovered.
M‘Curley rode Tricolor when that nag won the Sydney
Cup easily, and started at 50 to 1 against.
• * » *
What must the Sydney “ shop ” harvest be ? Of
22 in the list for Tatt’s Flying Handicap at Randwick on
the 17th, only 7 faced the flag, and of 19 in a similar
event at Moorefield on the 24th, 13 were withdrawn—all
on the course at meeting. Small wonder that the town
Mo. can pay heavy fines and still smile.and live on the fat
of this droughty, plague-stricken ,_.ad. New Westralian
criminal law, in operation since Ist inst., which provides
that betting-shop-keepers shall be liable to 3 years’ im-
prisonment without the option, should break them up,
but, despite it, betting is as brisk and the police as in-
active as ever in Perth. Still the law is loaded, and
once it goes off there’ll be a slump in the Mo. business.
M.L. front-rank turfite, Mr. G. C. Stead, has
gathered in £12,235 in stakes this season—verging on
£4OOO in excess of Rupert Clarke’s premier Australian
haul last year. Mr. Stead’s grab includes £3765 con-
tributed through Cruciform as the proceeds of 10 wins.

Despite official assurances to the contrary, one
of the team, writing to a friend in Sydney, declares
positively that five of the members of the present Aus-
tralian XI. are on half gate-receipts. Perhaps, however,
the organisers of the team have changed the agreements
since the matter has leaked out.
Jack Want, K.C.i “A tote-shop is a place where
you never get your money back ; a betting-shop is a place
where you may get it back if you are very lucky.”

was destroyed at Wagga t’other day aged 28. Crack

through this nag.
* m * *
Brisbane Cup winner, Palmer, was foaled in
Victoria and bred by Mr. Jas. Wilson, junr., by Pilgrim’s
Progress from Zeph, by First King, from Yardley (imp.).
Ringmaster and Lonsdale were brothers to Zeph. As a
two-year-old Palmer won the Third Caulfield Futurity
Stakes for his breeder. Sydney nags, Robato and Luck-
now, started first and second favorites respectively ;
former ran second, and latter nowhere. Palmer humped
8.11 —the third heaviest burden carried to the front in
the same event. Bonnie Bee’s load (in ’B6) was 9.11.
Battalion (in ’97) had 10.3 up. Robato won last year, with
Brise de Nuits second, and Circe (who occupied a similar
position, Saturday) third. Palmer’s time (3.32 J) for the
two miles is the fastest accomplished since the journey
was increased Jmile —14 years ago.
> * * •
Narelle —winner of Q.T.C. St. Leger, recently
—was bred by Mr. J. R. Smith at Tucka Tucka (N.S.W.),
and got by the imported Tucka Tucka sire Gozo, from the
Tucka Tucka mare Sing Song by Bandmaster from
Canary, by Kelpie from Mary Blane, by Plover. Previous
form indicated Narelle’s success. In seven starts as a
two-year old he got away with Q.T.C. Hopeful Stakes and
Separation Plate. This season Toowoomba Shorts Handi-
cap, Q.T.C. Derby, Stanley Plate and Toowoomba Xmas
Handicap were appropriated by him, also he ran second
in Queensland Cup, and third in the Guineas. Future
engagements embrace Brisbane Cup, wherein Narelle (as
a gelding) has 9st.—l6lbs. in excess of w.f.a. allotment.
• • • •
Startling evidence re the punting infatuation of
the toilers in Great Britain has been laid before the
“ Betting Inquiry Board.” One reliable witness declared
that the miners and factory-hands in the Northern coun-
ties lose a considerable percentage of their earnings to
the pestilent “ metallician,” who waits for them on pay-
days. One fellow at South Shields was seen to take 236
bets in three hours from men, women and children.
These victims of the punting habit can’t be said to live
on hope. They only exist on disappointment, mocked at
by their stronger-minded co-workers. Meanwhile, the
middle-classes grow more and more antagonistic to horse-
racing.
m * m *
Cricketer Jack Worrall—smarting under exclu-
sion from the Australian Xl.—has written to London
Sportsman vigorously condemning the team. The only
member whom he eulogises is Hugh Trumble, who,
strange to say, hasn’t played in a match yet. Every
other member is either a “ has been,” or a “ never was,”
or else a “ never will be.” But, for all that, every mem-
ber whom he has abused has justified his inclusion ; Jones,
Howell, and Noble have bowled exceedingly well ; and all
have, at one time or another, “ come off ” with the bat.
Further, Worrall states that he “ cannot see how the pre-
sent Australian team can hope to hold their own against
England on any class of wicket, but on one damaged by
rain they must go down, for they have not a forcing
batsman on the side, and very few of the men have any
idea of batting when the ball is biting.” And, as an
answer to this, every match has been stopped by rain and
the wicket damaged, and the team hasn’t lost a match
yet, having played five —won three, drawn two. So much
for Mr. Jack Worrall’s opinion !
* * • *
Maclaren will almost certainly be captain in the
first test-match, the English, anxious to win, wishing to
have the advantage of his knowledge of the visiting team
and its weak points. Up to date, this Aus. XI. has
done better than any other, and the Englishmen are now
not so sure of the ultimate result of the tour as they were
when Darling’s men left these shores.
» • » *
Success of Jones and Noble in Leicestershire
match placed them top of Australian bowling averages—
Jones having 10 for 12T0, and Noble 27 for 1274. Noble’s
bag was far bigger than any other bowler’s ; Howell’s 20
ranked next. With the ’99 team Jones’ most notable feat
was 7 for 31 against Truro ; then followed 10 for 84
(Middlesex), and 7 for 88 in the second test-match. Three
years since, Jones and Noble got rid of Leicestershire for
28—Noble getting 1 for nil and 7 for 15, while Jones
credited himself with 3 for 5. Leicestershire and Aus-
tralia have contended nine times, and former only suc-
ceeded once (in ’88). Two matches were drawn.
* * * *
Australia beat Leicestershire by seven wickets.
Latter’s initial innings closed before lunch, and so
bad was the pitch through previous wet weather, that
first day saw 14 wickets bagged for an aggregate of 94
runs—less than seven a man. Australia responded with
126, and Leicestershire’s second effort produced 143 ;
then the visitors hit up 69 for three wickets. The all-
round hero was Australian Jones, who topped all scores
with 40, and secured eight wickets for 55. Noble captured
12 for 69 —eight of them in the second innings for 48—
which shaves Armstrong’s figures of eight for 47, opposed
to Notts. Yet the latter’s performance was much the
better, as the bats against him were superior and got no
aid from the wicket.
« * * *
Oxford v. Australia match, Thursday and Fri-
day last week, went to the ’Stralians by an innings and
54 runs. ’Varsity totals were 77 and 183. Australians
declared first innings closed with six down for 314.
Speedy scoring on second day secured this result, 227
being-tacked on in 2J hours. Trumper notched 121, the
fourth century of the tour and the second to his credit ;
other two were Darling’s and Hill’s. Oxford was this
season rated not over-strong, which explains its poor
showing. Up to now ’Stralians have contested six
matches, won four, while two were abandoned. In three
engagements Australia’s innings were prematurely closed.
Visitors’ aggregate of runs so far is 1858 for 59 wickets,
which averages 31'49, as against 1568 for 102 wickets and
an average of 15.37. Australians haven’t on any occasion
up to now had the full benefit of two innings.
* * * *
Apropos Australia-Marylebone C.C., commenced
Monday on M.O. Ground, and in which at time of
writing Australia seems to have a fairly good
position. In that sensational match of ’7B, which
opened and ended on one day, and Australia
won by 9 wickets, old-timers Spofforth and Boyle
wrought the havoc. In ’96, Marylebone side went near
getting even by outing all the Australians for 18. Pougher
and Hearne did the damage. Last tour the Australians
won both matches with a wide margin to the good, and
brought honors so close that if they succeed this time the
announcement will be “ easy”—five wins all. Personnel
of the ’Ome team, as cabled, is exceptionally
strong and much ahead of any so far tackled.
Storer has the reputation of being (with Lilley)
the best wicket-keep in England. “ Ranji.,” Fry,
Palairet, Warner, Mitchell, and W. G. Grace are all fore-
front bats, while ex-Australian Albatrott and Hearne are
bowlers of the first order, and Llewellyn and Braund rate
among the best all-rounders. Team for first test-match,
which opens at Birmingham this Thursday, could hardly
be stronger than M. 0.0., though room may be found for
Rhodes, Maclaren, and Hayward, while Lilley might fill
Storer’s place. Regarding Trott: he (in ’99) performed
the previously unparalleled feat of accumulating over
1000 runs, and accounting for 200 wickets, and equalled
this achievement a year later.
* * * *
Cabled Tuesday that English XI. for first test
beginning this Thursday will be chosen from Maclaren,
Jackson, Fry, Mason, Ranjitsinhji, Jessop, Lilley, Hirst,
Braund, Rhodes, Tyldesley, Hayward, and one other.
Ten of these have previously figured in similar contests in
England, other two (Braund and Mason) played in
Australian tests. Six of the combination belonged to
Maclaren’B last allegedly “inferior” lot. All except
Rhodes are first-class bats, and the bowling strength is
Rhodes, Hirst (easily the best to be found at ’Ome)
Braund, Hayward, Mason and Jessop.
* * • *
Second round of Sydney first grade Metropoli-
tan Rugby competition was got rid of on Saturday.
Thirteen thousand gathered to see ’Varsity and Glebe
contend, and sixpences paid yielded £262. These clubs
tied for supremacy last year, but owing to absence of
N.S.W. toeballers in M.L., and, for other reasons, no
decider was contested. Glebe absolutely ran away with
the match, scoring 31 to Pother’s 9, thus springing a huge
surprise on the opposing side and most of the public who
follow the game. Form got a hard knock in this struggle,
for previous Saturday saw ’Varsity shape excellently
against South Sydney while Glebe had it all out to van-
quish Western Suburbs. North Sydney-Newtown match
resulted in a draw, Western Suburbs beat Balmain by 7
to 6, and South Svdney went under to Eastern Suburbs
9—7.
* * * *
The brutality of football, as played in Britain
by professionals, was never better illustrated than by the
awful accident at the Govan match, England v. Scotland.
A stand collapsed and some hundreds were killed or
wounded ; but the game went on as usual ; it wouldn’t
have been stopped for a thousand funerals.
* * • *
Rev. W. Allan O’Neill (aged 30), of Redfern,
Sydney, plague-stricken last Monday, was, until recently,
champion of Newington College, and centre three-quar-
ter of the best Rugby 15 that institution ever sent out.
* * * *
N.S.W. mannikin pug. “ Tibby ” Watson, who
reached London in March, had a trial go at the swell
National Sporting Club, and three fights in as many
weeks, all limited to 6 rounds. One he won, next was a
draw —which sport writers generally declared should
have been the other way about. In the third he
was walloped. Watson ought to make anything near
his weight (7.10) sit up when he gets him over a
longer journey, where there’s a chance of stamina hav-
ing a say, and the prospects of landing a knock-out punch
are better because of the wider period. Watsou was the
smallest man in the first fighting line here, and always
had to concede heaps of weight ; he seems to be doing
the same thing “ down-under,” though not to as great an
extent.
* * * *
Hottest bruising struggle seen in Sydney for
many months was between Black and Toohey at Sydney
Gaiety A.C. last week. Black punched hard and freely,
but Toohey took it all without a squeak, and came along
for more, ladling out a good dose himself occasionally.
The crowd roared like cheerful hyaenas. Black fought
with sting, and did some clever things that were very
unexpected. Toohey collapsed in the 17th round, and
result proved a facer for most of the clever section which,
when it got the chance, laid 2 to 1 on Toohey.

A. Ralston, the Maoriland road-crack, is
riding well this season. In a recent 50-miles road-race at
Invercargill, he started from scratch and ran close up to
the placed-men, covering the 50 miles, unpaced, in 2hr.
20min.—over 21i miles an hour. Ralston promises to be
in grand fettle" to represent Maoriland in the Dunlop
Warrnambool to Melbourne road-race next Sept.*
* * • »
Sydney amateur bikist Somerville a second time
went for the Goulburn-to-Sydney record on Saturday,
and succeeded in reducing both amateur and professional
figures by 7 getting over the 135 miles in 9h. 45min.
Weather was perfect right through, and the miles were
thrown behind at the rate of 14 an hour. Cyclist Lyon
will essay the new task this Saturday.
* * * *
Veteran English racing bikist, J. Green —well
known in Australia—has opened a pub. which means he
has gone the way of athletes and permanently settled
down to win the bingey-growing stakes.
* * * m
Told that French road bikist Lesna purposes
invading Australia in October with some up-to-date motor
pacing machines. Lesna raced here five years back.
Just now the planet doesn’t own a finer long distance
traveller. Last year the big European road grind from
Paris to Berlin fell to him, and later, he showed promi-
nently in the Paris-Brest struggle.

.... THERE IS NOTHING BETTER
After putting in a couple of days lately
dragging the Yarra for the supposed owner of a
cap and pipe found on the bank, the Melbourne
police were requested by an angry cabby to drop
their foolishness and come and arrest a man quick.
Gabby said he was meandering slowly home a
night or two before when a bootless and hatless
gentleman, dripping wet and with a Scotch accent,
stopped him and demanded to be driven in haste
to his boat at Port Melbourne. Cabby complied,
and the moist person disappeared on board. That
was the last he had seen of him —and he wanted
his fare. The law interviewed the fare who said
he was a Scotchman travelling for his health. He
had been strolling along Yarra bank one evening
when three men asked him for a match. This
unusual demand so staggered him that he threw
away his pipe and cap, jumped into the river and
swam across, undoing his boots on the way over.
Then he took the cab and drove aboard. He was
persuaded to pay the fare and left, promising to
send for his cap and pipe from Maoriland, and
grieving much because the police refused to fish
for his boots. Take it all round, it was an
astonishing tale and he was an astonishing person
—but he was a Scotchman. That much is certain.
Robert Brothers writes of Kyabram :
You would think that Kyabram, the accredited initiator
of the bogus “ reform ” movement, would be a regular
thunder-ahd-lightning sort of a place, a home of declama-
tion and denunciation. You would believe that its men
are fiery, untamed, hairy, hungry revolutionaries, so
goaded by oppression and the chanceful struggle for a
crust that they are ready to set Australia in flame and
then go down in song to the unborn as the Kyabramese.
But when you see and hear Kyabram you would wonder
how it came to get up any movement bigger than a
Sunday-school picnic. A Yarra-banker describes the
place as a pub., a store, a blacksmith’s shop and 76 adults.
He was wrong. It is a bit bigger than that. It is a
Wesleyan town, a local-preaching sort of hamlet, where
each fellow in turn holds forth to a few other fellows.
This is how Kyabram oratory powerfully recalls the
pulpit—a second-hand sort of pulpit. It has an oiliness
and sanctity that comes of too much tart and tea and bun-
struggle. Each speaker gets something off his chest in a
meek and mild and gentle sort of way, and before he
speaks he seems to be praying for Divine guidance for
the struggle. I can’t understand how it came about that
Kyabram (of all places) applied the match to the com-
bustible bogus reform movement. The other evening this
holy and suave town set about fanning the flames some
more, and the news was sent about, and special trains ran
so that outside towns could come to help fan. The few
who turned up found the meeting packed and well-padded
with womenfolk, and there was a holy air of sanctity over
the congregation. The men on the platform looked smug
and were evidently holding communion over the momen-
tous struggle they were engaged in. Then the oil of suave
speeches lubricated the congregation. As writer sat and
listened he could distinctly see the old pastor of his
boyhood, when he had laid aside sending us to hell with
big thumps on the big Book, and became one of us in
the enjoyment of the good things provided. Somehow I
seemed to expect that he should knock off speechifying
for a while and sing the 244th hymn, or that some of us
should now and then emit doleful groans, or that one
old hairy reformed one should get up and say, “ Oh,
Lord, what a miserable sinner I have been ! ” One fellow
who had fallen over a scarifier and broken out said that:
“ As he sat there that evening, he could understand what
it felt like to wear corsets," and there w'as a subdued
congregational titter among the female portion of the
meeting. All the evening there was no sparkle of
intellect, no declamation, no denunciation. It was just
a stranded jelly-fish affair. Just such a meeting as one
would expect from a lot of smug, low-down Noncon-
formists, who had clamored for irrigation and entered
into bonds to get it, and then refused to pay for the
blessing, thereby swindling the State of many thousands
of pounds.
The friends (Southampton, Eng.) of Mary
Tucker, who left London for Australia 17 years
ago, are anxious to hear from or about her.

Under-hand wood-chopping supremacy of
Southern Tasmania was contested Saturday by Helm
(North Huon district) and Harris (Carnarvon). Stakes of
£lOO hinged upon the result, and bushmen came from all
parts to view the struggle. Logs were 2ft. through, and
Oft. fin. round. Helm finished in 3.36 —22,\sec. inside the
world’s best. Helm, the shorter in stature, put in 165
strokes —all short—and had the first half of the job
(prior to turning) finished in 1.39, with Harris only ssecs.
behind ; but latter fell further away subsequently and
completed in 3.s9—Jsec. over the world’s best prior to
Helm’s remarkable performance. Tasmania hasn’t wit-
nessed a better match or more skilful work.
a * * *
Table-tennis craze has a big grip of Sydney.
Championship tourney, finishing at N.S.W. Sports Club
this Saturday, induced 83 entrants and big crowds nightly
for two weeks. Capabilities of contestants on the whole
were poor, but perhaps half-a-dozen top-notchers might
be singled out; and these (boys from 20 to probably 16)
will have the latter stages to themselves. Judged by
their showing, the presence of a big majority in the
tussle was unaccountable, and Could only be ascribed to
“ gall ” of the most pronounced brand.
* * * *
N.S.W. National Coursing Association Derby
and Oaks meet supplied unusually good sport. Great
Heart, an aptly-named beauty, ran chockful of grit and
won a lot to the good. He is by Lindsay Gordon out of
Gipsy Queen. Former is among the best hounds Aus-
tralia has owned, and latter is full sister to famous bitch
Lucille, now at Bathurst in the possession of Tom
Loughnane, and winner in 68 courses of 76 run. Crack
performers Corinthe, Loch Dhu (much fancied for coming
N.S.W. Oha'lenge Stakes), and Bazeke are out of Lucille.
Veteran N.S.W. courser Sam. Bladon owns the Oaks
winner Bequest (by Duke M’Pherson, from Stars and
Stripes) who shaped promisingly.
**‘ * *
Editor Bulletin.—Have just returned from a
long residence in far-northern Australia where the dried-
up native can’t sweat. Going the rounds of my old
Sydney haunts things appeared (to me) very much “ off.”
The rowing-clubs doing practically nothing ; football, as
a game, fallen back, and no athletic club worth mention-
ing. Also, just learned that our N.S.W. eight were beaten
for the ninth successive time, this trip coming absolutely
last; and I pondered much on this apparent decadence.
Next day I was invited to an evening’s “ ping-pong and
progressive euchre,” and now I know the reason. Pasty-
faced, anaemic youths flitted about the tables toying with
babies’ racquets, or sat at progressive-euchre tables bask-
ing in the smiles of equally wooden women. Others
lolled about verandahs sucking cigarettes. Lovely
indulgences indeed for the best years of a young man’s
life —18 to 25 ! No wonder the rep. eight contains no
more men like the Fitzhardinges, J. P. Garvan, Tom
Strange, Brett, Bros, M‘Gill, and Nat M‘Donald ; no foot-
ballers now like Abbott, Walker, Dr. Veech, “Chunk”
Bowman, Carson, and Jim M‘Mahon ; no smiters like
Bonnor, Massie, or Percy M'Donnell; no boxing amateurs
like Seale and Sandall—and the State hasn’t a man in any
of the running distances fit (athletically) to lace-up Mr.
Billy Macpherson’s shoes. So much for ping-pong, pro-
gressive euchre and cigarettes ! —A. J.S.
* * » *
A whist yarn. Four keen players used to meet
daily, but one day one was unavoidably absent, and the
trio lamented their luck till X appeared. “Did he play
whist?” “Of course—very fond of it.” Eureka! and
the game was made. Clubs were trumps, and they being
cleared the dealer and his partner (not X) established
their long spade suit until X and his partner had to dis-
card their long suits. When the 13th round was played,
X played the big black ace of spades, and his partner ex-
ploded : “ Why the deuce didn’t you take the spade trick
and play hearts?” “Why,” said X, “is this the ace of
spades ? Well, it’s the only card in the pack I don’t
know ! ” Then X’s partner burst publicly on the premises
with the expansion of his concealed feelings.

Seven people played poker recently on a Ger-
man mail steamer. Their nationalities were—American,
Austrian, Belgian, Dane, Swiss, German and Australian.
A Russian, a Hungarian and a Welshman looked on with
deep interest. Poker is the cosmopolitan game of the
earth.
The threatened departure of Lord Hope-
toun seems to have caused consternation among
those sympathetic people who have been in the
habit of partaking of his Lordship’s refreshments,
So great is their grief that only a more frequent
appeal to their beloved brand of whisky (D.C.L.)
can soothe it. One of the great charms of that
favorite brand is its capacity to provide one with
comfort and solace in the hour of social distress or
business worry. Lord Hopetoun is taking D.C.L.
His sorrowing admirers are taking it, —all well
informed people take it. JD.C.L. the Whisky of
the Wise.

The coming summer should prove a golden
harvest for the London hotels, and the huge Hotel
Cecil, with its fine position overlooking the
Thames, promises to obtain a large proportion of
the best of the visitors who will throng to Eng-
land for the Coronation festivities. As on the
occasion of the Diamond Jubilee in 1897, the
Colonial Office has made arrangements whereby
the whole of the Premiers to be present during
the Coronation will be accommodated at the Hotel
Cecil. These include Sir Wilfred Laurier
(Canada), Mr. Edmund Barton (Australia), Sir J.
Gordon Sprigg (Cape Colony), Sir Albert Hime
(Natal), Sir Robert Bond (Newfoundland), and
Mr. Richard Seddon (M.L.). Other Royal guests
also to be provided for at the Cecil are the Sultan
of Perak and the Chinese Special Embassy. The
management appears to have anticipated, a big
season, and the handsome northern extension on
the Strand is now completed, and will be opened
about the middle of May. Now that this wing is
finished the hotel forms a complete quadrangle,
and may fairly claim to be the largest hotel in
Europe.*
Cabled that King Edward wishes ministers
in the North of Scotland to learn Gaelic so that
they can preach to the Highlanders in their own
tongue. It would be much more to the point if
Edward VII. could formulate some scheme to
preserve a few Highlanders to be preached to.
The map issued with the 1902 edition of the
Statesman’s Year Book marks most of the
northern two-thirds of Scotland as “uninhabited.”
Freetrade is killing British agriculture. The
British landlord is allowed to evict the population
wholesale; it pays him better to turn the land
into a wilderness like William I.’s New Forest,
and let it for deer and grouse-shooting purposes
than to have men on it; and the mere people, the
free enlightened British people, have no right to
be on the surface of their own country if the land-
lord bids them go. So the best fighting race in
the British Isles is disappearing out of the country
as completely as the Tasmanian aborigines have
disappeared. The clans whose army almost
marched on London 157 years ago are practically
extinct in their native Highlands. . That huge
blank area marked “uninhabited” is about the
saddest commentary ever published on the rotten-
ness of the policy of Freetrade and of big estates
owned by dukes and such-like trash.

ff The Present outbreak of the Monte Carlo
“system ” cranks makes it interesting to note that
the lease of the Casino, which expires in 1913. has
been lately renewed by the syndicate for a further
50 years in consideration of £1,000,000 cash down
and an annuity of £70,000 for the Prince of
Monaco £130,000 yearly for expenses of running
the State (the people going untaxed), and an
annual £9OOO for the bishop and clergy. In con-
sequence of this cute transaction, the other roval
personages of Europe have wiped the name of the
reigning Prince Albert from their visiting lists.
A cheerful lady told the Melbourne
coroner lately that after she had left her husband
for the society of a nicer man the pair proceeded
down street and bombarded her late home with
stones, until her lawful owner came out and
threatened to rebuke the pair of them with a
waddy. A day or two later the newly-acquired
love : 1 )1S dr ay, fatally, whereupon the ladv
went home and took up her humdrum life of virtue
once more. There was no disturbance, nor any
remarks about the stone-throwing episode or the
lui j t6ncle ? cy to wa,lder away. She just cooked
the old man s tea as aforetime, and he just said the
steak was dashed tough and sat down and ate it
as m the happy days of yore.

Australia is just now remarkable for
•nothing so much as undetected and unpunished
murders. Dalke and Doyle, are supposed to have
been murdered by the Keniffs, and the Keniffs
seem to have vanished into space. In the Ithaca
case; the'medical evidence is that the girl was
not “outraged.” It was a case of plain murder.
No murderer has yet been caught. Recently an
old womaii was found dead in her hut at Illawarra
(N.S.W.) with a gash across her forehead. Her
purse was empty when the body was found. Of
the murderer not a trace has been discovered. In
all these three very recent cases the black-tracker
seemed to ha ve been relied on i m plicitly to find traces
of the supposed criminals. Even at his best the
myall black was never a match for a white man
who really knew the bush. Take him out of his
own country, and he is almost as useless as a raw
newehum. Now the drink-sodden town or station
blacks, whom the police commonly employ as
trackers, are good for little but to cut and cart
firewood for the station, run up the horses in the
paddock, and act as grooms for the sergeant or
the inspector. In very few cases have they con-
tributed to an arrest. In fact, the tracker is an
exploded superstition, and the sooner he is
abolished and the police learn to trust to such wit
as they have, the better it will be

Dear Bulletin.— l have noted with regret a vast defi-
ciency in your list of “ Sundry Shows ” (Melb.) during
the last few weeks. You publish no criticisms of an
entertainment running to crowded houses at the Exhibi-
tion Building every night, with matinees at the Town
Hall at 1 and 3 p.m. The American Mission Novelty co.,
though supposed to be of a religious nature, is, in all re-
spects, artistic and commercial. It has got the daily
press by the wool; photos, of the star performers are
exhibited in the shops; big calico signs are placed in
conspicuous positions all over the city ; copies of the
music used at the performances, and books written by
the players, are on sale at the doors and in the shops.
True, there is no box-plan. Admission is free. But, once
inside, the players’ powers of taking money are so good
that it practically amounts to a silver-coin admission.
The ‘ stars, ” it should have been mentioned, are Geil and
Torrey.
Fritz Muller, the young Melbourne pianist, turns out to
have been not quite such an infant as was supposed in
Sept. ’99 when he departed this country for Berlin. What
time Miss Castles figured in a short frock as “ little Amy,”
her rival prodigy, Fritz (who made his last public appear-
ance at her farewell concert, and followed her to Europe
a few days later) was understood to be in his 11th year.
The fragile youngster certainly looked no older. Now, in
a casual way, the Berlin reports of Fritz Muller’s progress
mention that he was 16 last January, i.e., he was
a youth of 13J when Melbourne last saw him. If
this is a true bill, some future mannikin instrumen-
talist of 21, with 15 years tuition up his sleeve,
may yet appeal to credulous enthusiasts as a child per-
former whose talent is great by comparison with that of
superior artists actually younger than himself? It is
satisfactory for the Melb. supporters of Fritz to learn that
Herr Joachim praises him for “ an unusually gifted young
man.” At the same time it is laughable to think that the
small boy of less than three years ago has already come to
be called any sort of “ man.”
Harry Rickards has not done too well at Adelaide
Tivoli, now temporarily closed. In future his Adelaide
co. will occasionally tour W.A., and the pious city will no
longer have a continuous vaudeville show. The only
thing that is absolutely dead certain in Adelaide is the
W.C.T.U.
“ Melb.” : Demise, Charley Walsh, age 78. His right
name was Charles Edward Pugh, his latest employ keep-
ing a Fitzroy hotel, whence he migrated from the Hen
and Chickens, Little Collins-st., where he had a great
fright, from Beath’s fire opposite. Charley sprang from
highly respectable licensed victualling interests, in Lon-
don. Came to Melbourne about 50 years ago and engaged
at the Queen’s. Acted Sir Oliver Surface in the “ School
for Scandal” at the opening of Melb. Royal, and long
remained on the Australian stage. His last Melb. engage-
ment was with Barry Sullivan, and he acted Green Jones
in the “Ticket of Leave Man.” Returned to London, and
was well known at minor theatres there. Came back to
Melbourne about 20 years ago.
“The advance booking at Drury Lane for Ben Hur
is of a most contrasted kind. While the religious
character of the play is drawing ecclesiastical
bookers, the reports concerning the chariot race
are enticing bookers from the sporting division, and
on first night you will find these widely-divergent enthu-
siasts sitting side by side.”—London Referee. Add the
Hebrew element (which is always strong everywhere on
first nights), and it reads quite like the opposition to a
Totalisator Bill, doesn’t it?
Mr. Stinson, photographer.—Please call
at Bulletin office and see manager.
Sydney plague-outbreak scored another
Australian record on Monday, when the Rev.
W. A. O’Neill, a clergyman of a manly, vigorous
type, was attacked. He was infected, it is
thought, by “slumming.”
The solidity of the Labor Party in N.S.W.
(just now the only party with any very definite
policy) was shown by Pyrmont bye-election.
Labor candidate M'Neil scored 875 votes of 1302
cast, having three opponents.
The (N.S.W.) British Empire League—a
fierce association of pink-eyed rabbits and Italian
greyhounds—intends “to bring before Parlia-
ment” the opposition of Messrs. Holman and
Griffith to the pro-Gore policy and make them feel
that they cannot “ ride over the feelings of
Britishers with impunity.”
The Federal Senate’s “amendments” in
the tariff at this stage represent nothing but an
exceedingly cheap display of alleged principle.
The real fight will come when the Reps, send back
refusals in 9 out of 10 of the amendments. It is
odds on that the Govt, then —at the right time—
will have a majority.
Sydney University law students re a public
statement by W. A. Holman, M.L.A.
That since the Battle of Agincourt England had been
“ nowhere” as a European military power, and had never
fought any battle since then without the aid of foreign
allies, who greatly outnumbered the British troops,
decided, with school-boy satire, to present him
with a copy of “ Little Arthur’s History of Eng-
land,” the most inane of the many inane English
histories. Holman’s statement was, in the main,
true enough (if he had said ‘ * campaign ” instead
of “ battle ” it would have been almost absolutely
true), though it hadn’t got much practical appli-
cation. But do the University children really
think that one gets facts from “Little Arthur,”
or from the average English history, as to
England’s doings? Do English histories, for
instance, tell of the employment of German
mercenaries or Red Indian scalpers against the
American colonists ? Do they mix up with facts
as to the glorious Crimean victories a quotation of
Acts 18 and 19 of Queen Victoria’s reign when,
after England had sent 30,000 men to the Crimea,
the English Government pleaded that they could
raise no more British troops for foreign service,
and sought for and obtained Parliamentary
authority to enlist 15,000 foreigners to form
“the thin red line”! Historical truth? You
can only get it by reading all the different nations’
accounts and finding out the truth from many
falsehoods.
As this issue goes to press the negotia-
tions for peace in S’Africa are apparently coming
near a conclusion. The frenzied Australian and
M.L. Jingoes who declared that the only terms
were unconditional surrender, and that the Empire
would be disgraced if any conditions whatsoever
were entertained, are on a back seat for the time
being. And Seddon, fooling around in S’Africa,
is in danger of not arriving in London till the war
is all over, and then there will be a terrible slump
in his importance. There was something pathetic
about the big man’s cablegram to the 10th Con-
tingent that peace was threatening, and if it
didn’t hurry up it might not arrive in time—to
slay anybody or burn anything.

Broughton, N.S. Wales M.L.A., has dis-
covered the great secret of politics—that the
public has a short memory, and the opinion of
yesterday doesn’t matter so long as the opinion of
to-day suits. He is just now in the forefront of
the agitation to reduce the number of M’s.P.,
though in October last his view, according to
Hansard, was this:
I have modified my views since April last. My short
experience of Parliamentary life has taught me that it
would be absolutely out of the question to have any
reduction in the number of members representing the
country constituencies. I myself would be quite pre-
pared to vote for a reduction of the number of city con-
stituencies, but I shall strenuously oppose any reduction
in the number of country members.
A pretty good record : April, 1901, for reduction
of members; October, 1901, against reduction;
April, 1902, for reduction. Sydney D.T. gets
very angry with its rival in jump-Jim-Crowism.
Mr. Broughton is very small potatoes indeed, and
this frost-bitten but angry press antagonism
and his cheerful weather-cock ways threaten to
make him quite an important political personage.
Some remarks by “ Aus.” :
The regulations of the N.S.W. Arbitration Court are a
sad-eyed brood, and look as if they had been hatched in
a legal mare’s nest. F’rinstance: Wanted to lodge an
application with the court for leave to begin proceedings
under Section 35 of the Act, and found that the regula-
tions contained no provision for the wording of such
application ; but it was set forth in Section 16 that “ any
person entitled to make an application to the court or
president, the procedure in respect of which is not other-
wise provided by these rules, shall make such application
by summons in Form 7 hereto.” Now Form 7 is simply
the form of summons to be served on the defendant, and
if used in making an application for leave to begin pro-
ceedings, would simply resolve itself into the court sum-
moning itself to appear before itself to satisfy itself
whether, under the regulations framed by itself and the
Act framed by someone else, there existed any good
reason to justify it in granting leave under Section 35 of
the Act. Didn’t make any application on Form 7. Felt
too tired.
An officer in Ashbourne’s Light Horse,
writing from S’Africa to a friend in England
Out of one trainload of 260 horses that arrived yester-
day 72 were dead, lying at the bottom of the trucks, and
we had to kill 47 more. We know all about the re-mount
scandal, and the fact of having to put 219 out of their
misery in a consignment of 280 causes no surprise. . . .
Many of the boys sent here as Imperial yeomen hadn’t
ever been astride a horse before. Three days are allowed
us to make riders of them. We have no less than 30 of
these poor fellows down with double rupture. These
men are crippled for life, and there are hundreds in other
camps.

Latest phthisis cure announced is an in-
jection of formaldehyde (a formalin product).
Said that
A solution of 1 in 2000 injected into a vein near the
elbow was found not to interfere with the heart’s action.
Solutions of 1 in 1000 and 1 in 500 have been used with
safety. The solution is reduced by mingling with the
blood to 1 in 100,000 on reaching the right ventricle, and
is then pumped through the lungs, which are impregna-

170,000 of formaldehyde is fatal to the tubercular bacilli.
It is declared that in many cases the tubercle had dis-
appeared ; cavities in the lungs had healed up; the
patient had been so far cured that if examined by another
medical man it would be declared that he never had
consumption at all.

Tales of the Penance Track.
The Strategist.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I?P:ere Was a lot of gaol homage paid to The Long
Tough, and the “screws” generally left him
alone. He had quite a faculty for making flank
moves when up before the visiting “ beak,” which
moves generally disconcerted the enemy and won
the day. Besides, The Tough never missed a
chance of impressing everybody with hi 3 inno-
cence. ! ' -
His own story was this Me and my pal had
a little cane stick which unscrewed at the top and
bottom, just leaving space enough to plant a
quid. I carried the stick and left it in a bar,
leaving my pal to look after it. He unscrewed
the top one night before a couple of mugs, and
took out a quid, just for a lark. When I come
back I remarked, inneroent like, that I was glad
I didn’t lose ther stick, as it was always worth a
quid ter me, and bless yer hearts, if those two
innercents didn’t up and stake a fiver it wasu t,
my mate chipping in a quid to keep the game up.
I unscrewed the top, no quid ; then I unscrewed
the bottom, and there the quid were. Now, what
der yer think if those two soft shells didn’t go and
give me in charge'as a confidence-man, and I got
two stretch straight.”
The Tough had a record in gaol. He had got five
days for fishing “ weed” from his window with a
bent pin and cotton, his window overlooking No. 2
yard. Then a quarter of a plug was found in a
boiled potato going to him in his mess-kit. Of
course The Tough had had nothing to do with
another man’s generosity. . Some of The Tough’s
more violent admirers said that he sent out a
“stiff ” (a secret letter) behind the glass eye of a.
late chum (Dummy Peters) discharged, the “stiff”
being written on tissue paper; but many doubted
this.
The trouble at present lay between The Tough
and first-class Warder Looney. The Tough being
an agitator, it was decided to work him by him-
self where he could not contaminate others. So
he was put in charge of the Church under the per-
sonal supervision of Warder Looney. , Now
Warder Looney knew The Tough smoked in the
church, but he could not catch him. In the first
place, it was against the regulations for The
Tough to have tobacco; he hadonly served three
months, and 12 months’ servitude was necessary
to qualify for smoking. Secondly, he smoked in
the church —the greater crime of the two. First-
class Warder Looney had The Tough in “D”
wing, where he was stripped and searched five
times. But no good; and 25 times he searched
the church for the plant with no better results.
Warder Looney was nonplussed. He rushed in at
all sorts of unexpected moments, but always
found The Tough busily employed scrubbing and
cleaning, and ever ready to pass an innocent
remark, which never failed to annoy the vanity of
first-class Warder Looney.
• • * •
One day Father Pat O’Donnell could not per-
form Mass, having broken his fast, and prayers were
offered instead. During the proceedings the
priest’s rosary beads slipped through his cassock
pocket on to the floor. In the vestry afterwards
Father Pat investigated the pocket, and found it
burnt through and smelling vilely of stale tobacco.
Next day Warder Looney caught The Tough’s
plant, and that worthy received six days’ cells—
Warder Looney, a strict R.C. himself, opining
that he should have got six months extra for
sacrilege.
The Spider’s Gold Mine.
The Spider, No. 54, andjßajah Riley, No. 86,
were employed in the outside gang, that tilled the
twelve acres adjoining the gaol walls. Here they
produced the gaol supply of vegetables ; at least
the others did, The Spider and The Rajah doing
small jobs, or talking to the third-class warder,
Bill MacManus, who carried a gun all day, and
cursed the whole gaol service day and night.
MacManus was discontented, and The Spider
industriously fanned the discontent, thereby pro-
ducing many small chews of “flat.” The Spider
was a glutton for “ weed ” ; the gaol tobacco had
lost him any small chance of salvation he might
have possessed. There is no surer way of gaining
a man’s affections than by telling him he is wasting
his abilities in a paltry position, so The Spider and
The Rajah did what they pleased.
A small creek found its way through the
garden, and Bill MacManus awoke from a day-
dream of future greatness to. find the pair in-
dustriously washing sand on its edge with two
rusty jam-tins. The energy displayed surprised
him, and he became an interested spectator.
“ I think there’s gold here, Mr. MacManus,” said
one worthy, in an awe-stricken voice. Instantly,
MacManus was interested, and the Spider, not
Wasting any more time in explanation, washed
away vigorously, producing a small nugget, the
size of a pin’s head. MacManus gasped ; words
failed him. If the whole outside gang had at that
moment chosen to make their escape, Warder Bill
MacManus would not have interfered. The
Washing continued. Small specks came alternately
from The Spider and his pal, MacManus sitting
down to watch them, with his rifle on the ground
(this breach of rules carries a fine of seven days’
pay). Presently, it was time for dinner-muster,
and the artful Spider played his game. Holding
the eight small pins’-heads of gold in the palm of
his hand, he started, “ This ’ere ain’t no good to
us blokes, Mr. MacManus; we ain’t goin’ out for
a bit, an’ we can’t hide it with you screws always
romping a man going in and out, so what do
yer say a couple of cakes of ‘flat,’ eh?”
Good! Warder Toohey lent his cake, bought
that morning, and MacManus’s own cake com-
pleted the bargain. The pin’s heads were passed
over, and the Outside Gang went to dinner.
• * * *
The Spider and The Rajah got the Chief Over-
seer to transfer them to the wood-heap gang next
morning. They had a far easier time of it there
than they would have had if they had worked
outside that day. . Pincher Wilson, The Spider’s
mate, employed in the brass shop, smoked, flat
for that week. The Pincher possessed some slight
knowledge of metallurgy.
The Pincher’s “Book.”
Pincher Wilson, No. 47, undergoing a sentence
of two years for “japanning,” known to the
initiated as cash-box or till-snatching, was a
gambler out and out. The Pincher, too, had an
inordinate craving for tobacco, and, as 12 months’
servitude forms the necessary preliminary to a
weekly supply of the.weed, The Pincher had to
find means. Hence the “book.” The latest
betting on the Cup was procured from Jack
Peters, just sentenced, and plunging became the
order of the day. Asa “plant” for the wagers
was necessary, on account of the marauding
tactics of the warders, the large bellows of the
blacksmiths’ shop was considered the safest and
best, and into the improvised bag, plugs, half-
plugs and quarters rattled cheerfully. The pos-
session of a lead-pencil is considered one of the
seven deadly sins in gaol, and as a memo of each
bet was absolutely necessary, The Pincher made
separate entries on. small slips of paper by means
of a piece of pipe-lead, and as soon as it was safe
and convenient, put them into a canister in the
bellows.
The danger of carrying contraband articles in
gaol lies in the fact that you never know when a
“screw” will make a raid on your person. So
The Pincher took as few risks as possible. Bets
came from all quarters. Rajah Riley, having
won a small pool the week before, plunged on
Revenue to the extent of ten plugs (or “ long
tailors ”), The Pincher laying the. odds of ten to
one. The betting list was revised always accord-
ing to reliable information received from new
arrivals or from occasional scraps of paper blown
over the prison walls by the wind, and promptly
secreted. If the scraps contained, as they often
did, a betting list, it showed a nice discrimination
on the part of the wind for which the “lumbered ”
Ikey Mo was truly grateful.
But an opposition cropped up, a regular anti-
betting league in the person of Third-Class Warder
Hennessey, eager for stripes and revenge. Months
previously a half plug of “flat” belonging to
Hennessey mysteriously disappeared while The
Pincher was in close proximity to him. A smart
search of the suspect proved unsuccessful, and as
a warder is not supposed to have tobacco in his
possession inside the walls, Hennessey had no
redress. But he bided his time in patience, and
the moment had arrived. The greatest penetra-
tion his mind could bring to bear had not solved
the mystery of The Pincher being in such great
demand among his fellow unfortunates, but an
overheard betting transaction gave him the cue,
and the rest was plain sailing.
On the night before the Cup The Pincher closed
operations, the book showing the enormous total
of 372£ plugs—Revenue, on account of Riley’s
plunge, being the one horse overlaid. On the
same night, after lock-up, The Pincher’s bag was
raided by Warder Hennessey and Chief-Warder
Fielding, and the whole boodle secured.
The news was passed around quietly next day.
No move was made to secure any of the prisoners,
but everybody believed completely in the occur-
rence till the next morning, when the news fil-
tered in that Revenue had won. Rajah Riley
worked on the wood-heap and the Pincher in the
tinsmiths’ shop, but the news travelled quickly.
The Rajah asserted that the tale of the warders
confiscating the “bag” was a yarn got up by
the Pincher, and that the Pincher had the swag,
and was a low welsher. The Pincher went down
to expostulate, but ’twas no avail. A fight fol-
lowed. In the second round it was stopped, and
the bookmaker and his client locked up,
The Rajah was discharged by the visiting
magistrate. The Pincher, for being away from
work, received seven days’ cells (16oz. of bread,
and water unlimited, per day). After all his time
and trouble, slandered, pillaged, and then —seven
days ! Yet a greedy betting public always says the
Tommy Rooks get the best of it.
An Inquest “Inside.”
No. 212 was dead. A widowed mother had
worn herself to a skeleton tramping the country
with a petition for his release at half-time. He
was only a boy ; it was only a matter of three
pounds odd, and his first time. But the Execu-
tive knew nothing of her struggles, only of his
crime, and it said, “ Let him serve the lot! ”
Then another interfered. Death proved stronger
than the petition, and No. 212 lay in the little
dead-house at the back of “C” wing alone—no
flowers, no watchers, nothing. The door was
even locked on him, as it had been during his
last hard 12 months. And three pounds odd did
it all !
A hush was over the gaol. The “greycoats”
were depressed. They picked the six for the
coroner’s jury. The Prison Act states that a
jury shall consist of six prisoners and six freemen.
So the library was cleared, and at 11 the coroner
and doctor arrived together. The Spider, Rajah
Riley, Pincher Wilson, and three other greycoats
comprised the representatives of the bond, and
compared very favorably with the six freemen,
amongst whom was the gaol wood-carter. They
viewed the body, and then sat. The G-overnor
and Deputy-Governor were accommodated with a
seat at the table; the prisoners sat on one side of
the room ; those who still maintained their places
on the greasy path of virtue on the other. The
coroner opened with a small speech to the
effect that if the boy had died outside the
faol, having medical attendance, no inquest would
ave been necessary, but here ’twas compulsory
by law.
The witnesses were called. The Night-watch
Senior Warder (212 died at 11 p.m.) detailed the
death scene. He had kept him alive while the
brandy lasted, but the brandy gave out, and
Wardsman (a prisoner in Hospital, No. 93) called,
gave evidence how the prisoner had died in his
arms, he being wakened up half an hour previous
by his groaning. (What a blessing the widowed
mother never heard the evidence !)
Then the doctor, a fat, grumpy Scotchman, gave
his evidence: “Best of medical attendance—■
couldn’t be saved—chronic case of heart-disease of
long standing.”
The Spider rose at this juncture with a hard
look, but the Governor happened to look at a
bookcase behind The Spider; this, combined with
a tug from The Rajah, made him sit gently down
again. Then the Coroner charged them, the fore-
man collected the verdict and returned it: “That
James Maitland (the corpse had one advantage
over the live body, it got its name) had died from
natural causes.”
No. 187 told No. 3 yard that the natural cause*
consisted of “chronics” and heart-disease. That
night, after lock-up, the sound of the hearse was
heard going to the dead-house, and the Rajah
climbed to his window to see. The widowed old
mother, 200 miles away, had claimed the body.
* * • «
Next morning, at the wash-basins, “The Long
Tough,” after slowly rubbing his head with the
coarse towel, said : “A man’s mother never turns
dawg ! ” And no one spoke for a few minutes
afterwards.
THE WASP.
“ Writes “ Juried ” :
There is shortly to be thrown open for alleged settle-
ment a big reserve near Temora (N.S.W.), comprising
8700 acres of young pine and ironbark, which, experts
estimate, would yield in 20 years’ time timber valued at
£56,000. The Forestry Department was too tired even to
protest against the revocation of this reserve—though one
feeble little “no” would have preserved this valuable
State asset. Now it will be handed over to a host of
cookies, who will yell for re-appraisement, owing to the
timber on it ! And there are millions of acres of Crown
Lands in N.S.W. locked up for no apparent reason—except
perhaps the fostering of a rabbit-export tradg in the
future.
