What Things We Crown!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
What things we crown !
I heard the distant cries of Tartar clan
That thronged around the car of Genghis Khan;
I saw them pile their pyramids of bones
To form the ruthless conquerors’ ghastly thrones ;
I saw his swarthy warriors sweep in flood,
I marked their trail of ruined lands and blood,
I heard their shouts each cry for mercy drown,
—What things we crown !
What things we crown !
Again I heard the cry of shrieking Rome,
When madman Nero fired it, cot and dome;
I saw them tremble at his fatuous rule,
I saw them crouch before the fiddling fool,
I saw them fawn on each Imperial craze,
And kiss the hand that set their homes ablaze,
I saw them bless his smile and dread his frown—
What things we crown !
What things we crown !
’Twas France who crowned a regal imbecile
And rent the sky with joyful peal on peal;
’Twas Russia’s peers, with reeking hands and red,
Laid diadem on Catherine’s guilty head ;
While England greeted loud with loyal lay
The doddering king who cast a world away—
From Tartar boor to clumsy German clown
What things we crown !
What things we crown !
There is but little note on little Earth
Of true desert and truly regal worth ;
Our ears are filled with sounding brass and bray,
Our eyes see naught but tinsel-trappings gay ;
The kingly man we pass, and seek afar
The masquerading rout of prince and Czar —
From blood-drunk chiefs to bloated Caesars down,
What things we crown !
What Things we Crown !
The Calves of Gold, the Vulgar Gods of Clay,
'Hie stupid Kings of padding, gilt and gay,
The pale degenerate wights, so weak and vain
(Their Right Divine—a half-demented brain !)
The princely spawn of ancient crafty knaves,
Whose thrones were built on heads of timid
slaves,—
In ev’ry palace fair, and stately town
What Things we Crown !
Q.
P. LUFTIG.
Grandfather Dan.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There was a vague sort of an illusion abroad to
the effect that Grandfather Dan had once been a
young man. I never for a moment believed any
nonsense of the kind. I had seen a picture of a
small, jaunty, handsome dandy of 25 or so, in the
strange, quaint dress of some fanciful epoch that I
knew perfectly well only existed in books—an
epoch in which impossible men dressed in trousers
extremely tight at the ankles and preposterously
slack at the hips, coats with waists and busts, and
ridiculous high ties that looked like a cure for sore
throat. These men cultivated the most shameful
side whiskers, too, wore their hair long and rolled
under, and always carried thin, tasselled canes,
and leered like satyrs. The picture represented
an indecent object of that mythical age. Grand-
father Dan? People think boys such awful
asses!
Grandfather Dan was very small, and thin, and
old, with silvery white hair, and a long-nosed,
clean-shaven face that had a comical way of
shutting up close like a concertina. He was very
bent, and walked with a stout stick, grunting
forcibly as he shuffled along. Extremely careful
of his dress was Grandfather Dan, but his eyes
were not much good now, and the frock coat he
thought so immaculate and the belltopper he
thought so glossy were both stained and frayed,
and well on in years.
In spite of his decrepitude and his shabby ap-
parel, there was a pathetic jauntiness about
Grandfather Dan ; the ghost of a leer lingered in
the tail of his right eye; he tilted his old bell-
topper slightly to the left, tied his cravat with
elaborate care, and his eyes at their darkest never
lost a certain amative expectancy. He was ever
on the qui vive for possible conquests.
The mythical Grandfather Dan, the youthful
dandy of the portrait, provided much conversa-
tion amongst the female visitors. Endless tales
attached to that picture, the point of which was
always confided in a half-wliisper, and received
with upturned eyes and eloquent, protesting
palms. The impression I gathered was of a dap-
per, handsome devil-of-a-fellow, gay of dress,
careless and happy of manner, caring not one of
Grandfather Dan’s queer old blasphemous oaths
for any man under heaven, sauntering in ridicul-
ous raiment along crowded streets or in public
parks, for ever getting into mischief, for ever
being pursued by angry men, even being shot at,
but never for one moment being of the smallest
possible use in the world.
“He was loved by dozens of women,” said
Aunt Sarah, “and he never deserved the affec-
tions of a cat.”
Sometimes Grandfather Dan came hobbling out
amongst the tattlers, his old head lolling "from
side to side like that of a toy turtle, peering right
and left, chin, nose, and eyes almost running
together in the abnormally contracted face. He
might pass half through the sitting-room before
discovering a strange petticoat, but when he did,
he plucked-up wonderfully, giving characteristic
little tugs and touches to coat and tie and hair,
and chuckling joyously.
“What, ladies? Ladies, ladies!” he cried.
“Gad, if I was only young again ! ”
Then he would dig the nearest woman wickedly
in the ribs with the point of his thumb, and fall
into quite a fit of chuckling and mumbling,
always ending in a bad cough.
Let him once get hold of a plump little hand in
that party, and it was a hard matter to induce
him to let it go again. He would cling to his
prize, caressing it and stuttering antiquated com-
pliments. Grandfather Dan, though he lived to
be 90 odd, never reached years of discretion.
Of course, the veteran’s advances were not
cheerfully received, but he could hear nothing,
and could not see much, and, clinging to the hand
he had captured, he would recite the disreputable
doings of his gay youth, regardless of frowns and
protestations, if powerful Hannah were not caller!
in to carry -him bodily, kicking, squealing, and
swearing, into his own den again.
After Grandfather Dan encountered the austere
and elderly wife of the Methodist minister alone
in the parlor, that eminently respectable lady
never called again.
“ Gad, if I was only young again ! ” chortled
grandfather, and he dug the good lady giddily
with his venal thumb, and, capturing a fat hand,
presently drifted into his raffish reminiscences, the
pious creature backing away the while, and regard-
ing him with the startled expression of a woman
who finds herself in the power of a maniac. She
broke and fled at the second chapter, and the
reputation of the family suffered much as a result
of her wide and frequent recitation of her ex-
periences.
Grandfather Dan was rarely given a chance of
going far with the chronicles of his early amours,
but on one ocasion he spent half-an-hour affection-
ately reciting his confidences to a seamstress’s
dummy that had been borrowed to further some
dressmaking operations, and when he discovered
his error he made frantic but feeble efforts to
throw the thing through the window, breaking
much property in the attempt, and squealing the
while like an excited mandrill.
On one occasion he was discovered, a tiny little
handful of a man, sitting alongside the new
washerwoman, a female of enormous proportions.
The washerwoman sat with her hands placidly
folded before her, and Grandfather Dan was
making his arm go as far as possible around her
great bulk.
“Mrs. Henty!” cried Aunty in shocked dis-
may.
“ Sure, ma’am,” said Mrs. Henty composedly,
“ it do seem to be such a comfort to the poor body,
I haven’t the heart to shift him.”
Hannah shifted him
But Grandfather Dan took to growing old very
quickly—he dwindled every week—he was shrink-
ing visibly. His legs went first; they would
buckle under him when he attempted to take his
usual walk to the street corner and back, and one
of Carton’s big boys would pick him up, and carry
him home bodily. Those were terrible experi-
ences for Grandfather Dan. He was so helpless
in the strong arms, so scared, and so fierce. His
mouth formed a black oval, his eyes stared wildly,
he kicked and clawed like a man falling into an
abyss, piping all the time parrot-like cries of fear,
and swearing at random, and, for long after, he
would sit sobbing and shaking in his chair, grunt-
ing and swearing strange obsolete oaths, or mut-
tering for hours, “ Oh, my ! oh, my ! Damn my
eyes ! Oh, my ! ”
"At length, it became necessary to provide him
with artificial means of locomotion, and Grand-
father Dan was enthroned in a small three-
wheeled affair, propelled by handles that worked
backwards and forwards. Grandfather’s arms
were still strong, but, after he had wheeled him-
self into many difficulties, a nurse-girl was hired to
take care of him, and wheel him about. There was
little left of him to wheel, and the girl was a
strong, deep-chested, strapping damsel of 17, who
handled him like a babe.
Kitty had been in charge a couple of days before
Grandfather Dan awoke to the fact that his cus-
todian was of the delectable sex. Kitty was
leaning over to buckle a strap when the old man
made the discovery.
“ By gad ! a girl,” he piped, “and a real pretty
one, too ! He, he—a damned pretty one 1”
He had Kitty’s hand, and he kissed it, and
a queer, ghost-like resemblance to the jaunty,
leering portrait came back to him.
“ How are you, ray dear ? ” he mumbled. “ Gad,
if I was only young again ! Gad, if I only was !
He, he, he ! ” He chuckled and coughed, and
Kitty moved away, with all the scorn virtue has
for a dotard.
Grandfather followed her up, working the
handles of his wheeled-chair.
“Come here, my dear,” he crooned. “Devil
burn me, but you’re a plum ! Come here, my
little love.”
Kitty had suddenly conceived a dread of the
shrunken man; she moved more quickly, and
Grandfather Dan, still a deuce of a fellow, followed
her up. Kitty ran ; arid, working his handles as
hard as he could, Grandfather wheeled after her,
sprinting like a hero.
Kitty’s screams brought members of the family
to the balcony. Down the central path, hot on
Kitty’s heels, raced Grandfather. Round the big
circular flower bed, possessed with a terror as of
something inhuman, Kitty fled, and nervously
jerking at the levers with all the power left in
him—his toothless mouth working, his eyes bright
with the excitement of the chase—the villain still
pursued her.
Grandfather Dan luffed badly at the far turning
and crashed into a large, glass-covered bed, and
disappeared among the ruins. The old man was
carried in, bleeding from many scratches, and put
to bed, and there he lay for weeks, counting
his life away. Of late he had counted everything
he did, counted his footsteps, the number of times
he stirred his tea, the buttoning of his clothes, the
winding of his clock, the lacing of his boots, and
now he spent his time counting up to ten. We
could hear him at it in the night, piping drearily,
“One, two, three, four, five, six, seven, eight,
nine, ten —one, two, three ”
On a pleasant afternoon, when the sun was let
into his room, Grandfather Dan discovered the
nurse who had been attending on him. He was
staring brightly, amatively into her face. “Eh,
gad,” he crooned, “a petticoat ! Lord, if I was
only young again ! ” .
He attempted to dig the nurse in the ribs in
his familiar, rakish way, but the effort exhausted
him and he fell back, a little soft white man,
among the soft white sheets, and died; and
on the waxen, dead face, in the coffin, next day, I
found the jaunty smile of the ribald young man of
the portrait.
EDWARD DYSON.
The “ Simplex ” time-table, in use at
Spencer-street (Melb.) railway station is a great
improvement on the usual mystery of a thing that
is plastered in great blanket sheets all over the
premises. The “ Simplex ”is an upright, com-
pact, gaudy-looking machine with a glass face.
It has its innards chockful of time-tables, each
with a big black number on it. If there are, say,
50 railways in a State, the time-tables are num-
bered from 1 to 50 ; then there is an alphabetical
index of all the stations in the State, worn on
Simplex’s stomach, and numbered to correspond
with the time-tables. So, if you want to know
when the train starts for Happy Land or Mud
Hole, you just note its number on the index, say,
37 ; then give the handle a turn, and churn up
the intestines of “ Simplex ” till a big black 37
looks out of its face at you—and there is your
time-table. As well as being a boon to travellers,
it has become a great favorite with the small
boys for miles round. They all want to know
what time the train starts for somewhere, several
times a day, and poor “Simplex” is getting no
rest.
